"Easy come, easy go","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is easily gained is also easily lost. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 19th-century proverb about the nature of wealth or luck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I won $50 and spent it all an hour later. Easy come, easy go."
"Eat humble pie","<b>Meaning:</b> To admit you were wrong and apologize. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Umble pie' was made from the offal (organs) of a deer and served to the lower classes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After bragging about his win, he had to eat humble pie when the results were corrected."
"Eleventh hour","<b>Meaning:</b> The latest possible moment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; the last hour of a 12-hour workday. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The two countries reached a peace agreement at the eleventh hour."
"Every cloud has a silver lining","<b>Meaning:</b> There is something good in every bad situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a John Milton poem; even a dark cloud has a bright edge where the sun hits it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I lost my job, but it gave me time to start my own business; every cloud has a silver lining."
"Face the music","<b>Meaning:</b> To accept the unpleasant consequences of your actions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the military practice of drumming a soldier out of the regiment. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After breaking the window, I had to go home and face the music."
"Fair-weather friend","<b>Meaning:</b> A friend who is only there when things are going well. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A nautical metaphor; someone who sails with you only when the weather is good. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He disappeared as soon as I lost my money; he was just a fair-weather friend."
"Fall on one's sword","<b>Meaning:</b> To take the blame for something or resign to protect others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Roman generals would literally kill themselves with their swords to avoid the shame of defeat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The manager decided to fall on his sword and take responsibility for the failed project."
"Feather in your cap","<b>Meaning:</b> An achievement you can be proud of. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the custom of warriors adding a feather to their headgear for every enemy they defeated. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Getting your article published in a national magazine is a real feather in your cap."
"Feel the pinch","<b>Meaning:</b> To experience hardship because of a lack of money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the 'tightness' of a small budget. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> With the new taxes, many families are starting to feel the pinch."
"Fine-tooth comb","<b>Meaning:</b> To examine something in great detail. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using a comb with teeth very close together to find small things like lice. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The lawyers went through the contract with a fine-tooth comb."
"Fish out of water","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is in an unfamiliar and uncomfortable situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A fish cannot survive or behave normally on land. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> At the fancy gala, I felt like a fish out of water."
"Fly on the wall","<b>Meaning:</b> An unnoticed observer of a private conversation or event. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests being as small and insignificant as a fly so no one notices you are there. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'd love to be a fly on the wall when they discuss the new promotions."
"Follow suit","<b>Meaning:</b> To do the same thing as someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From card games, where you must play a card of the same suit as the first player. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When the first company lowered its prices, all the others followed suit."
"From the horse's mouth","<b>Meaning:</b> Directly from the original or most reliable source. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a horse’s teeth can tell you its true age, regardless of what the seller says. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know the company is closing; I heard it straight from the horse's mouth."
"Get off on the wrong foot","<b>Meaning:</b> To start a relationship or a task badly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the ancient superstition that starting to walk with the left foot brought bad luck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I think we got off on the wrong foot; let's start our meeting again."
"Get your ducks in a row","<b>Meaning:</b> To get well-organized and prepared. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from bowling; the pins used to be called 'ducks' and had to be reset in a row. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to get our ducks in a row before we pitch to the investors."
"Gift of the gab","<b>Meaning:</b> The ability to speak eloquently and persuasively. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Gab' is an old word for chatter; possibly linked to the Blarney Stone in Ireland. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He has the gift of the gab; he could sell a heater to someone in the desert."
"Give someone the slip","<b>Meaning:</b> To escape from someone who is following you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 16th century; 'slip' means to move quietly or smoothly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The thief managed to give the police the slip in the crowded market."
"Go the whole hog","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something completely and thoroughly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from an old word for a coin (a hog), meaning to spend the whole thing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you're going to renovate the kitchen, you might as well go the whole hog and do the bathroom too."
"Green-eyed monster","<b>Meaning:</b> Jealousy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Coined by Shakespeare in Othello; green was associated with illness and bile. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He saw the green-eyed monster when his friend got the promotion he wanted."
"Hard nut to crack","<b>Meaning:</b> A problem that is difficult to solve or a person who is difficult to understand. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal comparison to a nut with a very tough shell. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This physics equation is a real hard nut to crack."
"Harp on","<b>Meaning:</b> To talk about the same subject repeatedly and annoyingly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From playing a harp; repeatedly plucking the same string. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know I made a mistake, but you don't have to keep harping on it."
"Have a card up your sleeve","<b>Meaning:</b> To have a secret plan or advantage. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Cheating at cards by hiding one in a sleeve. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't worry about the competition; I have a card up my sleeve."
"Have the last laugh","<b>Meaning:</b> To succeed in the end after people previously doubted you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A proverb dating back to the 1600s. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone mocked his invention, but he had the last laugh when it became a global success."
"In a nutshell","<b>Meaning:</b> Summarized in a very few words. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the idea of writing a story so small it could fit inside a nutshell. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> In a nutshell, we need more money and more time."
"Keep your chin up","<b>Meaning:</b> To stay positive in a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for pride and hope; looking down is a sign of sadness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you're disappointed, but keep your chin up; things will get better."
"Know the ropes","<b>Meaning:</b> To be experienced and know how a system works. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sailing; sailors had to know which ropes controlled which sails. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Ask the supervisor; she’s been here 20 years and knows the ropes."
"Level playing field","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where everyone has the same opportunity to succeed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In sports, if a field is slanted, one team has an unfair advantage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Tax cuts for small businesses would create a more level playing field."
"Lion's share","<b>Meaning:</b> The biggest or best part of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables where a lion claimed all the meat for himself. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The eldest son received the lion's share of the inheritance."
"Make no bones about it","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very direct and honest about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'finding bones in soup'; if there are no bones, you can eat it without hesitation or difficulty. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He made no bones about the fact that he hated the new boss."
"Front","Back"
"Hard feelings","<b>Meaning:</b> Feelings of anger or resentment after a dispute. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to emotions that have 'hardened' into a grudge rather than being forgiven. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I hope there are no hard feelings after our argument yesterday."
"Have a bone to pick","<b>Meaning:</b> To have a complaint to discuss with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 16th century; refers to a dog chewing on a bone for a long time, just as a person might 'chew' on a grievance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a bone to pick with you regarding the mess you left in the kitchen."
"Have a field day","<b>Meaning:</b> To have a lot of fun or success doing something you enjoy, often at someone else's expense. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally a military term for a day of maneuvers or exercise in the field. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The press had a field day when the celebrity was caught in a scandal."
"Have a heart of gold","<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely kind and generous. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing a person's character to gold, the most precious and 'pure' metal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She spends all her weekends volunteering; she truly has a heart of gold."
"Have your cake and eat it too","<b>Meaning:</b> To have two desirable things that are usually impossible to have at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal observation; once you eat the cake, you no longer possess it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He wants a high-paying job but doesn't want to work any overtime; he wants to have his cake and eat it too."
"Head in the clouds","<b>Meaning:</b> Being unrealistic or not paying attention to what is happening around you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone whose mind is far above the 'ground' of reality. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s always got his head in the clouds, dreaming of becoming a rock star."
"Head over heels","<b>Meaning:</b> To be completely in love with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally 'heels over head' (like a somersault); it was later inverted to 'head over heels.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Within a week of meeting her, he was head over heels in love."
"Heard it through the grapevine","<b>Meaning:</b> To learn news or rumors through informal conversation rather than official sources. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the tangled appearance of telegraph wires in the 1800s, which resembled grape vines. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I heard it through the grapevine that the manager is quitting."
"High and dry","<b>Meaning:</b> Left in a difficult situation without help. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A nautical term for a ship that is stranded on the shore after the tide goes out. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When the partner left the business, he was left high and dry."
"Hit the books","<b>Meaning:</b> To study hard. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for physically starting to work with textbooks. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a final exam tomorrow, so I really need to hit the books tonight."
"Hit the ceiling","<b>Meaning:</b> To become very angry. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests that a person's temper is so explosive they physically 'rise' to the top of the room. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My father hit the ceiling when he saw the dent in his car."
"Hit the road","<b>Meaning:</b> To begin a journey or leave a place. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor for tires or feet 'hitting' the pavement of a road. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s getting late; it's time for us to hit the road."
"Hold your horses","<b>Meaning:</b> To wait or slow down; be patient. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the days of horse-drawn carriages; literally pulling the reins to stop the horses. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Hold your horses! We aren't ready to leave yet."
"Ignorance is bliss","<b>Meaning:</b> It is better not to know about something if it would make you unhappy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 1742 poem by Thomas Gray. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't know the house was haunted when I bought it, and honestly, ignorance is bliss."
"In a jam","<b>Meaning:</b> To be in a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to being physically stuck or 'jammed' so that movement is impossible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m in a bit of a jam because my car won't start and I have a meeting."
"In a pickle","<b>Meaning:</b> To be in a troublesome or difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from 'pickled' vegetables being trapped in a jar of vinegar. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We’re in a real pickle now that we've lost the map."
"In the bag","<b>Meaning:</b> Certain to be won or achieved. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 19th-century hunting, where the day's catch was placed in a bag. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> With our star player back, the championship is in the bag."
"In the dark","<b>Meaning:</b> Unaware of something; not having information. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor where 'light' equals knowledge and 'darkness' equals ignorance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They kept me in the dark about the layoffs until the very last day."
"In the limelight","<b>Meaning:</b> At the center of public attention. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Limelight was a type of stage lighting used in theaters before electricity. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Ever since his book became a bestseller, he’s been in the limelight."
"In the red","<b>Meaning:</b> Spending more money than you are earning; in debt. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Accountants traditionally used red ink to record losses or debts. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company has been in the red for three consecutive quarters."
"It’s not rocket science","<b>Meaning:</b> Something is not difficult to understand. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Compares the task at hand to the extreme complexity of aerospace engineering. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Just follow the instructions; it’s not rocket science."
"Jump the gun","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something too soon, before the right time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From track races where a runner starts before the starting pistol is fired. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We jumped the gun by announcing the product before it was finished."
"Keep an eye on","<b>Meaning:</b> To watch or monitor something carefully. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for focused observation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Can you keep an eye on the soup while I’m out of the room?"
"Keep someone at arm’s length","<b>Meaning:</b> To avoid becoming too friendly or close with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Maintaining a physical distance to prevent intimacy or conflict. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I try to keep my coworkers at arm’s length to avoid office drama."
"Keep your fingers crossed","<b>Meaning:</b> To hope for good luck. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An ancient superstition that crossing fingers would trap good spirits or ward off bad luck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m keeping my fingers crossed that I get the job."
"Kick the bucket","<b>Meaning:</b> A euphemism for dying. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Disputed, but possibly from a person standing on a bucket to hang themselves and سپس kicking it away. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He lived a long life before he finally kicked the bucket at age 98."
"Kill two birds with one stone","<b>Meaning:</b> To achieve two things with a single action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from ancient hunting using a sling. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ll drop you at the mall on my way to work; that way I kill two birds with one stone."
"Knock on wood","<b>Meaning:</b> Tapping on something made of wood to avoid bad luck. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ancient beliefs that spirits lived in trees and tapping them would protect you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I haven't been sick all year, knock on wood."
"Know the ropes","<b>Meaning:</b> To understand how a particular job or task is done. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sailing; a new sailor had to learn which ropes controlled which sails. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Ask Sarah for help; she’s been here for years and knows the ropes."
"Last but not least","<b>Meaning:</b> The last person or thing mentioned is just as important as the others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> First appeared in literature in the 1500s. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> And last but not least, I’d like to thank my parents."
"Lend a hand","<b>Meaning:</b> To help someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for offering one's manual labor. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Could you lend a hand with these heavy boxes?"
"Let bygones be bygones","<b>Meaning:</b> To forget past disagreements and move on. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Bygone' means something that has gone by or passed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They decided to let bygones be bygones and start their friendship over."
"Let the cat out of the bag","<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old markets where a cat was substituted for a pig; opening the bag revealed the fraud. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It was supposed to be a surprise, but someone let the cat out of the bag."
"Like a fish out of water","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling uncomfortable or awkward in a new environment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A fish cannot survive or function properly outside of water. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As a city girl in the countryside, I felt like a fish out of water."
"Long in the tooth","<b>Meaning:</b> Being old. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> As horses age, their gums recede, making their teeth appear longer. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That car is getting a bit long in the tooth; it might be time for a new one."
"Look before you leap","<b>Meaning:</b> Think about the possible consequences before you do something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables; a warning not to act impulsively. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You should check the contract thoroughly; look before you leap."
"Lose your touch","<b>Meaning:</b> To lose an ability or talent you once had. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for no longer having the 'feel' or sensitivity for a skill. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He used to be a great cook, but he’s losing his touch."
"Make a mountain out of a molehill","<b>Meaning:</b> To exaggerate a small problem and make it seem like a major one. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Compares a tiny mound of dirt (molehill) to a massive mountain. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It was just a small mistake; don't make a mountain out of a molehill."
"Make ends meet","<b>Meaning:</b> To earn enough money to pay for your basic expenses. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 17th century; possibly referring to the ends of a year's income and expenses meeting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> With the rent increase, it’s hard to make ends meet."
"Method to my madness","<b>Meaning:</b> Having a specific plan or reason for doing something that seems crazy or disorganized. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Shakespeare's Hamlet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My filing system looks messy, but there is a method to my madness."
"Miss the boat","<b>Meaning:</b> To miss an opportunity because you were too slow to act. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Arriving at the dock after the ship has already sailed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I missed the boat on that stock investment; now the price is too high."
"Money doesn't grow on trees","<b>Meaning:</b> Money is limited and must be worked for; it is not easily acquired. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal observation that money (unlike fruit) is not a natural resource you can just pick. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Turn off the lights; money doesn't grow on trees."
"Neck and neck","<b>Meaning:</b> Very close in a race or competition. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; when two horses are running so close that their necks are level. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The two candidates are neck and neck in the polls."
"Never bite the hand that feeds you","<b>Meaning:</b> Do not hurt or be ungrateful to someone who helps or supports you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An observation of dog behavior; a loyal dog would never bite its owner. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Even if you disagree with your boss, don't bite the hand that feeds you."
"No-brainer","<b>Meaning:</b> A decision that is so obvious or easy it requires no thought. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A modern term implying the task doesn't require the use of a brain. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Choosing the job with better pay and shorter hours was a no-brainer."
"Not my cup of tea","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that you do not like or are not interested in. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the British culture of tea drinking; if a tea isn't to your taste, you don't drink it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know opera is popular, but it’s just not my cup of tea."
"Nutty as a fruitcake","<b>Meaning:</b> Very crazy or eccentric. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fruitcakes are literally full of nuts; 'nutty' is slang for crazy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That old man’s stories are wild; he’s nutty as a fruitcake."
"Off the top of my head","<b>Meaning:</b> Providing information from memory without checking or thinking deeply. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for information that is sitting right at the 'top' of the mind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't know the exact number off the top of my head, but it’s around fifty."
"On cloud nine","<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely happy and excited. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From early cloud classifications where 'Cloud 9' was the highest, loftiest type. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She’s been on cloud nine since she got engaged."
"On the fence","<b>Meaning:</b> Undecided about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sitting on a fence between two properties, not choosing to stand in either yard. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m still on the fence about whether to go to the party or stay home."
"On thin ice","<b>Meaning:</b> In a risky or dangerous situation where a mistake could lead to disaster. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor for walking on ice that might break at any moment. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After being late for the third time, he is on thin ice with the manager."
"Once in a blue moon","<b>Meaning:</b> Very rarely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A blue moon is the second full moon in a month, happening only about every 2.7 years. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I only see my distant relatives once in a blue moon."
"Out of the blue","<b>Meaning:</b> Suddenly and unexpectedly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Short for 'a bolt from the blue,' referring to lightning hitting from a clear sky. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She called me out of the blue to offer me a job."
"Over the top","<b>Meaning:</b> Excessive or exaggerated. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In WWI, it meant soldiers climbing out of their trenches to attack; now it means going beyond limits. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The decorations for the party were a bit over the top."
"Painting the town red","<b>Meaning:</b> Going out and celebrating wildly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from an 1837 incident where a group of men literally painted buildings red during a night of rowdy drinking. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s her 21st birthday, so we’re going to paint the town red."
"Pass the buck","<b>Meaning:</b> To shift responsibility or blame to someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From poker; a knife with a buckhorn handle (the 'buck') was passed to the person whose turn it was to deal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't try to pass the buck; it was your job to lock the door."
"Piece of cake","<b>Meaning:</b> Something very easy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From easy competitions where cakes were given as prizes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The math test was a piece of cake."
"Play it by ear","<b>Meaning:</b> To decide how to deal with a situation as it develops rather than having a plan. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From musicians playing music by memory/sound rather than reading sheet music. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We don't have a hotel booked yet; we'll just play it by ear when we arrive."
"Pull someone’s leg","<b>Meaning:</b> To tease or joke with someone by telling them something untrue. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Historically meant to trip someone; now refers to 'tripping' them with a joke. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't really see a UFO; I was just pulling your leg."
"Put all your eggs in one basket","<b>Meaning:</b> To risk everything on a single venture. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal warning; if the basket falls, all eggs break. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't invest all your money in one stock; don't put all your eggs in one basket."
"Put your foot in your mouth","<b>Meaning:</b> To say something embarrassing, tactless, or hurtful without meaning to. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for a person 'tripping' over their own words. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I really put my foot in my mouth when I asked her how her husband was, forgetting they got divorced."
"Raining cats and dogs","<b>Meaning:</b> Raining very heavily. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain, but possibly from street drainage where animals were washed away during storms. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We can't go to the park; it's raining cats and dogs."
"Read between the lines","<b>Meaning:</b> To understand the hidden meaning in what someone says or writes. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From military cryptography where hidden messages were written in invisible ink between visible lines. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He said he was 'fine,' but if you read between the lines, you can tell he's upset."
"Right as rain","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling completely well or healthy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Right' once meant 'straight' or 'fitting'; rain is seen as a natural, correct occurrence. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was sick yesterday, but I'm as right as rain today."
"Rule of thumb","<b>Meaning:</b> A general principle or guide based on experience. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using the thumb as a rough unit of measurement. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As a rule of thumb, always arrive 10 minutes early."
"Run out of steam","<b>Meaning:</b> To lose energy or interest in something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From steam engines that stop moving when the steam pressure drops. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The project started well, but we ran out of steam by the end of the month."
"Saved by the bell","<b>Meaning:</b> Rescued from a difficult situation at the last possible moment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; a fighter who is about to lose is saved if the round-ending bell rings. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't know the answer to the teacher's question, but then the lunch bell rang; I was saved by the bell."
"See eye to eye","<b>Meaning:</b> To agree with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Isaiah 52:8). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My boss and I don't always see eye to eye on every issue."
"Sell like hotcakes","<b>Meaning:</b> To be bought quickly and in large numbers. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Hotcakes' (pancakes) are best when fresh and hot, so they sell very fast at fairs or markets. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new video game is selling like hotcakes."
"Set the record straight","<b>Meaning:</b> To correct a mistake or provide the true facts about a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the idea of correcting a formal written record. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I want to set the record straight: I did not quit; I was laid off."
"Shot in the dark","<b>Meaning:</b> A random guess or attempt without much hope of success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor for firing a weapon when you cannot see the target. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't know the answer, so my guess was just a shot in the dark."
"Skeleton in the closet","<b>Meaning:</b> An embarrassing or scandalous secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 19th century; refers to hiding something dead (scandalous) out of sight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He seems perfect, but everyone has a skeleton in the closet."
"Skin of your teeth","<b>Meaning:</b> To barely manage to do something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Book of Job in the Bible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I passed the class by the skin of my teeth."
"Sleep on it","<b>Meaning:</b> To wait until the next day to make a decision. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The idea that a night's rest will provide clarity or a fresh perspective. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m not sure if I should buy the car; let me sleep on it."
"Smell a rat","<b>Meaning:</b> To suspect that something is wrong or that someone is being dishonest. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A dog or cat smelling a rat even if they can't see it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When he asked for my bank details, I began to smell a rat."
"Speak of the devil","<b>Meaning:</b> When the person you were just talking about appears. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the old saying 'Speak of the devil and he shall appear.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Speak of the devil! We were just discussing your promotion."
"Spill the beans","<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Greek voting where beans were used; spilling them revealed the secret vote. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't spill the beans about the surprise party."
"Take it with a grain of salt","<b>Meaning:</b> To not believe something completely or take it too seriously. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ancient Rome; the idea that food is easier to swallow with a little salt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He exaggerates a lot, so take his stories with a grain of salt."
"The ball is in your court","<b>Meaning:</b> It is your turn to take action or make a decision. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From tennis. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ve made my offer; now the ball is in your court."
"The best of both worlds","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where you can enjoy the advantages of two different things at once. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A modern phrase describing a perfect balance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Living in the suburbs gives me the best of both worlds: a quiet home and a short commute to the city."
"The elephant in the room","<b>Meaning:</b> An obvious problem that everyone is ignoring. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Based on the fact that an elephant is too big to be missed, so ignoring it is intentional. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His drinking problem was the elephant in the room at Thanksgiving dinner."
"The whole nine yards","<b>Meaning:</b> Everything; the entire amount. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Disputed; possibly from the length of machine gun belts or fabric for a kilt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He did the laundry, cleaned the kitchen, and mopped—the whole nine yards."
"Through thick and thin","<b>Meaning:</b> In both good and bad times. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old English 'through thicket and thin wood.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My best friend has been with me through thick and thin."
"Throw in the towel","<b>Meaning:</b> To give up. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; a coach throws a towel into the ring to stop a fight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After trying for hours to fix the computer, I finally threw in the towel."
"Tie the knot","<b>Meaning:</b> To get married. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ancient marriage ceremonies where the couple's hands were literally tied together. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They are planning to tie the knot in June."
"Time flies","<b>Meaning:</b> Time passes very quickly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Latin 'tempus fugit.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't believe your son is already ten; time flies!"
"To cost an arm and a leg","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very expensive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from the price of portraits in the 18th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This new laptop cost me an arm and a leg."
"To cut corners","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something poorly to save time or money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From driving; cutting across a corner rather than following the road. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They cut corners on the construction, and now the roof leaks."
"To feel under the weather","<b>Meaning:</b> To feel sick. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sailors would go below deck (under the weather) when they felt seasick. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I think I’ll stay home today; I’m feeling a bit under the weather."
"To hit the nail on the head","<b>Meaning:</b> To describe exactly what is causing a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From carpentry; hitting a nail perfectly on its head. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You hit the nail on the head when you said the problem is a lack of communication."
"To kill time","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something to keep busy while waiting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'destroying' the minutes so they pass faster. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I played games on my phone to kill time at the airport."
"To let the cat out of the bag","<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal a secret by accident. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 18th-century street markets. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I accidentally let the cat out of the bag about the surprise."
"To make a long story short","<b>Meaning:</b> To get to the point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal summary. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Long story short, we got lost and missed the party."
"To steal someone's thunder","<b>Meaning:</b> To take credit for someone else's work or take attention away from them. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an 18th-century playwright whose thunder-making machine was copied by another theater. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She stole my thunder by announcing her pregnancy at my wedding."
"Under the sun","<b>Meaning:</b> Everything on Earth. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Ecclesiastes). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We talked about everything under the sun."
"Up in the air","<b>Meaning:</b> Uncertain; not yet decided. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Like an object thrown into the air that hasn't landed yet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our plans for the summer are still up in the air."
"Walking on air","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling extremely happy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for feeling so light you could float. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s been walking on air since he got the promotion."
"Water under the bridge","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that happened in the past and is no longer a problem. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Water that has already flowed away cannot be retrieved. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We used to fight, but that's all water under the bridge now."
"Wear your heart on your sleeve","<b>Meaning:</b> To show your emotions openly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From medieval jousting where knights wore a lady's favor on their sleeve. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You always know how he feels; he wears his heart on his sleeve."
"Weather the storm","<b>Meaning:</b> To survive a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sailing; a ship surviving a literal storm. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company is struggling, but we will weather the storm."
"Wild goose chase","<b>Meaning:</b> A hopeless or senseless search for something unattainable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Shakespeare; originally referred to a type of horse race. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Looking for my lost keys in the dark was a wild goose chase."
"Front","Back"
"A penny saved is a penny earned","<b>Meaning:</b> It is as useful to save money that you already have as it is to earn more. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Often attributed to Benjamin Franklin in Poor Richard's Almanack. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I decided to cook at home instead of eating out; a penny saved is a penny earned."
"A picture paints a thousand words","<b>Meaning:</b> A complex idea can be conveyed with just a single still image. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> First appeared in the early 20th century in newspaper advertisements. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't need to explain the mess; I just showed him the photo, and a picture paints a thousand words."
"A sight for sore eyes","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone or something that you are very pleased to see. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests that the person’s eyes were 'sore' from looking for the object/person and are now healed by seeing it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After three days in the desert, the small oasis was a sight for sore eyes."
"A toss-up","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where two outcomes are equally likely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the practice of tossing a coin to decide a result. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Both teams are playing so well that the final result is a toss-up."
"Against the clock","<b>Meaning:</b> Doing something as fast as possible to finish before a deadline. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from racing or athletics where the clock is the opponent. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We are working against the clock to get the stage ready for the concert."
"Against the grain","<b>Meaning:</b> Doing something that is contrary to one's natural tendencies or social norms. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From woodworking; it is harder to cut wood across the natural fiber (the grain) than with it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It goes against the grain for me to lie to my parents."
"All in a day's work","<b>Meaning:</b> Part of what is expected and normal in one's job. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests that even difficult or unusual tasks are just standard routine. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Helping people escape the fire is all in a day's work for a firefighter."
"All in the same boat","<b>Meaning:</b> To be in the same unpleasant situation as other people. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; if a boat sinks, everyone on board faces the same fate regardless of rank. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We are all in the same boat during this power outage, so let's help each other."
"An axe to grind","<b>Meaning:</b> Having a private reason for being involved in something or a grievance to settle. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized by Benjamin Franklin; refers to a man who needed his axe sharpened and tricked a boy into doing it for him. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She isn't criticizing the plan for the team's sake; she has an axe to grind with the manager."
"As a rule of thumb","<b>Meaning:</b> A broadly accurate guide or principle based on experience rather than theory. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Historically, people used the width of their thumb as a rough measurement for length. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As a rule of thumb, you should save 20% of your income."
"At the eleventh hour","<b>Meaning:</b> At the latest possible moment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Parable of the Workers in the Vineyard), where the last group was hired at the 11th hour of a 12-hour workday. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The deal was signed at the eleventh hour, just before the deadline."
"At the end of your tether","<b>Meaning:</b> Having no patience or energy left to deal with a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to an animal tied to a rope (tether) that has reached its physical limit of movement. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Between the crying baby and the broken sink, I’m at the end of my tether."
"Back to the salt mines","<b>Meaning:</b> Returning to work, usually with a sense of reluctance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Historically, being sent to work in salt mines was a punishment for prisoners. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My vacation is over; tomorrow it's back to the salt mines."
"Ballpark figure","<b>Meaning:</b> A rough numerical estimate. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From baseball; if a ball is within the ballpark, it’s close enough to the action. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't need the exact cost yet, just give me a ballpark figure."
"Barking up the wrong tree","<b>Meaning:</b> Following a wrong lead or blaming the wrong person. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hunting dogs barking at a tree after the raccoon has already jumped to another. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you think I took your lunch, you're barking up the wrong tree."
"Beating a dead horse","<b>Meaning:</b> Wasting time on something that has already been settled or is beyond change. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Attempting to make a horse move when it is no longer alive is useless. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We already decided to cancel the project; talking about it now is just beating a dead horse."
"Beggars can't be choosers","<b>Meaning:</b> People who depend on the generosity of others should not complain about what they receive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 16th-century proverb; those asking for help cannot dictate the terms of the help. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The hotel room was tiny, but it was the only one available and we needed sleep; beggars can't be choosers."
"Behind the eight ball","<b>Meaning:</b> In a difficult or disadvantageous position. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the game of pool; if the cue ball is behind the black 8-ball, the shot is nearly impossible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After starting the project late, we are really behind the eight ball."
"Below the belt","<b>Meaning:</b> A remark or action that is unfair or cruel. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; hitting an opponent below the waistline is illegal and unsportsmanlike. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Bringing up his past failures during the argument was a bit below the belt."
"Bend over backwards","<b>Meaning:</b> To try extremely hard to please or help someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A gymnastics metaphor for extreme physical effort and flexibility. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I bent over backwards to make sure the guest was comfortable."
"Beside oneself","<b>Meaning:</b> Overcome with a very strong emotion, like grief or anger. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the ancient idea that a person's soul could literally leave their body during extreme emotion. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was beside herself with joy when she heard the news."
"Best thing since sliced bread","<b>Meaning:</b> A person or thing that is thought to be excellent or innovative. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sliced bread was first sold in 1928 and was considered a major convenience. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This new smartphone app is the best thing since sliced bread."
"Bite your tongue","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop yourself from saying something that might be offensive or hurtful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for holding back speech. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I wanted to tell him he was wrong, but I had to bite my tongue."
"Blind leading the blind","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where people who know nothing are being led by someone who also knows nothing. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Matthew 15:14). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Two freshmen trying to find the library is like the blind leading the blind."
"Blow off steam","<b>Meaning:</b> To release strong emotions or energy by doing something active. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From steam engines, which have safety valves to release excess pressure so the boiler doesn't explode. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I went for a run after the meeting to blow off some steam."
"Born with a silver spoon in one's mouth","<b>Meaning:</b> Born into a wealthy and privileged family. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Historically, godparents would give silver spoons as gifts, but only the wealthy could afford them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He doesn't understand the cost of living because he was born with a silver spoon in his mouth."
"Break the bank","<b>Meaning:</b> To cost more money than one can afford. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; when a player wins more money than the casino (the bank) has on the table. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We can go out to dinner, as long as it doesn't break the bank."
"Bring home the bacon","<b>Meaning:</b> To earn the money that a family needs to live. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from 12th-century England, where a man could win a side of bacon if he hadn't argued with his wife for a year. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Now that I have a full-time job, I can finally bring home the bacon."
"Bull in a china shop","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is very clumsy or careless in a delicate situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of a large, powerful animal destroying fragile dishes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Be careful what you say to her; you're like a bull in a china shop when it comes to feelings."
"Burn the candle at both ends","<b>Meaning:</b> To work too hard without getting enough rest. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A candle lit at both ends burns out twice as fast and wastes wax. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Between her job and night school, she's been burning the candle at both ends."
"Bury the hatchet","<b>Meaning:</b> To end a quarrel or conflict and become friendly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a Native American custom of literally burying weapons to symbolize peace. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After years of not speaking, the two brothers finally decided to bury the hatchet."
"By the book","<b>Meaning:</b> Following all the rules or official procedures exactly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to a literal book of rules or laws. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The auditor did everything by the book to avoid any legal issues."
"Call it a day","<b>Meaning:</b> To decide to stop working on something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally 'call it a half-day' in the 1800s, meaning to leave work early. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We’ve been at this for ten hours; let's call it a day."
"Can't make an omelet without breaking eggs","<b>Meaning:</b> It is impossible to achieve something important without causing some problems or sacrifices. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Attributed to several 18th-century French figures. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The construction is noisy, but you can't make an omelet without breaking eggs."
"Carry the torch","<b>Meaning:</b> To continue a tradition or to continue loving someone who doesn't love you back. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the ancient Greek tradition of passing a torch in a relay race. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s still carrying the torch for his high school sweetheart."
"Cash in your chips","<b>Meaning:</b> To sell something for profit, or (euphemistically) to die. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; exchanging betting chips for actual money at the end of a game. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He decided to cash in his chips and sell his shares while the market was high."
"Cast pearls before swine","<b>Meaning:</b> Offering something valuable to someone who does not appreciate its worth. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Matthew 7:6). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Explaining classical music to him is like casting pearls before swine."
"Change of heart","<b>Meaning:</b> A change in one's opinion or feelings about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Heart is used as the metaphorical seat of emotions. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was going to sell the car, but I had a change of heart."
"Checkered past","<b>Meaning:</b> A background filled with both successes and failures or scandals. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a chessboard; the alternating black and white squares represent good and bad periods. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The politician struggled to get elected because of his checkered past."
"Climb the walls","<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely frustrated, anxious, or bored. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests being so trapped by emotion that one wants to physically escape by climbing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Being stuck inside during the rainstorm made the kids start climbing the walls."
"Cold feet","<b>Meaning:</b> To become nervous and lose courage to do something planned. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from an 1800s novel, or simply the physical feeling of blood leaving the extremities due to fear. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was supposed to go skydiving, but he got cold feet at the last minute."
"Come clean","<b>Meaning:</b> To be honest and admit something you have kept secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'cleaning' one's conscience by removing the 'dirt' of a lie. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s time to come clean and tell me where you were last night."
"Cool your heels","<b>Meaning:</b> To be kept waiting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 16th century, referring to horses whose 'heels' (hooves) became hot from running and needed to cool down. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They let him cool his heels in the waiting room for an hour."
"Couch potato","<b>Meaning:</b> A lazy person who spends a lot of time sitting on a couch watching television. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Coined in the 1970s; a 'potato' is a vegetable that just sits there. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Since he lost his job, he’s become a total couch potato."
"Cry over spilled milk","<b>Meaning:</b> Worrying about a past mistake that cannot be undone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor; once milk is spilled, it cannot be gathered back into the glass. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you lost the money, but there’s no use crying over spilled milk."
"Cut someone some slack","<b>Meaning:</b> To be less strict or critical with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; giving 'slack' to a rope means it isn't tight and allows for movement. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s new at the job, so cut him some slack if he makes a mistake."
"Dead ringer","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who looks exactly like another person. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a 'ringer' was a fast horse substituted for a slow one to cheat on bets. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He is a dead ringer for his grandfather."
"Diamond in the rough","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who has great potential but lacks polish or education. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal diamond that has not yet been cut or polished to look beautiful. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new player is a diamond in the rough; he just needs better coaching."
"Dog-eat-dog","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation that is very competitive and people will do anything to succeed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 16th century, based on the idea of animals preying on their own kind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s a dog-eat-dog world in the fashion industry."
"Don't hold your breath","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't expect something to happen anytime soon. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A warning that if you held your breath until the event happened, you would suffocate. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He said he’d pay me back today, but I’m not holding my breath."
"Double-edged sword","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that has both favorable and unfavorable consequences. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A sword with two sharp edges can cut the enemy, but it can also cut the person wielding it if they aren't careful. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Fame is a double-edged sword; you get money but you lose your privacy."
"Down for the count","<b>Meaning:</b> Tired, defeated, or unconscious. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; if a fighter is knocked down, the referee counts to ten. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After the long hike, I was down for the count by 8 PM."
"Down in the dumps","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling unhappy or depressed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Dumps' comes from an old word for a melancholy state or dazed state. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s been a bit down in the dumps since his girlfriend moved away."
"Draw a blank","<b>Meaning:</b> To be unable to remember something or find an answer. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old lotteries where tickets were drawn from a box; some tickets were blank and had no prize. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I tried to remember his name, but I drew a blank."
"Drop a bombshell","<b>Meaning:</b> To announce something shocking or unexpected. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the sudden, destructive impact of a bomb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My sister dropped a bombshell when she said she was moving to Japan tomorrow."
"Dressed to the nines","<b>Meaning:</b> Dressed in a very fashionable or formal way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain, but possibly from the use of nine yards of fabric for an expensive suit. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone was dressed to the nines for the charity gala."
"Eagle eye","<b>Meaning:</b> Excellent vision or the ability to notice small details. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Eagles are famous for their incredibly sharp eyesight used for hunting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The editor has an eagle eye for spotting spelling mistakes."
"Easy as pie","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is very simple to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to how easy it is to eat a pie, rather than how easy it is to bake one. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After studying all night, the test was easy as pie."
"Eat your heart out","<b>Meaning:</b> To feel great jealousy or regret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the idea that strong emotions can literally 'consume' or 'eat' a person from the inside. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I just bought the last pair of shoes—eat your heart out!"
"Eleventh hour","<b>Meaning:</b> The very last moment before something happens. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; the 11th hour of a 12-hour day. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The strike was averted at the eleventh hour."
"Face-to-face","<b>Meaning:</b> In person; directly in front of each other. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of two people looking at each other. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to discuss this face-to-face, not over email."
"Fair and square","<b>Meaning:</b> Honestly and according to the rules. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Square' in the 16th century meant honest or equitable. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We won the game fair and square."
"Fall on deaf ears","<b>Meaning:</b> To be ignored or not noticed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for speaking to someone who refuses to listen. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My suggestions for improving the office fell on deaf ears."
"Far cry from","<b>Meaning:</b> Very different from something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the distance one would have to 'cry' (shout) to be heard; if something is a 'far cry,' it is very distant. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This cheap hotel is a far cry from the luxury resort we stayed in last year."
"Feather your own nest","<b>Meaning:</b> To make yourself richer, especially in a dishonest way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From birds using feathers to make their nests comfortable for themselves. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was accused of feathering his own nest with company funds."
"Feel like a million bucks","<b>Meaning:</b> To feel very healthy and happy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests that the person feels as valuable as a large sum of money. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After a good night's sleep, I feel like a million bucks."
"Few and far between","<b>Meaning:</b> Rare or infrequent. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 1799 poem; refers to something spaced widely apart. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Good opportunities like this are few and far between."
"Fight tooth and nail","<b>Meaning:</b> To fight very fiercely or with all one's strength. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using everything available—including biting and scratching—to win. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She fought tooth and nail to keep her business from closing."
"Fish for compliments","<b>Meaning:</b> To try to get people to say nice things about you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'casting a line' to catch a compliment. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When she says she looks 'terrible,' she's just fishing for compliments."
"Flash in the pan","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is promising at first but fails to produce lasting results. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From flintlock muskets; if the gunpowder flared in the pan but didn't fire the bullet, it was a failure. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The band's first hit was great, but they turned out to be a flash in the pan."
"Follow in someone's footsteps","<b>Meaning:</b> To do the same thing as someone else did before you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor for following a path laid out by a predecessor. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He decided to follow in his father's footsteps and become a doctor."
"Food for thought","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that warrants serious consideration or reflection. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor suggesting that the mind 'digests' information like the stomach digests food. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The documentary gave me plenty of food for thought."
"Fool's gold","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that looks valuable but is actually worthless. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to iron pyrite, a mineral that looks like gold but has no value. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That 'get rich quick' scheme turned out to be fool's gold."
"Foot in the door","<b>Meaning:</b> A small but successful start in an activity that may lead to something bigger. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From door-to-door salesmen who would literally put a foot in the door so it couldn't be closed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Taking an internship is a good way to get your foot in the door at that company."
"For good measure","<b>Meaning:</b> In addition to what has already been done or given. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From bakers or merchants giving a little extra to ensure the customer was satisfied. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He cleaned the kitchen and mopped the floor for good measure."
"Full of beans","<b>Meaning:</b> Having a lot of energy and enthusiasm. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to horses that were fed beans (high energy food) and became lively. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The kids were full of beans after the birthday party."
"Get a grip","<b>Meaning:</b> To take control of one's emotions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for physically holding onto one's self-control so it doesn't 'slip.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You need to get a grip and stop panicking."
"Get cold feet","<b>Meaning:</b> To become nervous about doing something you had planned. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the physical sensation of feet feeling cold when one is scared. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I almost quit the race because I got cold feet."
"Get someone's goat","<b>Meaning:</b> To annoy or irritate someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; goats were placed in stalls with horses to calm them down. If someone stole the goat, the horse would become upset. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It really gets my goat when people talk loudly on their phones in the library."
"Get the hang of something","<b>Meaning:</b> To learn how to do something properly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from using tools like axes or scythes, which have a specific 'hang' or balance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It took me a while, but I’m finally getting the hang of this software."
"Get the show on the road","<b>Meaning:</b> To start an activity or journey. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From traveling circuses and theater troupes moving to the next town. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We have everything packed; let's get the show on the road."
"Get your feet wet","<b>Meaning:</b> To start doing something for the first time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for stepping into water to see how cold it is before swimming. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He took a small role in the play just to get his feet wet."
"Give someone the cold shoulder","<b>Meaning:</b> To intentionally ignore or be unfriendly toward someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the custom of serving an unwanted guest a cold piece of meat instead of a warm meal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I tried to say hello, but she gave me the cold shoulder."
"Give up the ghost","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop working or to die. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; the 'ghost' refers to the soul or spirit leaving the body. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My old car finally gave up the ghost on the highway."
"Go against the grain","<b>Meaning:</b> To be contrary to what is natural or normal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Working wood in the opposite direction of its fibers. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It goes against the grain for her to be so rude."
"Go bananas","<b>Meaning:</b> To become very angry, crazy, or excited. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from monkeys becoming wild when they see bananas. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The crowd went bananas when the band finally came on stage."
"Go bust","<b>Meaning:</b> To lose all one's money and go bankrupt. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'burst,' meaning to break apart under pressure. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Many small businesses went bust during the economic crisis."
"Go cold turkey","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop a habit suddenly and completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from the cold, clammy skin and 'goosebumps' associated with drug withdrawal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He decided to quit smoking cold turkey."
"Go down in flames","<b>Meaning:</b> To fail spectacularly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an airplane being shot down and crashing while on fire. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new restaurant went down in flames after only two months."
"Go for broke","<b>Meaning:</b> To risk everything in an all-out effort. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Hawaiian gambling slang meaning to bet everything. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We decided to go for broke and invest all our savings into the startup."
"Go with the flow","<b>Meaning:</b> To accept a situation as it is and move with it rather than resisting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for moving with the current of a river. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't have a plan for the weekend; I’m just going to go with the flow."
"Grass is always greener on the other side","<b>Meaning:</b> Other people's situations always seem better than one's own. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From cattle looking at a neighboring field and thinking the grass there is better. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He quit his job for a 'better' one, but soon realized the grass isn't always greener."
"Graveyard shift","<b>Meaning:</b> A work shift that runs through the middle of the night. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain, but associated with the quiet and darkness of a graveyard at night. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My brother works the graveyard shift at the hospital."
"Grease someone's palm","<b>Meaning:</b> To bribe someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 16th century; 'greasing' makes something move more smoothly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He had to grease the guard's palm to get into the building."
"Great minds think alike","<b>Meaning:</b> Used when two people have the same thought at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 17th-century proverb suggesting intelligence leads to similar conclusions. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> 'I was just about to suggest pizza.' 'Great minds think alike!'"
"Green thumb","<b>Meaning:</b> A natural talent for growing plants. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Algae from handling pots or plants can literally turn a gardener's thumb green. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My mother has a real green thumb; her garden is beautiful."
"Grin and bear it","<b>Meaning:</b> To accept an unpleasant situation without complaining. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> To 'bear' (endure) something while maintaining a smile. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The flight was delayed for six hours, but we just had to grin and bear it."
"Hair of the dog","<b>Meaning:</b> A small amount of alcohol taken to cure a hangover. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the old belief that the best cure for a dog bite was to place a hair of the dog that bit you in the wound. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s having a beer this morning as a hair of the dog."
"Hands down","<b>Meaning:</b> Easily or without question. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; if a jockey is so far ahead they can win with their hands down (not pulling the reins). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She is hands down the best singer in the competition."
"Happy camper","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is satisfied and happy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From summer camps where happy children were 'happy campers.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Give him a book and a cup of coffee, and he’s a happy camper."
"Front","Back"
"A bird in the hand is worth two in the bush","<b>Meaning:</b> It is better to keep something you definitely have than to risk losing it by trying to get something better. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From medieval falconry where a bird captured in the hand was a sure thing compared to those still in the wild. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I accepted the job offer rather than waiting for a better one; a bird in the hand is worth two in the bush."
"A blessing in disguise","<b>Meaning:</b> A misfortune that eventually results in something good happening later on. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> First appeared in the 1700s in a hymn, referring to how God's providence often looks like trouble at first. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Getting fired was a blessing in disguise because I started my own successful business."
"A chip on your shoulder","<b>Meaning:</b> Being angry or resentful because you feel you have been treated unfairly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In the 1800s, American boys would place a wood chip on their shoulder and dare others to knock it off to start a fight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s had a chip on his shoulder ever since he was passed over for the promotion."
"A dime a dozen","<b>Meaning:</b> Something very common and not valuable or special. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to goods so plentiful they only cost ten cents for twelve items. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Generic action movies are a dime a dozen these days."
"A hot potato","<b>Meaning:</b> A controversial issue or situation that is awkward or unpleasant to deal with. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Based on the physical difficulty of holding a potato straight from a fire. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new gun control legislation has become a political hot potato."
"A penny for your thoughts","<b>Meaning:</b> A way of asking what someone is thinking about when they are quiet. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 1500s, when a penny was a significant enough amount of money to buy a person's attention. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’ve been staring out the window for ten minutes; a penny for your thoughts?"
"A piece of cake","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is very easy to accomplish. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 19th-century 'cake walks' where the prize was a cake and the competition was easy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't worry about the driving test; it’s a piece of cake."
"Add insult to injury","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a bad situation even worse through words or further action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an ancient Roman fable where a bald man missed a fly and hit his own head. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The car broke down, and then, to add insult to injury, I got a parking ticket."
"All ears","<b>Meaning:</b> Listening very carefully and eagerly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor suggesting that the person has become nothing but ears to capture every word. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Tell me the gossip; I’m all ears."
"An arm and a leg","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is extremely expensive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from the cost of portraits where the price increased if limbs were included in the painting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That designer watch cost him an arm and a leg."
"At the drop of a hat","<b>Meaning:</b> Doing something immediately without any hesitation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 19th century, where a race or a fight would start as soon as a hat was dropped as a signal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She is so helpful that she will come over and help you at the drop of a hat."
"Back to square one","<b>Meaning:</b> To start over from the very beginning because a previous attempt failed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from early board games or radio football commentary where the field was divided into squares. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our budget was rejected, so it's back to square one for the planning committee."
"Barking up the wrong tree","<b>Meaning:</b> Accusing the wrong person or following the wrong line of thought. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hunting dogs barking at a tree where they think a raccoon is hiding, though it has escaped. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you think I’m the one who leaked the secret, you’re barking up the wrong tree."
"Beat the clock","<b>Meaning:</b> To finish a task before a time limit expires. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Derived from competitive sports and game shows. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We managed to beat the clock and submit the report at 4:59 PM."
"Bite off more than you can chew","<b>Meaning:</b> To take on a task that is way too big or difficult to handle. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to taking a bite of food so large it cannot be chewed or swallowed easily. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I think I bit off more than I could chew by taking five classes this semester."
"Bite the bullet","<b>Meaning:</b> To endure a painful or difficult situation that cannot be avoided. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Before anesthesia, soldiers would bite on a lead bullet to endure the pain of battlefield surgery. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have to bite the bullet and tell my parents that I failed the exam."
"Break a leg","<b>Meaning:</b> A way to wish someone good luck, specifically before a performance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A theatrical superstition that wishing someone 'good luck' would actually cause bad luck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you’ll be great in the play tonight. Break a leg!"
"Break the ice","<b>Meaning:</b> To say or do something to relieve tension or get a conversation started. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ships called 'icebreakers' that cleared paths through frozen water for other boats. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He told a funny story at the start of the meeting to break the ice."
"Burn the midnight oil","<b>Meaning:</b> To work or study late into the night. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to using an oil lamp to work after dark before electricity was invented. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I had to burn the midnight oil to finish the project on time."
"Burn your bridges","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something that makes it impossible to return to a previous situation or relationship. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military strategy where a retreating army burns a bridge so they cannot return and the enemy cannot follow. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't insult your boss on the way out; you don't want to burn your bridges."
"Butterflies in your stomach","<b>Meaning:</b> To feel very nervous or anxious about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Describes the fluttery physical sensation caused by adrenaline and anxiety. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I always get butterflies in my stomach before a big presentation."
"By the skin of your teeth","<b>Meaning:</b> To barely succeed or escape a disaster. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the biblical Book of Job, implying a margin of error as thin as the non-existent skin on teeth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He passed the math test by the skin of his teeth."
"Caught red-handed","<b>Meaning:</b> Caught in the act of doing something wrong or illegal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old laws where a person was only guilty of poaching if caught with the victim's blood still on their hands. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The thief was caught red-handed as he was climbing out the window."
"Chew the fat","<b>Meaning:</b> To chat in a friendly and leisurely way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from sailors chewing on tough salt pork fat while talking, or simply the jaw movement of talking. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We spent the whole afternoon just chewing the fat on the porch."
"Chip off the old block","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is very similar in character or appearance to one of their parents. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing a person to a small piece of wood (the child) taken from a larger log (the parent). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Mark is a chip off the old block; he has his father’s sense of humor."
"Clam up","<b>Meaning:</b> To suddenly stop talking or refuse to provide information. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to a clam closing its shell tightly when it feels threatened. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As soon as the lawyer arrived, the suspect clammed up."
"Close but no cigar","<b>Meaning:</b> To be near success but ultimately fail. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From carnival games where cigars were given out as prizes; if you almost won, you got nothing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You were only one second off the world record—close but no cigar."
"Cost a pretty penny","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very expensive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 18th-century Britain, referring to a large sum of money. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That new kitchen renovation must have cost a pretty penny."
"Crocodile tears","<b>Meaning:</b> False or insincere display of grief or sorrow. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the ancient myth that crocodiles weep while eating their prey to deceive others. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't believe her crying; those are just crocodile tears."
"Curiosity killed the cat","<b>Meaning:</b> A warning that being too inquisitive or nosy can lead to trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally 'care killed the cat' (meaning worry), it evolved into 'curiosity' in the late 1800s. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I wouldn't go looking in that abandoned house if I were you; curiosity killed the cat."
"Cut corners","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something poorly or skip important steps to save time or money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From driving or hunting, where taking a shorter route 'cuts the corner' but is less thorough. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The contractor cut corners on the foundation, and now the house is sinking."
"Cut to the chase","<b>Meaning:</b> To get to the main point without wasting time on details. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From silent films where the romantic plot was skipped to get to the exciting chase scene. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't need the whole backstory—just cut to the chase and tell me who won."
"Don't count your chickens before they hatch","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't make plans based on something that hasn't happened yet. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From one of Aesop’s fables, where a woman counts the money she'll make from eggs before they are born. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You haven't been offered the job yet, so don't count your chickens before they hatch."
"Don't put all your eggs in one basket","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't risk everything you have on a single venture or idea. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal warning; if you drop the basket, you lose every egg you own. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Diversify your investments; don't put all your eggs in one basket."
"Down to earth","<b>Meaning:</b> Practical, humble, and realistic. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone who stays connected to the ground rather than having their 'head in the clouds.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Despite being a famous actor, he is incredibly down to earth."
"Draw the line","<b>Meaning:</b> To set a limit on what you will allow or do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from marking boundaries in a field or a court in sports. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'll help you with your homework, but I draw the line at writing the whole essay for you."
"Drive someone up the wall","<b>Meaning:</b> To annoy or irritate someone intensely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests making someone so crazy they want to climb the walls to escape the annoyance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That loud clicking sound is driving me up the wall."
"Elephant in the room","<b>Meaning:</b> A major problem or controversial issue that is present but everyone is ignoring. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Based on the idea that an elephant is too big to be missed, yet people pretend it isn't there. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> No one wanted to talk about the company's debt, which was the elephant in the room."
"Fit as a fiddle","<b>Meaning:</b> In very good physical health. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Fit' used to mean 'suitable.' A fiddle must be kept in good condition and tuned correctly to play well. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My grandmother is 85 and she’s still fit as a fiddle."
"From scratch","<b>Meaning:</b> From the very beginning using basic ingredients or materials. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sports like racing where a starting line was scratched into the dirt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He built the entire computer from scratch."
"Get out of hand","<b>Meaning:</b> To lose control of a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse riding; if the reins leave the rider's hands, the horse is out of control. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The protest started peacefully, but things quickly got out of hand."
"Go the extra mile","<b>Meaning:</b> To do more than what is strictly required or expected. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; Roman law allowed soldiers to force civilians to carry gear for one mile, and Jesus suggested carrying it for two. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our teacher always goes the extra mile to make sure every student understands."
"Green with envy","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very jealous or envious of someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Green has been associated with bile and illness since ancient Greece; Shakespeare popularized it as the color of jealousy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was green with envy when she saw her sister's new engagement ring."
"Hang in there","<b>Meaning:</b> A way to tell someone not to give up despite difficulties. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized by a 1970s poster of a kitten hanging onto a branch. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know the recovery is slow, but hang in there; you're getting stronger."
"Hit the nail on the head","<b>Meaning:</b> To describe exactly what is causing a situation or problem. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From carpentry; hitting the nail exactly on its head is the most effective and precise way to work. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You hit the nail on the head when you said we need a better marketing strategy."
"Hit the sack","<b>Meaning:</b> To go to bed or go to sleep. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to old mattresses that were essentially sacks filled with straw or hay. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m exhausted; it’s time for me to hit the sack."
"In the heat of the moment","<b>Meaning:</b> Overwhelmed by what is happening at a specific time, often leading to impulsive actions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Relates to the metaphorical 'heat' of passion or anger. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He said some mean things in the heat of the moment that he later regretted."
"It takes two to tango","<b>Meaning:</b> Both people involved in a difficult situation or argument are responsible for it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 1920s dance, the Tango, which cannot be performed alone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She blames him for the divorce, but it takes two to tango."
"Jump on the bandwagon","<b>Meaning:</b> To join a popular trend or activity to become successful or popular. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 19th-century political parades where people would literally jump on a wagon carrying a band. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Now that the team is winning, everyone is jumping on the bandwagon."
"Keep your chin up","<b>Meaning:</b> To remain positive and hopeful during a difficult time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for pride and confidence; looking down suggests sadness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you didn't get the job, but keep your chin up; something else will come along."
"Kill two birds with one stone","<b>Meaning:</b> To accomplish two different things at the same time with a single action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from ancient hunting practices using slingshots. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> By walking to work, I kill two birds with one stone: I save money and get exercise."
"Last straw","<b>Meaning:</b> The final problem in a series of problems that makes a situation intolerable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the proverb 'The straw that broke the camel's back.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was already unhappy with the pay, but the long hours were the last straw; I quit."
"Let the cat out of the bag","<b>Meaning:</b> To accidentally reveal a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 18th-century markets where a 'pig in a poke' (bag) was sold; if the bag was opened, it might reveal a cat instead of a pig. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We were planning a surprise party, but my brother let the cat out of the bag."
