"Level playing field","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where everyone has the same opportunities or advantages. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sports, where a field that is not flat would give one team an unfair advantage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Small businesses need a level playing field to compete with large corporations."
"Like a house on fire","<b>Meaning:</b> To get along with someone very quickly and very well. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to how quickly a fire spreads through a wooden house. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I introduced my two friends, and they got on like a house on fire."
"Long story short","<b>Meaning:</b> Used when you are leaving out details to get to the main point of a story quickly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal instruction to shorten a long narrative. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Long story short, we missed the flight and had to drive."
"Loose cannon","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is unpredictable and likely to cause trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old warships where a cannon that came loose from its moorings would roll dangerously across the deck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The CEO is a bit of a loose cannon; you never know what he’ll say to the press."
"Make a long story short","<b>Meaning:</b> To skip the boring or unnecessary details and get to the conclusion. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A conversational filler used to wrap up a complex explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> To make a long story short, we decided to sell the house."
"Miss the boat","<b>Meaning:</b> To be too late to take advantage of an opportunity. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor for arriving at the pier after the ship has departed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I waited too long to buy the tickets and I missed the boat; they are all sold out."
"No pain, no gain","<b>Meaning:</b> You must work hard and suffer a little if you want to achieve something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized by fitness culture in the 1980s, but dates back to 17th-century proverbs. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m sore from the gym, but no pain, no gain."
"Not playing with a full deck","<b>Meaning:</b> Suggesting that someone is not mentally sound or is unintelligent. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A card game metaphor; a game cannot be played correctly if cards are missing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After hearing his wild conspiracy theories, I realized he’s not playing with a full deck."
"Off the hook","<b>Meaning:</b> To be released from a responsibility or an unpleasant situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From fishing; if a fish gets off the hook, it escapes being caught. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My sister agreed to do the dishes, so I'm off the hook."
"On the ball","<b>Meaning:</b> To be alert, quick to understand, and efficient. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sports like baseball or cricket, where a player must watch the ball closely. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our new intern is really on the ball; she finishes everything ahead of time."
"Once in a blue moon","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that happens very rarely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'blue moon' is the second full moon in a single month, which only happens once every few years. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I only see my cousins from Australia once in a blue moon."
"Out of the blue","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that happens unexpectedly and without warning. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Short for 'a bolt from the blue,' referring to lightning hitting from a clear blue sky. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He called me out of the blue after five years of silence."
"Over the moon","<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely happy or delighted. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the nursery rhyme 'Hey Diddle Diddle,' where the cow jumped over the moon. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was over the moon when she found out she won the lottery."
"Piece of cake","<b>Meaning:</b> Something very easy to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 1800s, when cakes were given as prizes for easy competitions. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Fixing the sink was a piece of cake."
"Pull someone's leg","<b>Meaning:</b> To joke with someone or tease them by telling them something that isn't true. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Historically, it meant to trip someone up; now it refers to 'tripping' them with a lie. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't really win the lottery; I was just pulling your leg."
"Pull yourself together","<b>Meaning:</b> To calm down and recover your self-control after being upset or angry. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for collecting your scattered emotions and 'assembling' yourself. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you’re stressed, but you need to pull yourself together for the interview."
"Put someone on the spot","<b>Meaning:</b> To force someone to answer a difficult question or make a decision immediately. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from old practices of placing someone in a specific place for public questioning or execution. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The teacher really put me on the spot when she asked me for the answer in front of everyone."
"Rain on someone's parade","<b>Meaning:</b> To spoil someone's plans or prevent them from enjoying their success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor; rain would ruin the festivities of a planned parade. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I hate to rain on your parade, but the concert has been canceled."
"Rock the boat","<b>Meaning:</b> To do or say something that upsets a stable situation or causes trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor; moving around in a small boat can make it tip over. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone is happy with the current schedule, so please don't rock the boat."
"See eye to eye","<b>Meaning:</b> To agree fully with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Isaiah 52:8), describing people looking directly at each other in agreement. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My business partner and I don't always see eye to eye on marketing."
"Sit on the fence","<b>Meaning:</b> To avoid making a decision or choosing a side in an argument. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone sitting on a boundary between two sides, belonging to neither. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You can't sit on the fence forever; you need to choose which candidate to support."
"Skeleton in the closet","<b>Meaning:</b> A shocking or embarrassing secret from the past that someone wants to hide. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 19th century, referring to the literal hiding of a body to avoid scandal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Every family has a skeleton in the closet."
"Speak of the devil","<b>Meaning:</b> Used when the person you were just talking about suddenly appears. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the old superstition 'Speak of the Devil and he shall appear.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Hi Tom! Speak of the devil—we were just talking about you."
"Spill the beans","<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal a secret, often accidentally. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ancient Greek voting where beans were used; spilling the container would reveal the secret results early. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't spill the beans about the surprise party!"
"Take it with a grain of salt","<b>Meaning:</b> To not take something too seriously or to be skeptical of what someone says. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ancient times; it was believed that food was easier to swallow or poisons were less effective if taken with salt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He tends to exaggerate, so take his stories with a grain of salt."
"The ball is in your court","<b>Meaning:</b> It is now your responsibility to make the next move or decision. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From tennis, where you must hit the ball back once it lands on your side. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ve sent the contract; now the ball is in your court."
"The best of both worlds","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where you can enjoy the advantages of two very different things at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A modern phrase used to describe ideal balance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Working from home gives me the best of both worlds: a good salary and more time with my kids."
"The elephant in the room","<b>Meaning:</b> An obvious problem that no one wants to discuss. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The impossibility of having an elephant in a room and pretending it isn't there. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to address the elephant in the room: our company is losing money."
"The last straw","<b>Meaning:</b> The final problem in a series that makes you lose your patience. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the phrase 'the straw that broke the camel's back.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When he showed up late for the third time, it was the last straw, and I fired him."
"The whole nine yards","<b>Meaning:</b> Everything possible or available; the full amount. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Disputed, but possibly from the length of ammunition belts in WWII aircraft or the amount of fabric in a formal kilt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> For our anniversary, he went the whole nine yards with dinner, flowers, and a limo."
"Through thick and thin","<b>Meaning:</b> To stay together or remain loyal through both good and bad times. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old English 'through thicket and thin wood,' referring to a difficult journey. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They have been best friends through thick and thin for twenty years."
"Throw in the towel","<b>Meaning:</b> To give up or admit defeat. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; when a fighter's coach throws a towel into the ring to stop the fight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After failing the test for the fourth time, he decided to throw in the towel."
"To make matters worse","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something that makes a bad situation even more difficult. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of escalating a problem. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I lost my keys, and to make matters worse, it started raining."
"Under the weather","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling sick or not well. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; when sailors felt sick, they would go below deck to get away from the 'weather' (wind and rain). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I won't be coming to the party because I'm feeling a bit under the weather."
"Up in the air","<b>Meaning:</b> Uncertain or not yet decided. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Metaphor for something that has not 'landed' or settled into a final position. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our travel plans are still up in the air because of the flight delays."
"Walking on eggshells","<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely cautious about what you say or do around someone to avoid upsetting them. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the extreme care needed to walk on delicate eggshells without breaking them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone at the office is walking on eggshells because the boss is in a terrible mood."
"Water under the bridge","<b>Meaning:</b> Problems or events that happened in the past and are no longer important or worth worrying about. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for flowing water that has already passed by and cannot return. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We had our arguments, but that’s all water under the bridge now."
"Weather the storm","<b>Meaning:</b> To survive a difficult period or situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A nautical term referring to a ship surviving a literal storm at sea. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company struggled during the recession, but they managed to weather the storm."
"When pigs fly","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that will never happen. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An old proverb used to mock the idea of impossible events. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> 'Do you think he'll apologize?' 'Yeah, when pigs fly!'"
"Wrap your head around something","<b>Meaning:</b> To understand something complicated or strange. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for your mind physically surrounding an idea to contain it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m trying to wrap my head around the new tax laws, but they are so confusing."
"You can't judge a book by its cover","<b>Meaning:</b> You shouldn't form an opinion about someone or something based only on outward appearance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal warning that a boring-looking book might be a masterpiece inside. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He looks mean, but he’s actually very kind; you can't judge a book by its cover."
"Your guess is as good as mine","<b>Meaning:</b> I have no idea; I don't know the answer either. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A modern conversational phrase expressing shared ignorance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> 'Why is the bus late?' 'Your guess is as good as mine.'"
"Zip your lip","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop talking or keep a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for physically zipping a mouth shut. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You have to zip your lip about the surprise party!"
"Actions speak louder than words","<b>Meaning:</b> What someone does is more important than what they say they will do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Dates back to the 1600s, popularized in the 1800s in American politics. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He says he loves me, but he never helps around the house; actions speak louder than words."
"Add fuel to the fire","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a bad situation even worse or more intense. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor for how fire grows when fuel is added. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Bringing up their old argument only added fuel to the fire."
"As right as rain","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling perfectly healthy or correct. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain, but possibly because rain is 'right' and necessary for the earth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was sick last week, but I'm as right as rain now."
"Barking up the wrong tree","<b>Meaning:</b> Pursuing a mistaken line of thought or course of action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Raccoon hunting dogs barking at a tree after the animal has leaped to another. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He thinks the problem is the software, but he's barking up the wrong tree; it's the hardware."
"Beat around the bush","<b>Meaning:</b> Avoiding the main topic or not speaking directly about an issue. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hunting; beaters would hit the bushes to flush out birds rather than going directly into the brush. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop beating around the bush and tell me if I'm fired."
"Front","Back"
"Achilles' heel","<b>Meaning:</b> A small but fatal weakness in spite of overall strength. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Greek mythology; the hero Achilles was dipped in the River Styx by his mother, making him invulnerable everywhere except the heel she held him by. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Mathematics has always been my Achilles' heel in competitive exams."
"Apple of discord","<b>Meaning:</b> A cause of envy or quarrel. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Greek myth of the Golden Apple of Discord which led to the Trojan War. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The ancestral property became an apple of discord between the two brothers."
"At arm's length","<b>Meaning:</b> To keep someone at a distance to avoid intimacy or conflict. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for keeping someone far enough away so they cannot touch or influence you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It is better to keep dishonest people at arm's length."
"Back to the drawing board","<b>Meaning:</b> To start a task over because the first attempt failed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely originated in the architectural or engineering fields where designs are literally drawn on boards. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The marketing plan failed, so it's back to the drawing board for the team."
"Beat around the bush","<b>Meaning:</b> To avoid the main topic or speak indirectly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hunting; participants would beat bushes to scare birds into the air rather than hitting the bush directly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop beating around the bush and tell me exactly what happened."
"Bed of thorns","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation full of difficulties and sorrows. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The opposite of 'bed of roses,' symbolizing pain and hardship. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The life of a refugee is often a bed of thorns."
"Between the devil and the deep blue sea","<b>Meaning:</b> To be in a dilemma with two equally unpleasant choices. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A nautical term referring to a sailor suspended over the side of the ship (the devil) with the ocean below. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was between the devil and the deep blue sea when both companies offered me difficult contracts."
"By hook or by crook","<b>Meaning:</b> By any means possible, fair or unfair. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to medieval forest laws where peasants could only gather firewood they could reach with a shepherd’s hook or a reaper’s crook. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He decided to win the election by hook or by crook."
"Call a spade a spade","<b>Meaning:</b> To speak plainly and truthfully about something, even if it is unpleasant. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Translated from ancient Greek, where 'spade' referred to a simple digging tool, implying no fancy language. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Let's call a spade a spade: the project failed because of poor leadership."
"Cats and dogs","<b>Meaning:</b> To rain very heavily. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain, but possibly from old street drainage systems where dead animals were washed away during heavy storms. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We couldn't go out because it was raining cats and dogs."
"Cock and bull story","<b>Meaning:</b> An unbelievable or ridiculous story used as an excuse. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from two inns in England ('The Cock' and 'The Bull') where travelers told tall tales. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He told me some cock and bull story about why he was late for the exam."
"Cold shoulder","<b>Meaning:</b> To intentionally ignore or be unfriendly to someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Allegedly from the custom of serving a cold shoulder of mutton (a cheap, cold cut) to an unwanted guest. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Ever since the argument, she has been giving me the cold shoulder."
"Cry wolf","<b>Meaning:</b> To give a false alarm. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fable 'The Boy Who Cried Wolf,' where a boy lied about a wolf so many times that no one believed him when one actually arrived. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you keep complaining about small things, people will think you're crying wolf when something real happens."
"Dark horse","<b>Meaning:</b> A candidate or competitor about whom little is known but who unexpectedly wins. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing, where a 'dark' horse was one not known to the gamblers. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was the dark horse of the BCS competition, eventually securing the first position."
"Eat humble pie","<b>Meaning:</b> To apologize and face humiliation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'umble pie,' made from the 'umbles' (innards) of a deer, which was served to lower-class people. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After mocking the winner, he was forced to eat humble pie when his own results were published."
"Every cloud has a silver lining","<b>Meaning:</b> Every difficult or sad situation has a positive aspect. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> First used by John Milton in his poem 'Comus' (1634). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Losing my job was hard, but it gave me time to study for the bank exam; every cloud has a silver lining."
"Face the music","<b>Meaning:</b> To accept the unpleasant consequences of one's actions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from the military practice of drumming a dismissed soldier out of the regiment to the sound of music. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After breaking the window, the child had to go home and face the music."
"Fair weather friend","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is a friend only when times are good. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A nautical metaphor; just as fair weather makes sailing easy, these friends only stick around when life is easy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I thought he was a close friend, but he turned out to be a fair weather friend when I lost my money."
"Feather in one's cap","<b>Meaning:</b> An achievement to be proud of. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the custom of warriors adding a feather to their headgear for every enemy they killed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Winning the national debate was a real feather in his cap."
"Fish out of water","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is uncomfortable in a specific situation or environment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal comparison to a fish that cannot breathe or function outside of its natural habitat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As a scientist among artists, I felt like a fish out of water."
"Fly in the ointment","<b>Meaning:</b> A small defect that spoils something valuable or pleasant. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Ecclesiastes 10:1), referring to dead flies making a perfumer's ointment stink. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The picnic was perfect; the only fly in the ointment was the mosquitoes."
"Gift of the gab","<b>Meaning:</b> The ability to speak easily and persuasively. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Gab' is an old word for chatter or talk; possibly linked to the Blarney Stone in Ireland. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> To be a successful salesman, you must have the gift of the gab."
"Hard nut to crack","<b>Meaning:</b> A problem that is very difficult to solve or a person difficult to understand. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal comparison to a nut with a shell so tough it is hard to open. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The final question in the math paper was a hard nut to crack."
"In a nutshell","<b>Meaning:</b> Briefly and clearly summarized. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comes from the idea of writing a large amount of text so small that it could fit inside a nutshell. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> To put it in a nutshell, we need more funding to finish the project."
"Kith and kin","<b>Meaning:</b> Friends and relatives. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Kith' originally meant 'known people' (friends), and 'Kin' meant family. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He invited all his kith and kin to the wedding."
"Lion's share","<b>Meaning:</b> The largest or best part of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables, where a lion hunts with other animals but claims all the meat for himself. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The eldest son took the lion's share of the inheritance."
"Maiden speech","<b>Meaning:</b> The first speech made by a person, typically in parliament. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Maiden' signifies 'first' or 'original' in this context. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Her maiden speech in the parliament was highly praised by the senior leaders."
"Nip in the bud","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop something at an early stage before it can develop. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A gardening metaphor; if you nip a flower bud, the flower will never grow. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We must nip this bad habit in the bud before it becomes a serious problem."
"Once in a blue moon","<b>Meaning:</b> Very rarely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'blue moon' is a second full moon in a single calendar month, which happens only about once every 2.7 years. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He visits his hometown only once in a blue moon."
"Pros and cons","<b>Meaning:</b> The advantages and disadvantages of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Abbreviation of the Latin phrase 'pro et contra' (for and against). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We weighed the pros and cons before deciding to move to the city."
"Front","Back"
"Achilles' heel","<b>Meaning:</b> A small but fatal weakness in spite of overall strength. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Greek mythology; the hero Achilles was dipped in the River Styx by his mother, making him invulnerable everywhere except the heel she held him by. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Mathematics has always been my Achilles' heel in competitive exams."

"Nip in the bud","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop something at an early stage before it can develop. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A gardening metaphor; if you nip a flower bud, the flower will never grow. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We must nip this bad habit in the bud before it becomes a serious problem."
"Once in a blue moon","<b>Meaning:</b> Very rarely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'blue moon' is a second full moon in a single calendar month, which happens only about once every 2.7 years. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He visits his hometown only once in a blue moon."
"Pros and cons","<b>Meaning:</b> The advantages and disadvantages of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Abbreviation of the Latin phrase 'pro et contra' (for and against). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We weighed the pros and cons before deciding to move to the city."
"Front","Back"
"Back to the drawing board","<b>Meaning:</b> Starting over from the beginning because a previous attempt failed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the world of design and engineering where a failed blueprint requires a new one. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The prototype failed the safety test, so it's back to the drawing board."
"Bail someone out","<b>Meaning:</b> To rescue someone from a difficult situation, often financial. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Removing water from a sinking boat using a bucket (bailing). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His parents had to bail him out after he overspent on his credit card."
"Barking up the wrong tree","<b>Meaning:</b> Following a false lead or accusing the wrong person. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hunting dogs barking at a tree that the prey has already escaped from. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you think I took your lunch, you're barking up the wrong tree."
"Beat someone to the punch","<b>Meaning:</b> To do or achieve something before someone else does. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; landing a hit before your opponent can. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I wanted to buy that house, but someone else beat me to the punch."
"Bend the rules","<b>Meaning:</b> To allow an exception to a rule without officially changing it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Treating a rigid rule as if it were flexible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The library is closed, but since you're almost done, I'll bend the rules and give you five more minutes."
"Best of both worlds","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where you can enjoy the advantages of two very different things at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 19th-century expression. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Working from home gives her the best of both worlds: a career and time with family."
"Bite the bullet","<b>Meaning:</b> To endure a painful or difficult situation that is unavoidable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Before anesthesia, soldiers would bite on a bullet during surgery to cope with pain. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I hate the dentist, but I’ll just have to bite the bullet and go."
"Bite off more than you can chew","<b>Meaning:</b> To take on a task that is way too big or difficult. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Taking a larger piece of food than your mouth can handle. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> By taking three jobs, she bit off more than she could chew."
"Blessing in disguise","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that seems bad at first but results in something good later. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 1746 hymn by James Hervey. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Losing that job was a blessing in disguise because I found a much better one."
"Blindside someone","<b>Meaning:</b> To surprise someone with a negative situation they didn't see coming. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From American football; being hit from a direction the player cannot see. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The sudden layoffs completely blindsided the staff."
"Blood, sweat, and tears","<b>Meaning:</b> Extreme hard work and effort. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized by Winston Churchill during WWII. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I put blood, sweat, and tears into building this company."
"Blow the whistle","<b>Meaning:</b> To report a person or organization for doing something illegal or dishonest. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Referees using a whistle to stop play when a rule is broken. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The accountant blew the whistle on the company's illegal tax scheme."
"Bottom of the barrel","<b>Meaning:</b> The lowest quality or the last remaining options. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The sediment and worst bits of food found at the bottom of a storage barrel. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> These applicants are the bottom of the barrel; we need to find better candidates."
"Break a leg","<b>Meaning:</b> A superstitious way to say 'good luck' to a performer. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Theatrical belief that wishing for good luck would cause bad luck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you'll be great in the play tonight—break a leg!"
"Bring to the table","<b>Meaning:</b> To provide something of value to a group or negotiation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Bringing food to a communal meal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She brings a lot of marketing experience to the table."
"Burn the candle at both ends","<b>Meaning:</b> To work too hard without getting enough rest. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A candle lit at both ends burns out twice as fast. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’ll get sick if you keep burning the candle at both ends."
"Buy time","<b>Meaning:</b> To delay an event or decision to gain an advantage. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Treating time as a commodity that can be purchased through delay. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I told them the check was in the mail to buy us some time."
"Call the shots","<b>Meaning:</b> To be the person in charge and make the important decisions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From target shooting where the shooter announces where they will hit. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> While the CEO is away, the vice president calls the shots."
"Card up your sleeve","<b>Meaning:</b> A secret advantage or plan. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Cheating at cards by hiding a strong card in your clothing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If they won't lower the price, I still have a card up my sleeve."
"Cash in your chips","<b>Meaning:</b> To sell something or quit something to get the profit; or to die. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; exchanging betting chips for real money at the end of a game. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He decided to cash in his chips and retire to Florida."
"Change of pace","<b>Meaning:</b> A temporary change from one's normal routine. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Shifting the speed of a journey. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Going to the mountains was a nice change of pace from the city."
"Chip on your shoulder","<b>Meaning:</b> To be easily angered or have a persistent grudge. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Challenging someone to a fight by daring them to knock a wood chip off your shoulder. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s had a chip on his shoulder ever since he was fired."
"Climb the corporate ladder","<b>Meaning:</b> To advance in a company's hierarchy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the vertical structure of a business. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He worked long hours to climb the corporate ladder."
"Close, but no cigar","<b>Meaning:</b> Almost successful but failing in the end. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fairground games used to give cigars as prizes for winning. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You almost beat the record—close, but no cigar."
"Come clean","<b>Meaning:</b> To tell the whole truth about something hidden. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Removing the 'dirt' of secrecy or lies. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It's time to come clean about where the money went."
"Cool your heels","<b>Meaning:</b> To be kept waiting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Letting a horse's hooves cool down after a long journey. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We were left to cool our heels in the waiting room for an hour."
"Corner the market","<b>Meaning:</b> To gain enough control of a commodity or service to set the price. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Forcing competitors into a 'corner' where they cannot move. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That company has cornered the market on smartphone screens."
"Cut corners","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something in the easiest or cheapest way, often sacrificing quality. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Moving across a corner instead of following the path to save distance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The builder cut corners on the foundation, and now the house is sinking."
"Cut to the chase","<b>Meaning:</b> To get to the point without wasting time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Silent films where the 'chase scene' was the most important part. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We only have ten minutes, so please cut to the chase."
"Dead heat","<b>Meaning:</b> A competition that ends in a perfect tie. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Horse racing; a 'heat' is a race that leaves the horses 'dead' (exhausted). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The election ended in a dead heat, requiring a recount."
"Deep pockets","<b>Meaning:</b> Having a lot of money or financial resources. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a person who can reach deep into their pockets for cash. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need an investor with deep pockets to fund this project."
"Deliver the goods","<b>Meaning:</b> To do what you promised or what is expected of you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fulfilling a shipping order. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He talks a lot, but he always delivers the goods."
"Diamond in the rough","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone with great potential who lacks training or polish. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Natural diamonds look like rough stones until they are cut. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new singer is a diamond in the rough; she just needs a coach."
"Dirt cheap","<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely inexpensive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Dirt is valueless and available everywhere. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I got this shirt dirt cheap at the clearance sale."
"Dog-eat-dog","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where people are ruthlessly competitive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 16th-century proverb about extreme survival. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The advertising industry is a dog-eat-dog world."
"Don't count your chickens before they hatch","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't assume success before it actually happens. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Not all eggs will produce a live chicken. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You haven't got the job yet, so don't count your chickens."
"Don't put all your eggs in one basket","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't risk everything on a single plan or investment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> If you drop the basket, you lose all the eggs. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You should invest in several companies; don't put all your eggs in one basket."
"Down to the wire","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is decided at the very last second. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The finish line of a horse race was marked by a wire. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The championship went down to the wire."
"Drag your feet","<b>Meaning:</b> To act slowly or reluctantly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Physically walking slowly to show you don't want to go. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The government is dragging its feet on passing the new law."
"Drop the ball","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a mistake or fail in a responsibility. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sports like football or baseball. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I really dropped the ball by forgetting to call the client back."
"Easy as pie","<b>Meaning:</b> Very simple to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the ease of eating a pie, not making one. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Setting up the new computer was easy as pie."
"Eat your words","<b>Meaning:</b> To admit that what you said was wrong. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Taking back your speech. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He said I'd never win, but he had to eat his words when I got the trophy."
"Eleventh hour","<b>Meaning:</b> At the last possible moment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; laborers hired just before the workday ended. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The deal was saved at the eleventh hour."
"Every cloud has a silver lining","<b>Meaning:</b> Every bad situation has a positive side. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a poem by John Milton. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I lost my flight, but I met an old friend at the airport; every cloud has a silver lining."
"Face the music","<b>Meaning:</b> To accept the unpleasant consequences of your actions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from being 'drummed out' of the military. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You broke the window; now you have to face the music."
"Fast track","<b>Meaning:</b> The quickest route to success or a goal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A dry, firm horse racing track that allows for high speeds. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She is on the fast track to becoming a partner at the firm."
"Feather your own nest","<b>Meaning:</b> To make yourself rich, often using unfair or dishonest means. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Birds using soft feathers to make their nests comfortable. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The politician was accused of feathering his own nest with tax money."
"Fill someone's shoes","<b>Meaning:</b> To take over someone's role and do it as well as they did. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for taking on another person's path. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The old manager was great; it will be hard to fill his shoes."
"Fine-tune","<b>Meaning:</b> To make small adjustments to make something work perfectly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Tuning a musical instrument or radio frequency. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We just need to fine-tune the engine before the race."
"Flash in the pan","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is successful for a very short time and then disappears. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Gunpowder flashing in a musket pan without firing the bullet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That band had one hit, but they were just a flash in the pan."
"Fly off the handle","<b>Meaning:</b> To lose your temper suddenly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An axe head becoming loose and flying off its wooden handle. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He tends to fly off the handle whenever he's under stress."
"Follow suit","<b>Meaning:</b> To do the same thing as someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From card games where you must play a card of the same suit. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When the first shop lowered its prices, the others followed suit."
"Food for thought","<b>Meaning:</b> Something worth thinking about carefully. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing information to food that needs to be digested. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The lecture gave us a lot of food for thought."
"From the horse's mouth","<b>Meaning:</b> Directly from the person who has first-hand knowledge. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Horse racing; getting a tip from the horse's owner or trainer. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know they're getting married; I heard it straight from the horse's mouth."
"Gain ground","<b>Meaning:</b> To make progress or become more successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; moving forward and taking territory from the enemy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our new product is finally starting to gain ground against the competition."
"Get a second wind","<b>Meaning:</b> To get a new burst of energy after being tired. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Running; when breathing becomes easier after the initial struggle. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was exhausted, but after a snack, I got a second wind."
"Get off on the wrong foot","<b>Meaning:</b> To start a relationship or task badly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient superstition that it was bad luck to step with the left foot first. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m sorry I was late; I don't want to get off on the wrong foot."
"Get out of hand","<b>Meaning:</b> To become uncontrollable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Losing control of a horse's reins. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The protest got out of hand when the crowds started breaking windows."
"Get wind of","<b>Meaning:</b> To hear a rumor or secret information. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hunting; an animal smelling a predator on the wind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The reporters got wind of the scandal before it was announced."
"Give someone the cold shoulder","<b>Meaning:</b> To intentionally ignore or be unfriendly to someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Serving an unwanted guest a cold piece of meat instead of a warm meal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After their argument, she gave him the cold shoulder for a week."
"Go back on your word","<b>Meaning:</b> To break a promise. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Reversing a statement of intent. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He promised to help me move, but he went back on his word."
"Go the extra mile","<b>Meaning:</b> To do more than what is required. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; Roman law forced people to carry a soldier's pack for one mile. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our teacher really goes the extra mile to help us learn."
"Go with the flow","<b>Meaning:</b> To accept a situation without trying to change it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Moving with the current of a river. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't have a plan for the holiday; I'll just go with the flow."
"Grease someone's palm","<b>Meaning:</b> To bribe someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for making a transaction 'slick' or 'smooth' with money. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You might have to grease his palm to get the permit quickly."
"Hands down","<b>Meaning:</b> Easily and without any doubt. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A jockey so far ahead they can drop their hands and still win. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She is hands down the best singer in the class."
"Hard nut to crack","<b>Meaning:</b> A difficult problem or a person who is hard to understand. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Literal difficulty of opening a nut with a tough shell. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This encryption is a very hard nut to crack."
"Have a sweet tooth","<b>Meaning:</b> To have a great love for sugary foods. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 14th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't say no to cake; I have a real sweet tooth."
"Head over heels","<b>Meaning:</b> Completely and deeply (usually in love). <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally 'heels over head,' describing a physical tumble. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He fell head over heels in love with her."
"Hear it on the grapevine","<b>Meaning:</b> To hear news through gossip or informal communication. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Civil War telegraph lines that looked like tangled vines. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I heard on the grapevine that the boss is retiring."
"Hit the books","<b>Meaning:</b> To study hard. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> American student slang. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a big exam tomorrow, so I need to hit the books."
"Hit the nail on the head","<b>Meaning:</b> To describe exactly what is causing a situation or problem. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Carpentry; hitting the nail perfectly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You hit the nail on the head when you said we're understaffed."
"Hold your horses","<b>Meaning:</b> To wait or slow down. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Pulling the reins of a horse-drawn carriage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Hold your horses! I’m not ready yet."
"In a nutshell","<b>Meaning:</b> In a few words; summarized. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The idea of fitting a large amount of text into the small space of a nutshell. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> In a nutshell, we need more money or the project will stop."
"In the same boat","<b>Meaning:</b> In the same difficult situation as others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> If a boat sinks, everyone on board is in trouble together. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We all lost our jobs today, so we're in the same boat."
"It takes two to tango","<b>Meaning:</b> Both people in an argument or situation are responsible. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The tango dance cannot be performed alone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He blames his wife for the fight, but it takes two to tango."
"Keep an eye on","<b>Meaning:</b> To watch something or someone carefully. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Keeping your vision fixed on a target. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Can you keep an eye on my bag while I go to the restroom?"
"Keep your chin up","<b>Meaning:</b> To stay positive in a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Looking up shows confidence; looking down shows sadness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you're sad, but keep your chin up; things will get better."
"Kill two birds with one stone","<b>Meaning:</b> To achieve two different things with one action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Efficiency in hunting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'll go to the bank while I'm at the grocery store and kill two birds with one stone."
"Last resort","<b>Meaning:</b> The final option used only when everything else has failed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Legal term for the final court of appeal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We will go to court only as a last resort."
"Let the cat out of the bag","<b>Meaning:</b> To accidentally reveal a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Dishonest merchants selling a cat in a bag instead of a pig. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't let the cat out of the bag about the surprise party!"
"Level playing field","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation that is fair for everyone involved. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sports; a field that isn't sloped gives no advantage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need a level playing field so small businesses can compete."
"Long story short","<b>Meaning:</b> To get to the main point without all the details. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Summarizing a long narrative. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Long story short, we missed the train and had to walk."
"Look before you leap","<b>Meaning:</b> Think carefully about the risks before doing something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> One of Aesop's fables. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s a big investment; you should look before you leap."
"Make a killing","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a large amount of money very quickly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hunting; a successful kill provided food for a long time. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He made a killing on the stock market last year."
"Money doesn't grow on trees","<b>Meaning:</b> Money is a limited resource and must be earned. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Parental warning. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You can't have a new toy every day; money doesn't grow on trees."
"No pain, no gain","<b>Meaning:</b> You must work hard and suffer a little to be successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Exercise and weightlifting culture. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Training for the marathon is hard, but no pain, no gain."
"Not my cup of tea","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that you do not like or are not interested in. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> British expression of preference. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't really like opera; it's not my cup of tea."
"Once in a blue moon","<b>Meaning:</b> Very rarely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The rare occurrence of a second full moon in a single month. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I only see my old classmates once in a blue moon."
"Out of the blue","<b>Meaning:</b> Suddenly and unexpectedly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A bolt of lightning from a clear blue sky. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She called me out of the blue after five years."
"Piece of cake","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is very easy to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 19th-century competitions where a cake was an easy prize to win. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That math test was a piece of cake."
"Play it by ear","<b>Meaning:</b> To decide how to act as a situation develops. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Playing a musical instrument without sheet music. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We don't have a plan yet; let's just play it by ear."
"Pull yourself together","<b>Meaning:</b> To calm down and control your emotions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Metaphor for gathering one's scattered thoughts or feelings. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop crying and pull yourself together; we have work to do."
"Put your foot in your mouth","<b>Meaning:</b> To say something embarrassing or hurtful by accident. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a physical social blunder. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I really put my foot in my mouth when I asked about her husband; I didn't know they divorced."
"Raise the bar","<b>Meaning:</b> To increase standards or expectations. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> High jump or pole vaulting; making the jump harder. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Her excellent presentation has really raised the bar for the rest of us."
"Read between the lines","<b>Meaning:</b> To find a hidden meaning in what someone says or writes. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Writing secret messages in invisible ink between lines of text. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He says he's not angry, but if you read between the lines, you can tell he is."
"Ring a bell","<b>Meaning:</b> To sound familiar. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from Pavlov's experiments with bells and memory. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Her name rings a bell, but I can't remember where we met."
"Rule of thumb","<b>Meaning:</b> A general principle or rough guide based on experience. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using the thumb as a basic unit of measurement. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> A good rule of thumb is to cook pasta for about ten minutes."
"See eye to eye","<b>Meaning:</b> To agree completely with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My sister and I don't always see eye to eye on fashion."
"Sit tight","<b>Meaning:</b> To wait patiently and not take any action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Staying still to avoid being noticed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Just sit tight; help is on the way."
"Sleep on it","<b>Meaning:</b> To wait until the next day before making a decision. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Letting the subconscious mind process information overnight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s a big decision; why don't you sleep on it?"
"Spill the beans","<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Greek voting using colored beans. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Who spilled the beans about the surprise party?"
"Front","Back"
"A perfect storm","<b>Meaning:</b> A particularly bad situation caused by a combination of unfavorable circumstances. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Meteorological; when several weather systems collide to create a massive storm. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> High interest rates and a supply shortage created a perfect storm for the housing market."
"All roads lead to Rome","<b>Meaning:</b> There are many different ways to reach the same goal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the ancient Roman Empire, where the road system was designed to radiate from the capital. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Whether we use this software or that one, all roads lead to Rome; the data stays the same."
"An axe to grind","<b>Meaning:</b> Having a private reason for being involved in a project or being angry. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Benjamin Franklin wrote a story about a man who tricked a boy into sharpening his axe for him. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She isn't criticizing the book because it's bad; she has an axe to grind with the author."
"At the end of one's rope","<b>Meaning:</b> To have no more patience or strength left to deal with a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Tethering an animal; if it reaches the end of the rope, it can go no further. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I've been trying to fix this car for hours and I'm at the end of my rope."
"Back to square one","<b>Meaning:</b> To start over because a previous attempt failed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from early radio broadcasts of football or board games where the starting point was Square 1. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The client rejected our design, so it’s back to square one."
"Barking up the wrong tree","<b>Meaning:</b> Pursuing a mistaken line of thought or course of action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hunting dogs barking at a tree that the prey has already left. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you think I took the money, you're barking up the wrong tree."
"Beating a dead horse","<b>Meaning:</b> To keep talking about a subject that has already been decided or discussed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of trying to make a dead animal move by whipping it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We’ve already said no to the plan; stop beating a dead horse."
"Bite your tongue","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a great effort to avoid saying something that might be offensive or hurtful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Physically stopping speech. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I had to bite my tongue when he started bragging about his 'talent'."
"Blood is thicker than water","<b>Meaning:</b> Family relationships are more important than other types of relationships. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely 12th-century German/English origins; though some argue the original meant the opposite (blood of the covenant). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't like my cousin much, but I'll help him; blood is thicker than water."
"Blow hot and cold","<b>Meaning:</b> To keep changing your attitude or opinion about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> One of Aesop's fables where a man blows on his hands to warm them and on soup to cool it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> One day he loves the idea, the next he hates it; he really blows hot and cold."
"Break the ice","<b>Meaning:</b> To do or say something to relieve tension or get a conversation started. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ships called icebreakers clearing a path through frozen water. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I told a joke to break the ice during the interview."
"Burn your bridges","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something that makes it impossible to return to a previous state. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; destroying a bridge so the enemy cannot follow, but also preventing your own retreat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Quitting without notice will burn your bridges with this company."
"Buy the farm","<b>Meaning:</b> To die, especially in battle or an accident. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> WWII slang; if a pilot crashed on a farm, the insurance would 'buy the farm' (pay it off) for the farmer. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s a dangerous job; many pilots have bought the farm on that route."
"By the book","<b>Meaning:</b> Following all the rules and procedures exactly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Referring to the 'rule book' or legal code. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The inspector does everything by the book."
"Call it a day","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop working on something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Deciding that a full day's work has been completed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We're all tired; let's call it a day."
"Cast the first stone","<b>Meaning:</b> To be the first to criticize someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (John 8:7) regarding judging others. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Before you criticize her for being late, remember you were late yesterday; don't cast the first stone."
"Change of heart","<b>Meaning:</b> A shift in opinion or feeling. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The heart as the center of decision-making and emotion. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was going to sell the house, but he had a change of heart."
"Chew the fat","<b>Meaning:</b> To chat casually or gossip. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely sailors chewing on tough salt pork while talking. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We spent the afternoon chewing the fat on the porch."
"Clean as a whistle","<b>Meaning:</b> Completely clean, or completely legal and honest. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A whistle must be clear to make a pure sound. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I checked his background; he’s clean as a whistle."
"Cold feet","<b>Meaning:</b> To become nervous or afraid to do something you had planned. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from 19th-century literature; metaphor for having no 'ground' to stand on. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He got cold feet and canceled the wedding at the last minute."
"Come hell or high water","<b>Meaning:</b> No matter what difficulties arise. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Referencing fire (hell) and floods (high water). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ll be there for your graduation, come hell or high water."
"Crack the whip","<b>Meaning:</b> To use your authority to make people work harder. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse or ox drivers using a whip to speed up the animals. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The manager had to crack the whip to get the project finished."
"Curiosity killed the cat","<b>Meaning:</b> Being too inquisitive can lead to trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A warning against prying into things that aren't your business. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I wouldn't go in there if I were you—curiosity killed the cat."
"Cut to the quick","<b>Meaning:</b> To hurt someone's feelings deeply. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The 'quick' is the sensitive flesh under the fingernails. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Her harsh criticism cut him to the quick."
"Dead as a doornail","<b>Meaning:</b> Completely dead or unusable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nails in doors were hammered through and bent over (clenched), making them 'dead' for any other use. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I dropped my laptop and now it's dead as a doornail."
"Devil's advocate","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who takes a position they don't necessarily agree with to start an argument or test a theory. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A role in the Catholic Church to argue against a candidate for sainthood. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I agree with the plan, but let me play devil's advocate for a moment."
"Diamond in the rough","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who has good qualities but lacks social grace or education. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncut diamonds look like ordinary stones until they are polished. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That student is a diamond in the rough; he just needs some guidance."
"Dog in the manger","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who prevents others from using something they do not need themselves. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> One of Aesop's fables where a dog sits in a manger (hay bin) and stops the ox from eating the hay. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He doesn't even use the extra office, but won't let anyone else sit there; he’s a real dog in the manger."
"Don't cry over spilled milk","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't waste time worrying about things that have already happened and cannot be changed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Once milk is spilled, it cannot be recovered. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You missed the train; there's no use crying over spilled milk."
"Down in the dumps","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling unhappy or depressed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Dumps' comes from an old word for a dazed or melancholy state. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She’s been a bit down in the dumps since her car broke down."
"Drive a hard bargain","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very demanding in negotiations. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Forcing a deal forward with great pressure. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She drives a hard bargain, but we eventually reached an agreement."
"Drop a bombshell","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a sudden, shocking announcement. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; the sudden impact of a bomb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The CEO dropped a bombshell when he announced he was quitting."
"Eat humble pie","<b>Meaning:</b> To admit you were wrong and apologize. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Umble' pie was made from the 'umbles' (innards) of a deer, served to low-status people. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He had to eat humble pie after his 'guaranteed' plan failed."
"Eleventh hour","<b>Meaning:</b> At the last possible moment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a Bible parable about workers hired at the end of the day. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The settlement was reached at the eleventh hour."
"Every dog has its day","<b>Meaning:</b> Everyone will have a period of success or luck at some point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Proverb dating back to the 16th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I haven't won a game yet, but every dog has its day."
"Eye of the hurricane","<b>Meaning:</b> A period of calm in the middle of a chaotic situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The center of a tropical storm is eerily calm. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The morning was peaceful, but it was just the eye of the hurricane before the meeting started."
"Face value","<b>Meaning:</b> Accepting something as it looks without looking for hidden meanings. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The value printed on the face of a coin. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You shouldn't take his promises at face value."
"Feather in your cap","<b>Meaning:</b> An accomplishment to be proud of. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hunters or warriors adding a feather to their hat for a kill. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Getting your article published is a real feather in your cap."
"Fit as a fiddle","<b>Meaning:</b> To be in excellent health. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Fit' used to mean 'suitable' or 'well-made,' like a fine instrument. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My grandfather is 90 and still fit as a fiddle."
"Flash in the pan","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that starts out great but fails to last. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Flintlock guns where the primer powder flashed but didn't fire the main charge. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That new app was just a flash in the pan."
"Fly on the wall","<b>Meaning:</b> An unnoticed observer of a private conversation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The idea of being small and insignificant while watching others. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’d love to be a fly on the wall when they discuss my promotion."
"Fool's paradise","<b>Meaning:</b> A state of happiness based on false hope or ignorance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 15th-century literature. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They think the company is doing well, but they're living in a fool's paradise."
"From the horse's mouth","<b>Meaning:</b> From the highest or most reliable authority. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Horse racing; getting a tip directly from the horse's owner or trainer. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know the store is closing; I heard it straight from the horse's mouth."
"Get a raw deal","<b>Meaning:</b> To be treated unfairly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'deal' (transaction) that is unfinished or poorly handled. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He really got a raw deal when they fired him right before his bonus."
"Get wind of","<b>Meaning:</b> To hear a rumor or secret information. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hunting; smelling the prey on the wind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The press got wind of the scandal before the police did."
"Gild the lily","<b>Meaning:</b> To try to improve something that is already beautiful or perfect, often ruining it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Shakespeare (King John); though the original quote was 'paint the lily.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The house is perfect; adding that fountain would just be gilding the lily."
"Go against the grain","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something that is the opposite of what is natural or expected. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Woodworking; cutting against the direction of the wood fibers is difficult. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It goes against the grain for me to lie to my parents."
"Go for the jugular","<b>Meaning:</b> To attack an opponent's weakest or most sensitive point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Predators attacking the throat (jugular vein) of their prey. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> In the debate, he went for the jugular as soon as his opponent made a mistake."
"Green with envy","<b>Meaning:</b> Very jealous. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Greeks believed jealousy caused an overproduction of bile, turning the skin green. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was green with envy when she saw his new car."
"Hair of the dog","<b>Meaning:</b> A small amount of alcohol taken to ease a hangover. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Belief that the hair of a rabid dog would cure its bite. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a headache; I think I need a bit of the hair of the dog."
"Hands down","<b>Meaning:</b> Easily or without question. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A jockey so far ahead they can drop their hands and still win the race. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She is hands down the best player on the team."
"Hard to swallow","<b>Meaning:</b> Difficult to believe or accept. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Metaphor for a large or unpleasant piece of food. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His excuse was a bit hard to swallow."
"Have a card up your sleeve","<b>Meaning:</b> To have a secret plan or advantage. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Cheating at cards. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't worry; I still have a card up my sleeve if this fails."
"Head over heels","<b>Meaning:</b> Completely and deeply (usually in love). <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally 'heels over head,' describing a physical tumble. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They are head over heels in love with each other."
"Hear it on the grapevine","<b>Meaning:</b> To hear news through gossip or informal communication. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Civil War telegraph lines that looked like tangled vines. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I heard on the grapevine that you're getting married."
"Hit the ceiling","<b>Meaning:</b> To become very angry suddenly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An exaggeration of jumping up in rage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My dad will hit the ceiling when he sees the car."
"Hold your horses","<b>Meaning:</b> To slow down or wait. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Pulling the reins of a horse-drawn carriage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Hold your horses! We aren't ready to leave yet."
"In the doldrums","<b>Meaning:</b> Lacking energy, stagnant, or depressed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An area of the ocean near the equator where there is often no wind to move ships. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The economy has been in the doldrums for months."
"In the limelight","<b>Meaning:</b> Being the center of public attention. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Early theater lighting used a 'limelight' (burning lime) to spotlight actors. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She loves being in the limelight."
"It's a small world","<b>Meaning:</b> A comment on a surprising coincidence. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A common phrase regarding interconnectedness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You know my brother? It’s a small world!"
"Keep your chin up","<b>Meaning:</b> Stay positive in a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Looking up shows confidence; looking down shows sadness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you didn't get the job, but keep your chin up."
"Kick the bucket","<b>Meaning:</b> To die. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Slang; either from a person standing on a bucket to hang themselves or a 'bucket' being a beam used to hang animals. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He kicked the bucket last year at the age of 95."
"Kill two birds with one stone","<b>Meaning:</b> To accomplish two things with one action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Efficiency in hunting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ll go to the post office while I’m at the store and kill two birds with one stone."
"Knock on wood","<b>Meaning:</b> An expression used to avoid 'tempting fate' after making a positive statement. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient belief that spirits lived in trees. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I haven't been sick all year, knock on wood."
"Last straw","<b>Meaning:</b> The final problem in a series of problems that makes a situation intolerable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'The straw that broke the camel's back.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When he forgot our anniversary, it was the last straw."
"Lead someone down the garden path","<b>Meaning:</b> To deceive or mislead someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Leading someone into a maze or a private area where they lose their way. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He promised us a huge profit, but he was just leading us down the garden path."
"Leave no stone unturned","<b>Meaning:</b> To try every possible way to achieve something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Greek myth about a general looking for treasure under stones. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The police will leave no stone unturned until they find the thief."
"Let the cat out of the bag","<b>Meaning:</b> To accidentally reveal a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Dishonest merchants selling a cat in a bag and claiming it was a pig. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Who let the cat out of the bag about the surprise party?"
"Level playing field","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where everyone has the same opportunities. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sports; a field that isn't sloped gives no advantage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need a level playing field to compete with larger companies."
"Like a fish out of water","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling uncomfortable in an unfamiliar situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fish cannot breathe or move well on land. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> At the high-tech conference, the old farmer felt like a fish out of water."
"Long in the tooth","<b>Meaning:</b> To be old. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Horses' gums recede as they age, making their teeth look longer. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That car is getting a bit long in the tooth."
"Loose cannon","<b>Meaning:</b> An unpredictable person who may cause damage to their own group. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A cannon on a ship that has come untied and rolls dangerously during a storm. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We can't trust him with the secret; he's a loose cannon."
"Make no bones about it","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very direct and certain about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Finding no bones in soup meant it was easy to swallow. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He made no bones about his dislike for the new policy."
"Miss the boat","<b>Meaning:</b> To be too late to take advantage of an opportunity. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Literal; if you aren't at the dock, the ship leaves without you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you don't invest now, you'll miss the boat."
"Neck and neck","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very close in a race or competition. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Horse racing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The two candidates are neck and neck in the polls."
"Nip it in the bud","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop a problem while it is still small. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Gardening; cutting off a flower bud before it can bloom. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to nip this rumor in the bud before it spreads."
"No pain, no gain","<b>Meaning:</b> You must work hard and suffer a little to achieve something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Exercise and weightlifting culture. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The training is exhausting, but no pain, no gain."
"Off the cuff","<b>Meaning:</b> Spoken without preparation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Actors or speakers writing notes on the 'cuffs' of their shirt sleeves. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He gave a great speech off the cuff."
"On the fence","<b>Meaning:</b> Undecided between two options. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sitting on a barrier between two fields. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm still on the fence about which car to buy."
"Once in a blue moon","<b>Meaning:</b> Very rarely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The second full moon in a month, which is a rare event. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I only see my cousins once in a blue moon."
"Out of the blue","<b>Meaning:</b> Suddenly and unexpectedly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A bolt of lightning from a clear blue sky. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She called me out of the blue after ten years."
"Over the moon","<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely happy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nursery rhyme 'The Cow Jumped Over the Moon.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was over the moon about her new job."
"Piece of cake","<b>Meaning:</b> Something very easy to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 19th-century 'cakewalk' competitions where the prize was easy to win. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That test was a piece of cake."
"Play it by ear","<b>Meaning:</b> To decide how to deal with a situation as it develops. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Music; playing a tune without looking at the notes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We don't have a plan for dinner; let's just play it by ear."
"Pull someone's leg","<b>Meaning:</b> To tease or joke with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from thieves tripping people up, but now purely humorous. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't really win the lottery; I'm just pulling your leg."
"Rain on your parade","<b>Meaning:</b> To spoil someone's plans or happiness. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of bad weather ruining an event. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I hate to rain on your parade, but the concert is canceled."
"Read between the lines","<b>Meaning:</b> To find a hidden meaning in something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Cryptography; messages written in invisible ink between lines of text. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He says he's happy, but if you read between the lines, he's actually very stressed."
"Right off the bat","<b>Meaning:</b> Immediately. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Baseball; the moment the ball hits the bat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I knew right off the bat that we would be friends."
"Rule of thumb","<b>Meaning:</b> A general principle or rough guide. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using the thumb as a basic unit of measurement. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> A good rule of thumb is to save 10% of your income."
"See eye to eye","<b>Meaning:</b> To agree completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Isaiah 52:8). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My boss and I don't always see eye to eye."
"Shot in the dark","<b>Meaning:</b> A complete guess without much evidence. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Firing a gun in the dark without being able to see the target. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't know the answer, so I just took a shot in the dark."
"Skeleton in the closet","<b>Meaning:</b> A shocking secret from the past. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a hidden body. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Every family has a skeleton in the closet."
"Spill the beans","<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Greek voting where different colored beans were used and then 'spilled' to count. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't spill the beans about the surprise party."
"Take it with a grain of salt","<b>Meaning:</b> To not believe something completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient belief that a grain of salt made poison less effective. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He exaggerates a lot, so take what he says with a grain of salt."
"The elephant in the room","<b>Meaning:</b> An obvious major problem that everyone is ignoring. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An elephant is too big to ignore, but people pretend it isn't there. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to talk about the elephant in the room: our debt."
"Through thick and thin","<b>Meaning:</b> Through all kinds of difficulties and good times. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Traveling through 'thick' woods and 'thin' plains. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They have stayed friends through thick and thin."
"Tie the knot","<b>Meaning:</b> To get married. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient hand-fasting ceremonies where couples' hands were literally tied. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They are planning to tie the knot in June."
"Under the weather","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling sick. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; sailors who were sick went below deck to get away from the 'weather' above. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't come to work; I'm feeling a bit under the weather."
"Up in the air","<b>Meaning:</b> Uncertain or undecided. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Something that hasn't 'landed' yet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our travel plans are still up in the air."
"When pigs fly","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that will never happen. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Pigs are heavy and cannot fly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'll clean my room when pigs fly!"
"Front","Back"
"Backhanded compliment","<b>Meaning:</b> A comment that seems like praise but is actually an insult. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using the back of the hand (weak/clumsy) rather than the palm (strong/honest). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Saying 'you look surprisingly good today' is a backhanded compliment."
"Barking up the wrong tree","<b>Meaning:</b> Accusing the wrong person or following a false lead. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A hunting dog barking at the base of a tree where it thinks a raccoon is, but the animal has already jumped to another tree. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you think I took your keys, you're barking up the wrong tree."
"Beat around the bush","<b>Meaning:</b> To avoid getting to the point of an issue. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hunting; workers would beat the surrounding bushes to flush out birds before the actual hunt began. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop beating around the bush and tell me how much the repairs will cost."
"Beggars can't be choosers","<b>Meaning:</b> People who depend on the generosity of others should not complain about what they receive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 16th-century proverb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The hotel was old, but it was free, and beggars can't be choosers."
"Behind someone's back","<b>Meaning:</b> Doing something without someone's knowledge, often in a dishonest way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Acting where the person cannot see you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't believe he sold the car behind my back."
"Bite the hand that feeds you","<b>Meaning:</b> To treat someone badly who has helped you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal warning about a dog biting its owner. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't insult your boss; you shouldn't bite the hand that feeds you."
"Blind leading the blind","<b>Meaning:</b> Uninformed people trying to guide others who are also uninformed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Matthew 15:14). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Neither of us knows how to fix this computer; it's the blind leading the blind."
"Blow your own trumpet","<b>Meaning:</b> To boast about your own achievements. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Historically, heralds blew trumpets to announce important people; doing it yourself implies no one else will. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't mean to blow my own trumpet, but I did win the award."
"Bone of contention","<b>Meaning:</b> A subject or issue that causes a long-standing argument. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Two dogs fighting over a single bone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The division of the inheritance was a major bone of contention between the siblings."
"Bring home the bacon","<b>Meaning:</b> To earn the money that a family needs to survive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an old English custom of awarding a side of bacon to couples who didn't argue. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He works two jobs to bring home the bacon."
"Bull in a china shop","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is clumsy or tactless in a delicate situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of a massive animal in a store of fragile ceramics. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He tried to apologize but he was like a bull in a china shop."
"Burn the midnight oil","<b>Meaning:</b> To work late into the night. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the oil used in lamps before electricity. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I had to burn the midnight oil to finish the essay."
"Bury the hatchet","<b>Meaning:</b> To make peace. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Native American tradition of literally burying weapons to end a war. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Let's bury the hatchet and be friends again."
"Call a spade a spade","<b>Meaning:</b> To speak plainly and directly about something, even if it is unpleasant. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Greek; choosing the most basic word for a tool. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Let's call a spade a spade: the project was a total failure."
"Call the shots","<b>Meaning:</b> To be the person in control. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from target shooting where the shooter 'calls' where the bullet will hit. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> In this office, the manager calls the shots."
"Can't judge a book by its cover","<b>Meaning:</b> Decisions shouldn't be made based on outward appearances. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 19th-century proverb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He looks scary, but he's actually very kind; you can't judge a book by its cover."
"Carry the weight of the world","<b>Meaning:</b> To feel like you have a huge amount of responsibility or stress. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Greek myth of Atlas, who held up the sky. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Ever since his father died, he feels like he's carrying the weight of the world."
"Cast pearls before swine","<b>Meaning:</b> To offer something valuable to someone who does not appreciate it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Matthew 7:6). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Giving him expensive wine is like casting pearls before swine; he can't tell the difference."
"Change of heart","<b>Meaning:</b> A change in one's opinion or feelings. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The heart as the seat of emotion. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I wasn't going to go, but I had a change of heart."
