"Chip off the old block","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is very similar to their mother or father. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A small piece of wood or stone that is the same as the larger piece it came from. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a doctor just like his dad—a real chip off the old block."
"Clean slate","<b>Meaning:</b> A fresh start with no past mistakes being held against you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Wiping a chalk slate clean in a schoolroom. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After serving his sentence, he wanted a clean slate."
"Close but no cigar","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very near success but fail. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fairgrounds used to give cigars as prizes for games. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You were only one second off the record—close but no cigar."
"Come clean","<b>Meaning:</b> To tell the truth. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Removing the 'dirt' of a lie. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The thief finally decided to come clean."
"Cool as a cucumber","<b>Meaning:</b> Very calm and relaxed, especially under pressure. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Cucumbers stay cool inside even in hot weather. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Even during the emergency, she stayed cool as a cucumber."
"Cross that bridge when you come to it","<b>Meaning:</b> Deal with a problem only when it actually happens. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Don't worry about obstacles that are still far away. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We might run out of money, but we'll cross that bridge when we come to it."
"Cry over spilled milk","<b>Meaning:</b> To waste time worrying about a mistake that cannot be fixed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Once milk is spilled, it is gone forever. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I lost my wallet, but there's no use crying over spilled milk."
"Curiosity killed the cat","<b>Meaning:</b> Being too inquisitive can lead to trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Warning that prying into secrets is dangerous. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop asking so many questions; curiosity killed the cat."
"Cut to the chase","<b>Meaning:</b> Get to the important part without wasting time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From early silent films where the 'chase' scene was the most exciting part. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm in a hurry, so please cut to the chase."
"Dead ringer","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who looks exactly like another person. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a 'ringer' was a horse substituted secretly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He's a dead ringer for my brother."
"Devil's advocate","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who takes the opposite side in an argument just to test the logic. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Catholic Church's process of canonization. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I agree with you, but let me play devil's advocate for a moment."
"Dog-eat-dog","<b>Meaning:</b> Ruthlessly competitive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A world where people will do anything to succeed, even hurt others. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It's a dog-eat-dog world in high finance."
"Don't count your chickens","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't make plans based on events that haven't happened yet. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Not all eggs hatch into chickens. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You haven't won the lottery yet, so don't count your chickens."
"Don't give up your day job","<b>Meaning:</b> A humorous way of telling someone they are not good at something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Implying they will never make money from their hobby. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Your singing is okay, but don't give up your day job."
"Don't put all your eggs in one basket","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't risk everything on a single venture. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> If you drop the basket, you lose everything. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Invest in different stocks; don't put all your eggs in one basket."
"Down to the wire","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is decided at the very last moment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; the finish line was marked by a wire. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The election is going down to the wire."
"Draw the line","<b>Meaning:</b> To set a limit on what you will allow. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Marking a physical boundary. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'll help him move, but I draw the line at driving 500 miles."
"Drastic times call for drastic measures","<b>Meaning:</b> When a situation is very bad, you must take extreme actions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the Greek physician Hippocrates. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company was failing, so they fired half the staff; drastic times call for drastic measures."
"Drink like a fish","<b>Meaning:</b> To drink a lot of alcohol. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal comparison to a fish's mouth moving. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He really started to drink like a fish after he lost his job."
"Drive someone up the wall","<b>Meaning:</b> To irritate someone immensely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone trying to climb a wall to escape annoyance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That loud music is driving me up the wall."
"Drop in the bucket","<b>Meaning:</b> A tiny amount compared to what is needed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The $5 donation is just a drop in the bucket."
"Dumb luck","<b>Meaning:</b> Success achieved purely by chance, not skill. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Dumb' meaning silent or senseless. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Winning the game was just dumb luck."
"Eat crow","<b>Meaning:</b> To admit a humiliating mistake. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Crows are unpleasant to eat, just as admitting failure is unpleasant. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He had to eat crow when his prediction turned out to be wrong."
"Every cloud has a silver lining","<b>Meaning:</b> There is something positive in every negative situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a poem by John Milton. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I lost my job, but it gave me time to travel; every cloud has a silver lining."
"Everything but the kitchen sink","<b>Meaning:</b> Almost everything imaginable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From WWII; implies taking everything portable. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She packed everything but the kitchen sink for the weekend."
"Eye for an eye","<b>Meaning:</b> A punishment that matches the crime. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient law of retribution (lex talionis). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He broke my phone, so I broke his—an eye for an eye."
"Face the music","<b>Meaning:</b> To accept the consequences of your actions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from being 'drummed out' of the military. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You were caught cheating; now you have to face the music."
"Fair and square","<b>Meaning:</b> Completely honest and according to the rules. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Square as a symbol of integrity. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We won the match fair and square."
"Fall on your sword","<b>Meaning:</b> To take the blame and resign from a position. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Roman generals committing suicide after defeat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The CEO fell on his sword after the scandal was revealed."
"Far cry from","<b>Meaning:</b> Very different from. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Distance being further than a 'cry' or shout can carry. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This cheap hotel is a far cry from the palace we stayed in last year."
"Feather in your cap","<b>Meaning:</b> An achievement to be proud of. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Custom of adding a feather to headgear for every enemy killed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Getting your pilot's license is a real feather in your cap."
"Feel like a million bucks","<b>Meaning:</b> To feel very healthy and happy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Modern metaphor for high value. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After that nap, I feel like a million bucks."
"Fine-tooth comb","<b>Meaning:</b> To examine something in great detail. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using a comb with very close teeth to find tiny insects. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The police went through the room with a fine-tooth comb."
"Fish for compliments","<b>Meaning:</b> To try to get people to say nice things about you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using words as 'bait.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She kept saying she looked old, just fishing for compliments."
"Fish out of water","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is in an uncomfortable or unfamiliar situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A fish cannot survive on land. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> At the formal party, I felt like a fish out of water."
"Fit as a fiddle","<b>Meaning:</b> In very good health. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Fit' used to mean 'suitable.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My grandmother is 80 but she's fit as a fiddle."
"Flash in the pan","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is briefly successful but doesn't last. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Flintlock muskets where gunpowder 'flashed' but didn't fire the bullet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That band was just a flash in the pan."
"Fly off the handle","<b>Meaning:</b> To lose one's temper suddenly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An axe head becoming loose and flying off the handle. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He tends to fly off the handle when he's tired."
"Follow in someone's footsteps","<b>Meaning:</b> To do the same job or live the same way as someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Literal image of following tracks. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He followed in his father's footsteps and became a lawyer."
"Food for thought","<b>Meaning:</b> Something worth thinking about. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Metaphor for the brain 'digesting' information. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The documentary gave us a lot of food for thought."
"Fool's gold","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that looks valuable but is worthless. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Iron pyrite, which looks like gold. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That investment was fool's gold."
"Get a second wind","<b>Meaning:</b> To get a new burst of energy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Running; when breathing becomes easier after initial exhaustion. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was tired, but then I got a second wind."
"Get out of hand","<b>Meaning:</b> To become uncontrollable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Losing control of a horse's reins. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The party got out of hand after midnight."
"Give someone the cold shoulder","<b>Meaning:</b> To intentionally ignore someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Serving a guest a cold piece of meat to show they aren't welcome. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She gave me the cold shoulder at the office."
"Go back to the drawing board","<b>Meaning:</b> To start over after a failure. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Design and engineering. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The plan didn't work, so it's back to the drawing board."
"Go cold turkey","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop a habit suddenly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the cold, goose-flesh skin during withdrawal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He quit smoking cold turkey."
"Go the extra mile","<b>Meaning:</b> To do more than what is expected. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Biblical reference to Roman law. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our waiter really went the extra mile."
"Green with envy","<b>Meaning:</b> Very jealous. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Greeks believed jealousy caused bile to turn the skin green. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was green with envy when she saw my new house."
"Hard nut to crack","<b>Meaning:</b> A difficult problem or person. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Literal difficulty of opening a nut. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This math problem is a hard nut to crack."
"Have a sweet tooth","<b>Meaning:</b> To love sugary foods. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 14th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a real sweet tooth."
"Head over heels","<b>Meaning:</b> Deeply in love. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally 'heels over head' (a tumble). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He's head over heels in love."
"Heart of gold","<b>Meaning:</b> Very kind and generous. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Gold as a symbol of purity. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She has a heart of gold."
"High on the hog","<b>Meaning:</b> Living in luxury. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The best meat is on the top of the pig. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They've been living high on the hog since they won."
"Hit the nail on the head","<b>Meaning:</b> To describe a situation exactly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Carpentry. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You hit the nail on the head with that comment."
"Hold your horses","<b>Meaning:</b> To wait or slow down. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Driving a carriage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Hold your horses, I'm not ready yet."
"In the same boat","<b>Meaning:</b> In the same difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> If a boat sinks, everyone is in danger. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We're all in the same boat here."
"It takes two to tango","<b>Meaning:</b> Both people in a situation are responsible. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The dance cannot be done alone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He says it's her fault, but it takes two to tango."
"Keep an eye on","<b>Meaning:</b> To watch carefully. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Constant observation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Keep an eye on the soup."
"Kill two birds with one stone","<b>Meaning:</b> Achieve two goals with one action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Efficiency in hunting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'll go to the bank while I'm at the store."
"Last resort","<b>Meaning:</b> The final option. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Legal term. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We will call the police only as a last resort."
"Let the cat out of the bag","<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Market fraud. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He let the cat out of the bag about the party."
"Long story short","<b>Meaning:</b> To get to the point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Summarizing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Long story short, we got lost."
"Look before you leap","<b>Meaning:</b> Think before you act. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Aesop's fables. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Check the costs before you buy; look before you leap."
"Make a killing","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a lot of money quickly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hunting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He made a killing on the stock market."
"Money doesn't grow on trees","<b>Meaning:</b> Money is limited. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Parental warning. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Turn off the lights; money doesn't grow on trees."
"No pain, no gain","<b>Meaning:</b> Effort is required for success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fitness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The training is hard, but no pain, no gain."
"Not my cup of tea","<b>Meaning:</b> Something I don't like. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> British culture. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Jazz is not my cup of tea."
"Once in a blue moon","<b>Meaning:</b> Very rarely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Astronomy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I only see him once in a blue moon."
"Out of the blue","<b>Meaning:</b> Suddenly and unexpectedly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Lightning from a clear sky. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He called me out of the blue."
"Piece of cake","<b>Meaning:</b> Very easy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Cake walks. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The test was a piece of cake."
"Play it by ear","<b>Meaning:</b> To decide as you go. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Music. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We'll just play it by ear."
"Pull yourself together","<b>Meaning:</b> To calm down. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Emotional control. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop crying and pull yourself together."
"Ring a bell","<b>Meaning:</b> To sound familiar. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Pavlovian conditioning. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That name rings a bell."
"Rule of thumb","<b>Meaning:</b> A general rule. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Measurement. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As a rule of thumb, save 10 percent."
"See eye to eye","<b>Meaning:</b> To agree. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Biblical. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We don't see eye to eye."
"Sit tight","<b>Meaning:</b> Wait patiently. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Staying still. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Sit tight while I get help."
"Sleep on it","<b>Meaning:</b> Wait before deciding. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Subconscious processing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't answer now; sleep on it."
"Small world","<b>Meaning:</b> Surprising coincidence. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Common phrase. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You know her too? Small world!"
"Spill the beans","<b>Meaning:</b> To tell a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Greek voting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Who spilled the beans?"
"The ball is in your court","<b>Meaning:</b> It is your turn to act. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Tennis. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I've offered a deal; the ball is in your court."
"Under the weather","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling sick. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm feeling a bit under the weather."
"Front","Back"
"Above board","<b>Meaning:</b> Honest and open; not secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From card games where players kept their hands above the table (board) to prove they weren't cheating. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The deal was completely above board."
"Ace up your sleeve","<b>Meaning:</b> A secret advantage or resource kept in reserve. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From dishonest card players hiding an Ace in their clothing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have an ace up my sleeve for the final negotiation."
"Across the board","<b>Meaning:</b> Applying to all members or categories of a group. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing, where a bet on a horse to win, place, or show is marked across the bookmaker's board. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company gave a 5% raise across the board."
"Against the clock","<b>Meaning:</b> Doing something in a very short time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for racing against a stopwatch. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We are working against the clock to finish the report."
"All systems go","<b>Meaning:</b> Everything is ready to start. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From NASA rocket launches when every department confirms readiness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new store opens tomorrow, and it’s all systems go."
"As the crow flies","<b>Meaning:</b> The shortest distance between two points in a straight line. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Crows are perceived to fly directly to their destination without following roads. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The town is only ten miles away as the crow flies."
"At a snail's pace","<b>Meaning:</b> Moving extremely slowly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal comparison to the slow movement of a snail. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Traffic was moving at a snail's pace."
"At death's door","<b>Meaning:</b> Very near death or extremely ill. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A person being so sick they are standing at the entrance to the afterlife. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He looked like he was at death's door when he had the flu."
"Axe to grind","<b>Meaning:</b> To have a strong personal opinion or a hidden motive for doing something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a story about a boy tricked into sharpening an axe; now implies someone has a selfish reason to be involved. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s only helping us because he has an axe to grind with the boss."
"Back on your feet","<b>Meaning:</b> Recovered from a physical illness or financial problem. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone who has fallen and is now standing again. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It took months, but he’s finally back on his feet after the accident."
"Back to the wall","<b>Meaning:</b> In a defensive or desperate position with no room to maneuver. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; if you have a wall behind you, you cannot retreat further and must fight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> With the bank threatening to close the shop, he has his back to the wall."
"Ballpark figure","<b>Meaning:</b> A rough numerical estimate. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From baseball; a ball that lands inside the ballpark is 'close enough' to the target. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't need the exact price, just a ballpark figure."
"Bank on it","<b>Meaning:</b> To be so sure of something that you would bet your money on it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Financial; the bank is seen as a reliable and safe institution. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It will rain tonight; you can bank on it."
"Barking up the wrong tree","<b>Meaning:</b> Following a false lead or blaming the wrong person. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hunting dogs barking at a tree where they think an animal is hiding, but the animal has escaped. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you think I took your lunch, you're barking up the wrong tree."
"Basket case","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is extremely nervous, stressed, or unable to function. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> WWI slang for soldiers who lost all limbs and had to be carried in baskets; now used for mental state. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was a total basket case before my presentation."
"Beat the clock","<b>Meaning:</b> To finish something before the time limit is reached. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from game shows or sporting events. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We managed to beat the clock and submit the application at 11:59."
"Beg, borrow, or steal","<b>Meaning:</b> To do whatever is necessary to get something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A list of all ways—honest and dishonest—to acquire an item. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I need that ticket; I’ll beg, borrow, or steal to get it."
"Bells and whistles","<b>Meaning:</b> Extra features added to something to make it more attractive but not essential. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From steam locomotives or fairground organs that had many decorative sounds. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I just want a simple phone; I don't need all the bells and whistles."
"Better late than never","<b>Meaning:</b> It is better to arrive or do something late than not to do it at all. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A proverb dating back to the 14th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’re three hours late, but better late than never."
"Bite your head off","<b>Meaning:</b> To speak to someone in a very angry and sudden way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An exaggeration of a person's aggressive verbal reaction. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I only asked a question; you don't have to bite my head off."
"Bitter pill to swallow","<b>Meaning:</b> An unpleasant fact that is difficult to accept. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Medicine that tastes bad but must be taken for health. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Losing the championship was a bitter pill to swallow."
"Blow off steam","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something to get rid of stress or anger. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Steam engines release excess pressure through a valve to avoid exploding. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I go for a run after work to blow off steam."
"Blow the whistle","<b>Meaning:</b> To report an illegal or dishonest activity to the authorities. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From referees or police using a whistle to stop play or a crime. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He decided to blow the whistle on the company's tax fraud."
"Blue blood","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who comes from a noble or aristocratic family. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Spanish 'sangre azul'; aristocrats were so pale their veins looked blue. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She acts like blue blood, even though she grew up in a normal house."
"Bolt from the blue","<b>Meaning:</b> A sudden and unexpected event. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A bolt of lightning appearing in a clear blue sky. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His resignation was a bolt from the blue."
"Bottom line","<b>Meaning:</b> The most important point or the final profit/loss of a business. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The final line on a financial statement showing the total. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The bottom line is that we are losing money."
"Break the bank","<b>Meaning:</b> To cost more money than you have or more than is reasonable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; winning more money than the 'bank' or the house has on hand. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This dinner is expensive, but it won't break the bank."
"Bring to light","<b>Meaning:</b> To make something known or visible. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for taking something out of the dark. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The investigation brought new evidence to light."
"Bull in a china shop","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is very clumsy or careless in a delicate situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of a massive animal walking through a store full of fragile dishes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't let him handle the negotiations; he's like a bull in a china shop."
"Burn a hole in your pocket","<b>Meaning:</b> Money that you are very eager to spend. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The idea that money is so 'hot' it burns through the pocket until it is gone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As soon as he gets paid, the money burns a hole in his pocket."
"Bury the hatchet","<b>Meaning:</b> To make peace and stop an argument. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Native American tradition of literally burying a tomahawk to symbolize the end of a conflict. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After years of fighting, the brothers finally buried the hatchet."
"Butter someone up","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very nice to someone so that they will do you a favor. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An ancient Indian custom of throwing balls of butter at statues of gods to seek favor. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s trying to butter up the boss to get a promotion."
"By and large","<b>Meaning:</b> Generally speaking; on the whole. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; a ship sailing both 'by' (into) and 'large' (away from) the wind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> By and large, the project was a success."
"Call the shots","<b>Meaning:</b> To be the person who makes the decisions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from target shooting or billiards, where the player announces which shot they will make. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My wife calls the shots in our house."
"Carry the torch","<b>Meaning:</b> To continue a cause or a tradition. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From relay races where a literal torch is passed to keep the flame going. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He is carrying the torch for his father's charity."
"Change your tune","<b>Meaning:</b> To change your opinion or the way you talk about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A musician changing the melody they are playing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He used to hate that car, but he changed his tune when he drove it."
"Chew someone out","<b>Meaning:</b> To scold someone severely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military slang; a metaphor for 'eating' someone up with words. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The coach chewed the team out for losing."
"Clean as a whistle","<b>Meaning:</b> Completely clean or innocent. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A whistle must be clear of debris to make a sharp, pure sound. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I checked the car; it’s clean as a whistle."
"Clear the air","<b>Meaning:</b> To remove bad feelings or misunderstandings between people. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Weather; a storm clears the humid, heavy air. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We had a meeting to clear the air after our argument."
"Clutch at straws","<b>Meaning:</b> To try any possible thing to save oneself from a bad situation, even if it is unlikely to work. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A drowning person trying to grab onto floating pieces of straw. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He has no evidence; he’s just clutching at straws."
"Cold comfort","<b>Meaning:</b> Very little or no comfort at all. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comfort that is 'cold' feels unfriendly and unhelpful. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Telling me everyone failed the test was cold comfort."
"Come hell or high water","<b>Meaning:</b> No matter what happens. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to two major dangers: fire (hell) and floods (high water). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ll be there for your wedding, come hell or high water."
"Cooking the books","<b>Meaning:</b> Manipulating financial records illegally to hide a loss or steal money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Cooked' means altered or prepared; 'books' refers to accounting ledgers. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The accountant was arrested for cooking the books."
"Crack a book","<b>Meaning:</b> To open a book to study. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The sound of a new book's spine cracking when opened for the first time. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He failed because he didn't crack a book all semester."
"Cream of the crop","<b>Meaning:</b> The very best of a group. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The cream rises to the top of the milk; the best part of the harvest. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> These students are the cream of the crop."
"Cry over spilled milk","<b>Meaning:</b> To worry about a mistake that cannot be undone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I lost my keys, but there's no use crying over spilled milk."
"Cut a long story short","<b>Meaning:</b> To get to the point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal summary request. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> To cut a long story short, we missed the train."
"Cut to the bone","<b>Meaning:</b> To reduce something (like a budget) to the lowest possible level. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for cutting through meat until you reach the bone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our funding has been cut to the bone."
"Dance to someone's tune","<b>Meaning:</b> To do exactly what someone else wants you to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a puppet or dancer following a musician's lead. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m not going to dance to his tune anymore."
"Dark horse","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who surprises everyone by winning or succeeding when they were not expected to. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a horse that is unknown to the gamblers. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a dark horse in the election."
"Days are numbered","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone or something will not last much longer. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Daniel 5:26); God has 'numbered' the days of a kingdom. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This old computer’s days are numbered."
"Dead heat","<b>Meaning:</b> A race or contest that ends in a perfect tie. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; 'heat' is a round of a race. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The election ended in a dead heat."
"Diamonds in the rough","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who has great potential but lacks social grace or training. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Natural diamonds look like ordinary rocks until they are cut and polished. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a bit rude, but he’s a diamond in the rough."
"Dime a dozen","<b>Meaning:</b> Very common and easy to find; of little value. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> When an item is so cheap you can get twelve for ten cents (a dime). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Apps like this are a dime a dozen."
"Dirt cheap","<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely inexpensive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Dirt is free and everywhere; therefore, it has the lowest possible value. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I got these shoes dirt cheap at the flea market."
"Do a 180","<b>Meaning:</b> To completely change your opinion or direction. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 180-degree turn is a half-circle, facing the opposite way. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She used to love coffee, but she did a 180 and now only drinks tea."
"Dog-eat-dog","<b>Meaning:</b> Very competitive and ruthless. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A proverb suggesting that in extreme situations, even similar animals will turn on each other to survive. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s a dog-eat-dog world in the fashion industry."
"Down and out","<b>Meaning:</b> Having no money, no job, and no hope. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; a fighter who is knocked 'down' and is 'out' of the match. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was down and out after his business failed."
"Down the drain","<b>Meaning:</b> Wasted or lost completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for water or money disappearing into a sewer. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> All our hard work went down the drain."
"Draw a blank","<b>Meaning:</b> To be unable to remember something or get a result. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From lotteries where some tickets were blank and won nothing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I asked for his name, but I drew a blank."
"Drop the ball","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a mistake or fail in your responsibility. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sports like football or baseball; dropping the ball usually results in a loss for the team. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I really dropped the ball by forgetting to call the client."
"Drown your sorrows","<b>Meaning:</b> To drink a lot of alcohol to forget your problems. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'submerging' sadness in liquid. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He went to the bar to drown his sorrows after the breakup."
"Easy as pie","<b>Meaning:</b> Very simple to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Not from making pie, but from how easy it is to eat it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Fixing the bike was easy as pie."
"Eat your words","<b>Meaning:</b> To admit that what you said was wrong. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for being forced to 'take back' your statements. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I said he would fail, but he proved me wrong; I had to eat my words."
"Eleventh hour","<b>Meaning:</b> At the very last possible moment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a Bible parable about laborers hired at the end of the day. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The deal was signed at the eleventh hour."
"Every man for himself","<b>Meaning:</b> Everyone must look after their own interests because no one else will help. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Often associated with emergencies like a sinking ship. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When the fire alarm went off, it was every man for himself."
"Eye-opener","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation that makes you realize something surprising or shocking. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for literally opening the eyes to a truth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Visiting the slums was a real eye-opener for the students."
"Fast track","<b>Meaning:</b> The quickest way to achieve a goal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a dry, firm track that allows for the fastest speeds. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s on the fast track to becoming a partner."
"Feather your nest","<b>Meaning:</b> To make yourself rich, often dishonestly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was caught feathering his nest with government money."
"Feel the pinch","<b>Meaning:</b> To experience financial hardship because of a lack of money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the 'tightening' of a budget. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone is feeling the pinch with the high gas prices."
"Fight fire with fire","<b>Meaning:</b> To use the same methods as your opponent to defeat them. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From firefighting; a small fire is sometimes used to burn fuel before a larger fire reaches it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They are using aggressive ads, so we have to fight fire with fire."
"Fine-tune","<b>Meaning:</b> To make small adjustments to something to make it work perfectly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From tuning musical instruments or radio frequencies. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to fine-tune the engine before the race."
"Fishy","<b>Meaning:</b> Suspicious or dishonest. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fresh fish has no smell, but old or 'bad' fish smells strongly; therefore, a 'smell' indicates something is wrong. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Something about his story sounds fishy."
"Fit for a king","<b>Meaning:</b> Of very high quality; excellent. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Worthy of being served to royalty. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This meal is fit for a king."
"Follow suit","<b>Meaning:</b> To do the same thing as someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From card games where players must play a card of the same 'suit' as the lead player. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When the first company lowered prices, others followed suit."
"Fool's errand","<b>Meaning:</b> A task that is useless or impossible to complete. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A prank where a person is sent to find something that doesn't exist. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Looking for a gas station in this desert is a fool's errand."
"For a song","<b>Meaning:</b> Very cheaply. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a time when street performers would sing for a single coin. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I bought this antique table for a song."
"Full steam ahead","<b>Meaning:</b> To proceed with maximum energy and speed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From steamships or trains using the highest pressure to move as fast as possible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Once we get the permit, it's full steam ahead."
"Get a grip","<b>Meaning:</b> To control your emotions and act sensibly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for physically 'holding onto' one's self. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You need to get a grip and stop panicking."
"Get wind of","<b>Meaning:</b> To hear a rumor or secret information. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hunting; animals can 'smell' a predator if the wind carries the scent. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If the boss gets wind of this, we’ll be in trouble."
"Give and take","<b>Meaning:</b> Compromise; a situation where both people make concessions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal exchange of 'giving' something and 'taking' something else. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Every marriage requires some give and take."
"Go for broke","<b>Meaning:</b> To risk everything to achieve a big goal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Gambling slang; betting all your money (until you are 'broke' if you lose). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We decided to go for broke and invest our life savings."
"Go the distance","<b>Meaning:</b> To finish something even though it is very difficult. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; a fighter who lasts for all the scheduled rounds. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't know if I can go the distance with this marathon."
"Grease someone's palm","<b>Meaning:</b> To give someone a bribe. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for making a transaction 'smooth' with money. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’ll have to grease his palm if you want the permit today."
"Hands-down","<b>Meaning:</b> Easily and without question. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a jockey who is so far ahead they can drop their hands (reins) and still win. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She is hands-down the best singer in the competition."
"Hard up","<b>Meaning:</b> Short of money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; turning the tiller 'hard up' against the wind when in trouble. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a bit hard up since he lost his job."
"Harp on","<b>Meaning:</b> To talk about the same subject repeatedly in an annoying way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From playing the same note or tune on a harp. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop harping on about my mistake!"
"Have a bone to pick","<b>Meaning:</b> To have a complaint to discuss with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A dog 'picking' at a bone; a metaphor for struggling with a problem. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a bone to pick with you regarding the bill."
"Heads up","<b>Meaning:</b> A warning that something is about to happen. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal instruction to look up and see a danger. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Just a heads up, the meeting has been moved."
"Hit pay dirt","<b>Meaning:</b> To find something very valuable or successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gold mining; finding the 'dirt' that contains gold. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After months of research, we finally hit pay dirt."
"In the black","<b>Meaning:</b> Making a profit; not in debt. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Accountants used black ink for profits and red ink for losses. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company is finally in the black."
"In the red","<b>Meaning:</b> Losing money; in debt. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Accountants used red ink for losses. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We’ve been in the red for three months."
"It's a small world","<b>Meaning:</b> Used when you encounter a surprising coincidence. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A common phrase. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You know my cousin? It's a small world!"
"Keep a weather eye out","<b>Meaning:</b> To watch carefully for signs of trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; sailors watching the horizon for changes in the weather. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Keep a weather eye out for any changes in the market."
"Kick the bucket","<b>Meaning:</b> To die. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'bucket' (an old word for a beam used to hang animals); if the animal struggled, it 'kicked the bucket.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He kicked the bucket last year."
"Knock on wood","<b>Meaning:</b> Said to avoid bad luck after saying something positive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient belief that spirits lived in trees; knocking on wood would wake or thank them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I haven't been sick all year, knock on wood."
"Learn the ropes","<b>Meaning:</b> To learn how to do a job. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It took me a week to learn the ropes."
"Level playing field","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation that is fair for everyone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sports; a field that is not sloped gives no advantage to either team. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Small businesses need a level playing field to compete with large ones."
"Live on borrowed time","<b>Meaning:</b> To survive longer than expected. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The idea that you have used up your 'allotted' time and are now using time you don't 'own.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This old car is living on borrowed time."
"Make ends meet","<b>Meaning:</b> To have just enough money to pay for your basic needs. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'ends' of a budget or pieces of fabric meeting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s hard to make ends meet on a minimum wage."
"Front","Back"
"Back to basics","<b>Meaning:</b> Returning to the fundamental principles or simplest way of doing something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Often used in education or business to refocus on core values. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our marketing is too complex; we need to get back to basics."
"Bark is worse than their bite","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who sounds threatening or angry but is actually harmless. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing a loud, barking dog that doesn't actually attack. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't worry about the manager's shouting; his bark is worse than his bite."
"Beat a dead horse","<b>Meaning:</b> To waste time doing something that has already been finished or decided. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of trying to make a dead horse run by whipping it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We've already decided to cancel the project; stop trying to beat a dead horse."
"Below the belt","<b>Meaning:</b> An unfair or cruel remark. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; hitting an opponent below the waist is illegal and unfair. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Mentioning his past mistakes was really hitting below the belt."
"Bend over backwards","<b>Meaning:</b> To work extremely hard to help or please someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gymnastics; a difficult physical feat showing extreme effort. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I bent over backwards to get her the best seat in the house."
"Beside oneself","<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely upset or excited. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient belief that a person's soul could literally leave their body during intense emotion. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was beside herself with grief after her dog died."
"Big fish in a small pond","<b>Meaning:</b> An important or powerful person in a small and unimportant group. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal comparison of fish sizes relative to their environment. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was a big fish in a small pond at his old job, but now he's just one of many."
"Bite your tongue","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop yourself from saying something you want to say. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Physically biting the tongue to prevent speech. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I wanted to tell her she was wrong, but I had to bite my tongue."
"Break a leg","<b>Meaning:</b> A way of wishing someone good luck, especially before a performance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Superstition in theater that wishing for good luck would actually cause bad luck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You're going to be great on stage! Break a leg!"
"Burn your bridges","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something that makes it impossible to go back to a previous situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; destroying a bridge after crossing it so the enemy can't follow, but you also can't retreat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't be rude when you quit; you don't want to burn your bridges."
"Burst your bubble","<b>Meaning:</b> To ruin someone's happiness or illusions by telling them the truth. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a fragile, beautiful soap bubble being popped. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I hate to burst your bubble, but the concert is already sold out."
"Bury your head in the sand","<b>Meaning:</b> To ignore a problem and hope it will go away. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The false belief that ostriches hide from danger by burying their heads. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You can't just bury your head in the sand about your debt."
"By the skin of your teeth","<b>Meaning:</b> To succeed by the narrowest possible margin. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Job 19:20). Teeth don't have skin, implying a microscopic margin. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I passed the exam by the skin of my teeth."
"Can't hold a candle to","<b>Meaning:</b> To be much less good or talented than someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An apprentice used to hold a candle so a master could see their work; if you weren't even good enough for that, you were unskilled. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This new movie can't hold a candle to the original."
"Card up your sleeve","<b>Meaning:</b> A secret advantage or plan. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Cheating at cards by hiding a helpful card in a sleeve. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If they reject our first offer, I have a card up my sleeve."
"Chip on your shoulder","<b>Meaning:</b> To be easily angered because you feel you have been treated unfairly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 19th-century practice of placing a piece of wood on one's shoulder and daring someone to knock it off to start a fight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s had a chip on his shoulder ever since he was passed over for the promotion."
"Clam up","<b>Meaning:</b> To suddenly stop talking, usually because of shyness or fear. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A clam closes its shell tightly when it feels threatened. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Every time the police asked him a question, he would clam up."
"Clean slate","<b>Meaning:</b> A fresh start where past mistakes are forgotten. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the use of chalkboards (slates) in schools; a damp cloth wipes everything away. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Moving to a new city gave him a clean slate."
"Close but no cigar","<b>Meaning:</b> Almost successful but not quite. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fairground games used to give out cigars as prizes for winning. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You guessed 99 and the answer was 100. Close but no cigar."
"Come clean","<b>Meaning:</b> To tell the truth about something you have kept secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the idea of washing away the 'dirt' of a lie. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I think it's time you came clean about where you were last night."
"Cost an arm and a leg","<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely expensive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the high price of having a portrait painted with limbs included (more work for the artist). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That new car cost me an arm and a leg."
"Crocodile tears","<b>Meaning:</b> False or insincere tears. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient belief that crocodiles cried while eating their prey to lure more victims. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't believe her; those are just crocodile tears."
"Cry wolf","<b>Meaning:</b> To ask for help when you don't need it, so that people won't help you when you actually do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables; the boy who cried wolf. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you keep crying wolf, no one will believe you when there’s a real emergency."
"Curiosity killed the cat","<b>Meaning:</b> A warning that being too curious can lead to trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> First appeared in the late 1500s as 'care killed the cat' (meaning worry), later changed to curiosity. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I wouldn't open that box if I were you—curiosity killed the cat."
"Cut someone some slack","<b>Meaning:</b> To be less strict or critical of someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sailing; loosening a rope (slack) to give it more play. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s new at the job, so cut him some slack."
"Dead as a doornail","<b>Meaning:</b> Completely dead or finished. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nails in doors were hammered through and 'clenched' (bent over), making them unusable for anything else. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My phone is dead as a doornail; the battery won't charge."
"Down to earth","<b>Meaning:</b> Practical, sensible, and realistic. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Staying 'grounded' on the earth rather than having one's head in the clouds. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Even though she's famous, she's very down to earth."
"Drink like a fish","<b>Meaning:</b> To drink a lot of alcohol. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal observation of fish constantly opening their mouths underwater. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He really started to drink like a fish after his divorce."
"Drive someone up the wall","<b>Meaning:</b> To make someone very annoyed or crazy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone trying to climb a wall to escape a situation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That constant tapping sound is driving me up the wall."
"Drop in the bucket","<b>Meaning:</b> A very small amount that has little effect on a large situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Isaiah 40:15). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The $10 donation is just a drop in the bucket compared to what we need."
"Early bird gets the worm","<b>Meaning:</b> The person who arrives or acts first will have the best chance of success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal observation of birds hunting for worms at dawn. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I got to the sale at 6 AM; the early bird gets the worm."
"Eat your heart out","<b>Meaning:</b> To feel great jealousy or regret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the idea of grief or jealousy 'consuming' a person's heart. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I just won a free trip to Hawaii—eat your heart out!"
"Every cloud has a silver lining","<b>Meaning:</b> There is something good in every bad situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 1634 poem by John Milton. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I lost my job, but it gave me time to write my book; every cloud has a silver lining."
"Eye for an eye","<b>Meaning:</b> The idea that a person who has done something wrong should be punished by having the same thing done to them. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the ancient Code of Hammurabi and the Bible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He broke my window, so I broke his—an eye for an eye."
"Face the music","<b>Meaning:</b> To accept the unpleasant consequences of your actions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from the military, where a dismissed soldier was 'drummed out' to music. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I crashed the car; now I have to go home and face the music."
"Feather in your cap","<b>Meaning:</b> An achievement you can be proud of. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the custom of hunters or warriors adding a feather to their headgear for every enemy killed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Winning that award is a real feather in your cap."
"Fine line","<b>Meaning:</b> A very small difference between two things. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a thin boundary that is easy to cross. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> There's a fine line between being confident and being arrogant."
"Fish out of water","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who feels uncomfortable in an unfamiliar situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A fish cannot breathe or function outside of its natural habitat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> At the formal party, he felt like a fish out of water."
"Flash in the pan","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is successful for a very short time and then fails. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old flintlock muskets; sometimes the gunpowder would 'flash' in the pan but not fire the bullet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That band had one hit song, but they were just a flash in the pan."
"Follow your nose","<b>Meaning:</b> To go straight ahead or trust your instincts. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal instruction to move in the direction you are facing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> To find the bakery, just follow your nose."
"Get off on the wrong foot","<b>Meaning:</b> To start a relationship or activity badly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient superstition that it was bad luck to step into a room with the left foot first. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m sorry I was late to the interview; I don't want to get off on the wrong foot."
"Get something off your chest","<b>Meaning:</b> To tell someone something that has been worrying you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for removing a heavy weight of guilt or anxiety from your body. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I need to get something off my chest: I was the one who broke the vase."
"Get under someone's skin","<b>Meaning:</b> To annoy or irritate someone intensely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a parasite or splinter that causes constant irritation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His constant whistling really gets under my skin."
"Get your act together","<b>Meaning:</b> To organize yourself and your life so that you can be successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From theater; a performer needs to have their 'act' ready before going on stage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You need to get your act together if you want to pass this class."
"Give someone a piece of your mind","<b>Meaning:</b> To speak angrily to someone about something they have done wrong. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The idea of 'sharing' your angry thoughts with someone else. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m going to give the manager a piece of my mind about this service."
"Go out on a limb","<b>Meaning:</b> To take a risk or do something that puts you in a vulnerable position. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Climbing a tree; the further you go on a limb (branch), the more likely it is to break. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m going to go out on a limb and say that this plan will work."
"Go the whole hog","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something as completely and thoroughly as possible. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the 18th century; buying the entire pig instead of just parts of it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We’re already painting the kitchen, so let's go the whole hog and do the living room too."
"Greased lightning","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that moves extremely fast. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Lightning is fast; adding 'grease' implies making it even smoother and faster. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He ran across the field like greased lightning."
"Green thumb","<b>Meaning:</b> A natural talent for growing plants. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the green stains on a gardener's thumb from handling plants. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My grandmother has a real green thumb; her garden is beautiful."
"Hair of the dog","<b>Meaning:</b> A small amount of alcohol taken to cure a hangover. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the old belief that a person bitten by a rabid dog could be cured by a potion containing the dog's hair. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a terrible headache; I think I need a bit of the hair of the dog."
"Hard to swallow","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is difficult to believe or accept. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a piece of food that is too large or unpleasant to get down the throat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The news that the company is closing was hard to swallow."
"Have your head in the clouds","<b>Meaning:</b> To be unaware of what is happening around you because you are daydreaming. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of someone standing so tall they are in the clouds and can't see the ground. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He always has his head in the clouds and never finishes his work."
"Hold your horses","<b>Meaning:</b> To wait or slow down. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the days of horse-drawn carriages; physically pulling the reins to stop the horses. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Hold your horses! We’re not ready to leave yet."
"In the hot seat","<b>Meaning:</b> In a difficult position where you have to answer tough questions or make hard decisions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally slang for the electric chair; later became a metaphor for being under pressure. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The CEO was in the hot seat during the press conference."
"It's not rocket science","<b>Meaning:</b> Used to say that something is not difficult to understand. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing a task to the high complexity of aerospace engineering. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Just follow the instructions; it’s not rocket science."
"Keep your chin up","<b>Meaning:</b> To stay positive in a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Keeping the head high shows confidence and prevents crying. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know things are hard right now, but keep your chin up."
"Kill two birds with one stone","<b>Meaning:</b> To achieve two things at once with a single action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 17th-century metaphor for efficiency. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ll go to the grocery store while I’m out for my walk and kill two birds with one stone."
"Knock someone's socks off","<b>Meaning:</b> To impress someone very much. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An exaggeration of a physical reaction to a surprise. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Her singing performance will knock your socks off."
"Let your hair down","<b>Meaning:</b> To relax and behave in an informal way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a time when women wore their hair up in formal styles and only let it down at home. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s a party! Come on and let your hair down."
"Like a moth to a flame","<b>Meaning:</b> Being strongly attracted to something that is dangerous. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal observation of insects flying into fire and dying. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s attracted to trouble like a moth to a flame."
"Long in the tooth","<b>Meaning:</b> To be old. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> As horses age, their gums recede, making their teeth look longer. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That car is getting a bit long in the tooth; it's time for a new one."
"Make no bones about it","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very direct and honest about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation regarding bones in soup. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He made no bones about the fact that he was unhappy."
"Neck and neck","<b>Meaning:</b> To be exactly equal in a race or competition. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; when two horses are running with their necks side by side. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The two candidates are neck and neck in the polls."
"Night owl","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who stays up late at night. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Owls are nocturnal animals that are active at night. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ve always been a night owl; I do my best work after midnight."
"No use crying over spilled milk","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't worry about things that have already happened and can't be changed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Once milk is spilled, it's gone; crying won't bring it back. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You missed the bus; there's no use crying over spilled milk."
"Off the top of my head","<b>Meaning:</b> Without thinking or checking the facts. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Information that is at the surface of the mind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Off the top of my head, I think the meeting is at 2 PM."
"On a silver platter","<b>Meaning:</b> To get something easily without having to work for it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for being served like royalty. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He didn't have to work for his money; his father gave it to him on a silver platter."
"Out on a limb","<b>Meaning:</b> In a dangerous or unsupported position. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm out on a limb here; no one else agrees with my plan."
"Over the moon","<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely happy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the nursery rhyme 'Hey Diddle Diddle' where the cow jumped over the moon. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She’s over the moon about her new baby."
"Pain in the neck","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone or something that is very annoying. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for a constant, nagging ache. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Doing taxes is a real pain in the neck."
"Piece of cake","<b>Meaning:</b> Something very easy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That math test was a piece of cake."
"Play it by ear","<b>Meaning:</b> To decide what to do as you go. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We don't have a plan for tonight; let's just play it by ear."
"Pull someone's leg","<b>Meaning:</b> To tease or joke with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m not really a millionaire; I’m just pulling your leg."
"Put your foot in your mouth","<b>Meaning:</b> To say something embarrassing or hurtful by mistake. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a physical social blunder. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I really put my foot in my mouth when I asked her about her husband—they’re divorced."
"Rain check","<b>Meaning:</b> A promise to do something at a later date because you can't do it now. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From baseball; if it rained, spectators were given a 'rain check' for a future game. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't go to dinner tonight; can I take a rain check?"
"Right under your nose","<b>Meaning:</b> In a place that is very easy to see. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of something being extremely close. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I spent all day looking for my keys and they were right under my nose on the table."
"Roll with the punches","<b>Meaning:</b> To deal with problems and difficulties as they happen. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; moving your body with an opponent's punch to reduce its impact. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Business is tough right now, but we just have to roll with the punches."
"Rome wasn't built in a day","<b>Meaning:</b> Important things take time to achieve. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 12th-century French proverb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't get discouraged by your slow progress; Rome wasn't built in a day."
"Rule of thumb","<b>Meaning:</b> A general rule based on experience. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As a rule of thumb, you should drink eight glasses of water a day."
"Run out of steam","<b>Meaning:</b> To lose energy or interest in something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From steam engines; if the steam runs out, the engine stops moving. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was working hard on the project, but I’ve run out of steam."
"Saved by the bell","<b>Meaning:</b> Rescued by a timely interruption. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't want to answer his question, but the phone rang—saved by the bell."
"Second nature","<b>Meaning:</b> Something you can do easily because you have done it so many times. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Habits become so strong they feel like they were part of your natural self from birth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Driving is second nature to me now."
"See eye to eye","<b>Meaning:</b> To agree completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We don't see eye to eye on politics."
"Shot in the dark","<b>Meaning:</b> A random guess. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't know the answer, so I just took a shot in the dark."
"Skeleton in the closet","<b>Meaning:</b> A secret shame. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Every politician has a skeleton in the closet."
"Slow and steady wins the race","<b>Meaning:</b> Being consistent is more important than being fast. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables; the tortoise and the hare. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m taking my time with this project; slow and steady wins the race."
"Smell a rat","<b>Meaning:</b> To suspect that something is wrong or that someone is lying. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a cat's ability to smell a hidden rat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He says he’s helping us, but I smell a rat."
"Spill the beans","<b>Meaning:</b> To tell a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Who spilled the beans about the surprise?"
"Take it with a grain of salt","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't believe everything you hear. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He says he's a genius, but take it with a grain of salt."
"The apple of my eye","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is very special and loved. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; the 'apple' was the pupil of the eye, which is the most sensitive and precious part. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My little granddaughter is the apple of my eye."
"The best of both worlds","<b>Meaning:</b> Having the advantages of two different situations. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have the best of both worlds: a great job and a happy home."
"The early bird gets the worm","<b>Meaning:</b> Acting early leads to success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I got the best seat because I arrived early; the early bird gets the worm."
"The elephant in the room","<b>Meaning:</b> An obvious problem no one talks about. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to address the elephant in the room: no one is working."
"The grass is always greener","<b>Meaning:</b> Thinking others have it better. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s always unhappy with his job; the grass is always greener on the other side."
"The last straw","<b>Meaning:</b> The final problem in a series. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When he forgot my birthday, it was the last straw."
"The whole nine yards","<b>Meaning:</b> Everything. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We bought the furniture, the curtains, the whole nine yards."
"Through thick and thin","<b>Meaning:</b> Through good and bad times. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We’ve been friends through thick and thin."
"Throw in the towel","<b>Meaning:</b> To give up. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't do this anymore; I'm throwing in the towel."
"Tie the knot","<b>Meaning:</b> To get married. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They finally tied the knot after ten years."
"Tip of the iceberg","<b>Meaning:</b> A small part of a larger problem. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> These complaints are just the tip of the iceberg."
"Front","Back"
"A lot on one's plate","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very busy with many tasks or responsibilities. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a dinner plate piled so high with food that it is difficult to manage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'd love to help with the fundraiser, but I have a lot on my plate right now."
"All hands on deck","<b>Meaning:</b> Everyone must help or work together to solve a problem. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; a command for all sailors to come to the deck to handle an emergency. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We have a huge deadline tomorrow, so it's all hands on deck."
"Back to the salt mines","<b>Meaning:</b> Returning to work, usually with a sense of reluctance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the historical use of prisoners to work in harsh conditions in salt mines. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Lunch is over; time to get back to the salt mines."
"Bite the bullet","<b>Meaning:</b> To endure a painful or difficult situation that cannot be avoided. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Before anesthesia, soldiers would bite on a lead bullet to endure the pain of surgery. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't want to tell him the bad news, but I'll just have to bite the bullet and do it."
"Blood, sweat, and tears","<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely hard work and effort. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized by Winston Churchill in a 1940 speech regarding the effort needed for war. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I put blood, sweat, and tears into building this business from nothing."
"Blue-collar worker","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who performs manual labor. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the durable blue fabrics (like denim) traditionally worn by industrial workers. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My father was a blue-collar worker in a steel mill for thirty years."
"Break the ice","<b>Meaning:</b> To do or say something to make people feel more relaxed in a social situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to 'icebreaker' ships that clear a path through frozen water for other vessels. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He told a joke at the start of the meeting to break the ice."
"Bring home the bacon","<b>Meaning:</b> To earn the money that a family needs to live. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from an old English tradition where a couple who didn't argue for a year won a side of bacon. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Now that she has a promotion, she’s the one bringing home the bacon."
"Burn the candle at both ends","<b>Meaning:</b> To work too hard without getting enough rest. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> If you light a candle at both ends, it burns out twice as fast. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’ll get sick if you keep burning the candle at both ends."
"Buy a pig in a poke","<b>Meaning:</b> To buy something without looking at it or knowing its true value. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'poke' was a bag. Dishonest sellers would put a cat in the bag and claim it was a pig. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't buy that car online without seeing it; you don't want to buy a pig in a poke."
"Cash cow","<b>Meaning:</b> A business or product that generates a steady and significant profit. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing a profitable business to a cow that can be milked indefinitely. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Their software division has become the company's cash cow."
"Caught red-handed","<b>Meaning:</b> Caught in the middle of doing something wrong. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An old law where a person could only be convicted of poaching if they were found with the animal's blood on their hands. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The thief was caught red-handed as he was climbing out the window."
"Chew the fat","<b>Meaning:</b> To chat or gossip in a relaxed way for a long time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from sailors chewing on tough salt pork (fat) while talking during breaks. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We sat on the porch and chewed the fat for hours."
"Cloud on the horizon","<b>Meaning:</b> A sign of potential trouble in the future. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Weather; a distant cloud indicates a storm may be coming. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new tax law is the only cloud on the horizon for our expansion plans."
"Cool your heels","<b>Meaning:</b> To be kept waiting, often as a punishment or show of power. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Horses' feet (heels) get hot when they run; they need to stand still to 'cool' them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was left to cool my heels in the waiting room for two hours."
"Cut through the red tape","<b>Meaning:</b> To bypass complicated official rules and processes to get something done. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Legal documents in 17th-century England were bound with red ribbon (tape). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I hired a lawyer to help us cut through the red tape of the immigration process."
"Dead ringer","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone or something that looks exactly like another. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a 'ringer' was a fast horse substituted for a slow one to cheat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a dead ringer for that famous actor."
"Don't count your chickens before they hatch","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't make plans based on something that hasn't happened yet. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A reminder that not every egg will successfully produce a chicken. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You haven't gotten the job offer yet, so don't count your chickens before they hatch."
"Draw the line","<b>Meaning:</b> To set a limit on what you will allow or do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sports or boundary marking, indicating where a player cannot cross. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm happy to help him, but I draw the line at lending him more money."
"Drive a hard bargain","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very demanding and uncompromising in negotiations. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'driving' a deal forward with great force. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She drives a hard bargain, but we finally agreed on a price."
"Duck out","<b>Meaning:</b> To leave a place or situation quickly or secretly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the way a duck dives underwater to escape. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm going to duck out of the meeting a few minutes early."
"Eager beaver","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is very enthusiastic and works extremely hard. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Beavers are known for their constant work building dams. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new intern is a real eager beaver, always asking for more work."
"Eat crow","<b>Meaning:</b> To admit you were wrong and feel humiliated. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Crows are considered unpleasant to eat; therefore, eating one is a distasteful task. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He had to eat crow when his 'guaranteed' investment failed."
"Elbow grease","<b>Meaning:</b> Physical effort or hard work. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The idea that the only 'oil' needed to make something shine is the movement of your elbow. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This old table just needs a little elbow grease to look new again."
"Elephant in the room","<b>Meaning:</b> A major problem that everyone is aware of but no one wants to talk about. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An elephant is too big to ignore, but people often pretend it isn't there. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to address the elephant in the room: our company is losing money."
"Everything but the kitchen sink","<b>Meaning:</b> Almost everything imaginable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From WWII; soldiers would throw everything they could find at the enemy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She packed everything but the kitchen sink for a two-day trip."
"Face value","<b>Meaning:</b> Accepting something exactly as it appears without looking for a deeper meaning. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From currency; the value printed on the 'face' of a coin or bill. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You shouldn't take his promises at face value."
"Falling on a sword","<b>Meaning:</b> To take the blame for a mistake or to resign to protect others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ancient Roman generals who would commit suicide by falling on their sword after a defeat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The CEO fell on his sword to save the company's reputation after the scandal."
"Feather your own nest","<b>Meaning:</b> To use your position to make yourself rich, often dishonestly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Birds use feathers to make their nests soft and comfortable for themselves. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was accused of feathering his own nest with company funds."
"Feet to the fire","<b>Meaning:</b> To pressure someone to fulfill a commitment or tell the truth. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A historical reference to torture used to force confessions. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The committee is holding the director's feet to the fire regarding the missing budget."
"Fifth wheel","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is unnecessary or unwelcome in a group. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A four-wheeled carriage has no use for a fifth wheel. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I felt like a fifth wheel when I went to dinner with the two couples."
"Fly by the seat of your pants","<b>Meaning:</b> To act instinctively without a clear plan or using only the information available. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Early pilots used the physical 'feel' of the plane's vibrations to navigate. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We didn't have a strategy for the meeting; we were just flying by the seat of our pants."
"Follow the money","<b>Meaning:</b> To investigate the financial trail to find the person responsible for a crime or scandal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized by the 1976 film 'All the President's Men.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> To find out who is really behind the project, you just need to follow the money."
"Foot the bill","<b>Meaning:</b> To pay for something, especially when it is expensive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Adding up the total at the 'foot' (bottom) of a bill and then paying it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My parents offered to foot the bill for our wedding."
"Gain the upper hand","<b>Meaning:</b> To get into a position of advantage or control. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a game where two people move their hands up a bat to see who holds the top. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our company has gained the upper hand in the local market."
"Get a second wind","<b>Meaning:</b> To get a new burst of energy after being tired. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From long-distance running; the point where breathing becomes easier after initial exhaustion. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was exhausted, but after a cup of coffee, I got a second wind."
"Get the sack","<b>Meaning:</b> To be fired from a job. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Tradesmen used to carry their tools in a sack; if they were fired, they were given their sack back. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He got the sack for being late every day."
"Get your feet wet","<b>Meaning:</b> To start doing something new for the first time in a cautious way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Stepping into the edge of water to test it before swimming. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m just starting to get my feet wet in the world of investing."
"Give someone a run for their money","<b>Meaning:</b> To be a strong competitor and make it difficult for someone else to win. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Horse racing; a horse that runs well even if it doesn't win. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our small team gave the champions a real run for their money."
"Go south","<b>Meaning:</b> To deteriorate, decline, or fail. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From maps, where 'down' (south) represents a decrease or a fall. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The deal started to go south when they asked for more money."
"Golden handshake","<b>Meaning:</b> A large sum of money given to a high-ranking employee when they leave a company. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Golden' implies wealth; 'handshake' implies a friendly parting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The CEO received a multi-million dollar golden handshake when he retired."
"Gray area","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation that is not clear or does not fit into a specific rule. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The shade between black and white, where things are not clearly defined. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Whether this is legal or not is a bit of a gray area."
"Grease the wheels","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a process happen more smoothly by providing help or money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Putting grease on a machine's wheels to reduce friction. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> A few phone calls to the right people helped grease the wheels for our permit."
"Hammer home","<b>Meaning:</b> To make something very clear by repeating it or emphasizing it strongly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for driving a nail all the way into wood with repeated strikes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The teacher hammered home the importance of checking your work."
