"Hands are tied","<b>Meaning:</b> To be unable to help or act because of rules or circumstances. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of a person whose hands are bound and cannot move. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’d love to give you a discount, but my hands are tied by company policy."
"Have a card up your sleeve","<b>Meaning:</b> To have a secret plan or advantage that you can use when needed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From dishonest card players who hid good cards in their sleeves. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't worry about the competition; I have a card up my sleeve."
"Have a lot of balls in the air","<b>Meaning:</b> To be dealing with many different tasks at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a juggler keeping many balls moving. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a lot of balls in the air right now, so I might be slow to respond."
"Heads will roll","<b>Meaning:</b> People will be severely punished or fired for a mistake. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A historical reference to execution by beheading. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If this project fails, heads will roll in the marketing department."
"High-flyer","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is very successful or has the potential to be. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing a successful person to a bird or plane that flies higher than others. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was a high-flyer in the tech world before she started her own company."
"Hold the fort","<b>Meaning:</b> To take care of a place or situation while someone else is away. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; maintaining a defensive position until reinforcements arrive. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Can you hold the fort while I go to the bank?"
"In a jam","<b>Meaning:</b> In a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from logs being 'jammed' in a river, making them impossible to move. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm in a bit of a jam; my car broke down and I don't have any money."
"In the driver's seat","<b>Meaning:</b> To be in control of a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The person driving the vehicle determines where it goes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Now that he's been promoted, he's finally in the driver's seat."
"Keep a straight face","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop yourself from laughing even though you find something funny. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for keeping the facial muscles 'straight' or unmoving. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It was hard to keep a straight face when he walked in wearing that hat."
"Keep your eye on the prize","<b>Meaning:</b> To stay focused on your end goal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Common advice for athletes or competitors. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The training is hard, but keep your eye on the prize: the championship."
"Kick the can down the road","<b>Meaning:</b> To delay dealing with a problem instead of solving it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a children's game; metaphorically moving the problem further away without fixing it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The government is just kicking the can down the road instead of fixing the debt."
"Land on your feet","<b>Meaning:</b> To be successful or lucky after a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the way cats almost always land safely on their feet when they fall. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He lost his job, but he landed on his feet with an even better one two weeks later."
"Learn the hard way","<b>Meaning:</b> To learn something through a bad or painful experience. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Contrast to learning through instruction or advice. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I learned the hard way that you should always back up your computer files."
"Left hand doesn't know what the right hand is doing","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where different parts of an organization are not communicating. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Matthew 6:3), though the meaning has changed from charity to confusion. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The sales team is offering discounts the billing department won't accept; the left hand doesn't know what the right hand is doing."
"Light at the end of the tunnel","<b>Meaning:</b> A sign that a difficult period is almost over. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for traveling through a dark tunnel and seeing the exit. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We’ve been working on this for months, but we finally see the light at the end of the tunnel."
"Long shot","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that has a very small chance of succeeding. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From shooting or archery; a shot from a long distance is very difficult to hit. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s a long shot, but we might win the lottery."
"Loose ends","<b>Meaning:</b> Small details that haven't been finished yet. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sailing; untied ropes hanging from a ship's rigging. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I just have a few loose ends to tie up before I can leave for vacation."
"Mainstay","<b>Meaning:</b> A person or thing that something else depends on. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; the main rope that supports a ship's mast. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Tourism is the mainstay of the local economy."
"Make a mountain out of a molehill","<b>Meaning:</b> To treat a small problem as if it were a massive one. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'molehill' is a tiny pile of dirt; a mountain is enormous. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You only lost one pen; stop making a mountain out of a molehill."
"Move the needle","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a noticeable or significant difference in a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gauges or meters where a needle moves to show progress. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need an advertising campaign that will actually move the needle on sales."
"No-brainer","<b>Meaning:</b> A decision that is very easy to make because the answer is obvious. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A choice so simple you don't even need to use your brain. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Taking a free trip to Hawaii is a no-brainer."
"Not going to fly","<b>Meaning:</b> A plan or idea that will not be accepted or will not work. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a plane that cannot get off the ground. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Your request for a two-month vacation is just not going to fly."
"Odds and ends","<b>Meaning:</b> Small, unimportant things that are left over. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'odd' (leftover) amounts and 'ends' of fabric or lumber. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a drawer full of odds and ends like old keys and batteries."
"Off the clock","<b>Meaning:</b> Not working; during your personal time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'punching' a time clock to record work hours. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't talk about work right now; I'm off the clock."
"On the back burner","<b>Meaning:</b> Deciding to deal with something later; giving it low priority. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From cooking; the back burners of a stove are used for items that don't need immediate attention. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Let's put the website redesign on the back burner until next year."
"On the fence","<b>Meaning:</b> Unable to decide between two options. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A person sitting on a fence isn't in either of the two fields on either side. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm still on the fence about whether to buy a car or a motorcycle."
"On the same page","<b>Meaning:</b> To have the same understanding or agreement about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a group of people reading from the same book or document. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need a meeting to make sure everyone is on the same page."
"Out of the loop","<b>Meaning:</b> Not part of a group that is kept informed about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See 'In the loop.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was on vacation, so I'm completely out of the loop on the new project."
"Paper tiger","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone or something that appears powerful but is actually weak or harmless. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A Chinese phrase popularized by Mao Zedong. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Their legal threats are just a paper tiger; they have no real case."
"Passing the torch","<b>Meaning:</b> Handing over a responsibility or a role to someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From relay races where a literal torch is passed from one runner to another. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After thirty years, the professor is finally passing the torch to a younger colleague."
"Penny for your thoughts","<b>Meaning:</b> A way of asking someone what they are thinking about. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a time when a penny was a reasonable 'price' for a bit of information. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’ve been quiet for a while; a penny for your thoughts?"
"Pick someone's brain","<b>Meaning:</b> To ask someone for information or advice because they know a lot about a subject. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'extracting' knowledge. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Can I pick your brain about the new software?"
"Play your cards right","<b>Meaning:</b> To behave in a way that will give you an advantage or lead to success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From card games like poker or bridge. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you play your cards right, you could be the next manager."
"Poison pill","<b>Meaning:</b> A strategy used by a company to make itself less attractive to a buyer in a hostile takeover. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From spies who carried a literal poison pill to avoid being captured. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company's board of directors is considering a poison pill to stop the buyout."
"Pull out all the stops","<b>Meaning:</b> To use all the resources and energy available to achieve something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From pipe organs; pulling out the 'stops' allows all the pipes to sound at once. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They pulled out all the stops for their daughter's wedding."
"Put the cart before the horse","<b>Meaning:</b> To do things in the wrong order. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A horse must be in front to pull a cart. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Buying furniture before you have a house is putting the cart before the horse."
"Raise a red flag","<b>Meaning:</b> To signal that something is wrong or needs attention. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Red flags are traditionally used to signal danger or a stop. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The fact that he hasn't answered any emails is raising a red flag."
"Raking in the money","<b>Meaning:</b> To earn a lot of money very easily. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for gathering money as easily as raking leaves. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That new movie is raking in the money."
"Read between the lines","<b>Meaning:</b> To find a hidden meaning in something that is said or written. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From cryptography; sometimes messages were written in invisible ink between the lines of regular text. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He said he was 'fine,' but if you read between the lines, you can tell he's upset."
"Red-carpet treatment","<b>Meaning:</b> To give someone very special and luxurious treatment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the tradition of rolling out a red carpet for kings and celebrities. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The airline gave us the red-carpet treatment because it was our honeymoon."
"Reinvent the wheel","<b>Meaning:</b> To waste time trying to create something that has already been created. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The wheel is a perfect invention; trying to 'reinvent' it is pointless. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We don't need to reinvent the wheel; let's just use the existing template."
"Right-hand man","<b>Meaning:</b> A person's most trusted and helpful assistant. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Historically, the person standing at your right side was in the best position to defend you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s the CEO's right-hand man."
"Rock and a hard place","<b>Meaning:</b> Facing a choice between two equally unpleasant options. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See 'Between a rock and a hard place.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm between a rock and a hard place: I either lose my job or work on the weekend."
"Run a tight ship","<b>Meaning:</b> To manage an organization or group in a very strict and efficient way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; a captain who keeps his ship clean and his crew disciplined. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My grandmother runs a tight ship; you have to be at the table exactly at 6 PM."
"Safe bet","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is very likely to happen or be successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; a bet that has very little risk. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It's a safe bet that it will rain today."
"See the big picture","<b>Meaning:</b> To understand the whole situation rather than just the small details. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for stepping back from a painting to see the entire image. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need a manager who can see the big picture."
"Sell like hotcakes","<b>Meaning:</b> To sell very quickly and in large quantities. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Hotcakes' (pancakes) were very popular at church fairs and sold as soon as they were made. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new iPhone is selling like hotcakes."
"Sever ties","<b>Meaning:</b> To end a relationship or agreement completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for cutting the ropes (ties) that connect two things. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company decided to sever ties with their supplier."
"Shoot from the hip","<b>Meaning:</b> To speak or act quickly and impulsively without thinking. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From cowboys in the Old West who would fire their guns without aiming properly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s known for shooting from the hip during press conferences."
"Shot in the arm","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that gives you a sudden burst of energy or encouragement. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a medical injection of a stimulant. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new investment was a real shot in the arm for the struggling company."
"Show someone the ropes","<b>Meaning:</b> To teach someone how to do a job or activity. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; teaching a new sailor which ropes to pull. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Can you show the new employee the ropes?"
"Sink or swim","<b>Meaning:</b> To fail or succeed by your own efforts without any help. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for being thrown into water and having to figure out how to stay afloat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m not going to help him; it’s time for him to sink or swim."
"Small fry","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is not important or powerful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Fry' are newly hatched fish. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s just small fry in this company; he can't make any real decisions."
"Stay the course","<b>Meaning:</b> To continue with a plan even though it is difficult. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; keeping a ship on its intended path regardless of the weather. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to stay the course if we want to finish the project on time."
"Step up to the plate","<b>Meaning:</b> To take responsibility for something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From baseball; the batter 'steps up' to the home plate to take their turn. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need someone to step up to the plate and lead this project."
"Sweeten the deal","<b>Meaning:</b> To make an offer more attractive by adding something extra. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for adding sugar to something to make it taste better. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They offered me a higher salary and a company car to sweeten the deal."
"Front","Back"
"Fair and square","<b>Meaning:</b> Honestly and according to the rules. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Square' has long been used to describe things that are level or honest. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We won the game fair and square."
"Far cry from","<b>Meaning:</b> Very different from something else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to a distance being too far for a shout (cry) to be heard. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This hotel is a far cry from the luxury palace shown in the brochure."
"Feel like a million bucks","<b>Meaning:</b> To feel very healthy, happy, and confident. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the high value and quality associated with a million dollars. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After that vacation, I feel like a million bucks."
"Fill someone's shoes","<b>Meaning:</b> To take over someone's role and do it as well as they did. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for stepping into the physical space and responsibilities of another. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The old manager was great; it will be hard for anyone to fill his shoes."
"Fine-tooth comb","<b>Meaning:</b> To examine something very carefully for even the smallest details. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using a comb with teeth very close together to find tiny parasites or dirt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The lawyers went through the contract with a fine-tooth comb."
"Fish for compliments","<b>Meaning:</b> To try to make someone say something nice about you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing seeking praise to casting a line and waiting for a fish to bite. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She kept saying she looked tired, but she was just fishing for compliments."
"Fit as a fiddle","<b>Meaning:</b> In very good physical health. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Fit' originally meant 'suitable.' A fiddle must be kept in good condition to play correctly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My grandfather is 90, but he's still fit as a fiddle."
"Fly off the handle","<b>Meaning:</b> To suddenly become very angry. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an axe head becoming loose and flying off the handle unexpectedly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He tends to fly off the handle if you criticize his work."
"Follow in someone's footsteps","<b>Meaning:</b> To do the same job or live in the same way as someone else before you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of walking in the tracks left by a predecessor. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He decided to follow in his father's footsteps and become a doctor."
"Food for thought","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is worth thinking about seriously. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likening information to food that needs to be digested by the brain. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The documentary gave us a lot of food for thought regarding climate change."
"Fool's gold","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that looks valuable but is actually worthless. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Iron pyrite, a mineral that looks like gold but has no value. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That cheap stock was fool's gold; I lost all my money."
"Foot in the door","<b>Meaning:</b> To take a small first step toward a bigger goal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Door-to-door salesmen would literally put their foot in the door so it couldn't be closed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This internship is just a foot in the door at the film studio."
"From the get-go","<b>Meaning:</b> From the very beginning. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the 'get-go' or start of a race. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We knew this project would be difficult from the get-go."
"Get a kick out of","<b>Meaning:</b> To enjoy something very much. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'kick' refers to a sudden surge of excitement or adrenaline. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My dad really gets a kick out of watching old cartoons."
"Get down to brass tacks","<b>Meaning:</b> To start talking about the most important facts of a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from haberdashery; brass tacks on a counter were used to measure cloth precisely. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop wasting time and let's get down to brass tacks: how much will this cost?"
"Get out of hand","<b>Meaning:</b> To become chaotic or difficult to control. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse riding; when a rider loses control of the reins. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The party got out of hand once the music got too loud."
"Give someone the cold shoulder","<b>Meaning:</b> To intentionally ignore or be unfriendly to someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In the past, guests who weren't welcome were served a cold piece of meat (the shoulder) instead of a hot meal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I tried to say hello, but she gave me the cold shoulder."
"Give someone the green light","<b>Meaning:</b> To give permission for someone to start something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From traffic signals. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The committee finally gave us the green light to build the park."
"Go against the grain","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something that is the opposite of what is normal or natural. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Wood is easier to cut along the fibers (the grain) than across them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Telling people to spend less money goes against the grain of our consumer culture."
"Go back to the drawing board","<b>Meaning:</b> To start over because a previous attempt failed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From architectural or engineering projects where the plans are drawn on a large board. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The prototype didn't work, so it's back to the drawing board."
"Go cold turkey","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop a habit suddenly and completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the cold, clammy skin people get when withdrawing from an addiction. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He quit smoking cold turkey."
"Go down in flames","<b>Meaning:</b> To fail spectacularly and completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a plane crashing and burning. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His career went down in flames after the scandal."
"Go the extra mile","<b>Meaning:</b> To do more than what is required or expected. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; carrying a pack for a Roman soldier. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She always goes the extra mile for her clients."
"Good Samaritan","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who helps a stranger in trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a parable in the Bible about a man from Samaria who helped a traveler. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> A good Samaritan helped me change my tire in the rain."
"Great minds think alike","<b>Meaning:</b> Said when two people have the same idea at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A humorous way to suggest that both people are very intelligent. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> 'I was just about to suggest pizza!' 'Great minds think alike!'"
"Green with envy","<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely jealous. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Greeks believed jealousy caused an overproduction of bile, turning the skin slightly green. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was green with envy when she saw my new car."
"Hang in there","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't give up despite difficulties. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone hanging onto a ledge or rope. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know the work is hard, but hang in there; it's almost finished."
"Hard nut to crack","<b>Meaning:</b> A person or problem that is difficult to understand or deal with. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal reference to a nut with a very thick shell. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Solving this code is a hard nut to crack."
"Have a sweet tooth","<b>Meaning:</b> To have a great love for sugary foods. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 14th century; 'sweet' used to mean pleasing or dainty. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a real sweet tooth; I can't resist chocolate."
"Have bigger fish to fry","<b>Meaning:</b> To have more important things to do or think about. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for prioritizing a larger task over a small one. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm not worried about the broken pen; I have bigger fish to fry."
"Head over heels","<b>Meaning:</b> Deeply in love. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally 'heels over head,' describing a cartwheel or a tumble. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He's head over heels in love with his new wife."
"Heart of gold","<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely kind and generous. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Gold is the most valuable and 'pure' metal, representing a pure spirit. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He looks tough, but he has a heart of gold."
"High on the hog","<b>Meaning:</b> Living in luxury or eating expensive food. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The best cuts of meat are found higher up on the pig. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Since they won the lottery, they've been living high on the hog."
"Hit the nail on the head","<b>Meaning:</b> To describe exactly what is causing a situation or problem. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From carpentry; hitting the nail exactly on the top for a perfect result. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You hit the nail on the head when you said we need better communication."
"Ignorance is bliss","<b>Meaning:</b> You are happier when you do not know about the problems around you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 1742 poem by Thomas Gray. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't know the house was haunted, and I slept fine—ignorance is bliss!"
"In the same boat","<b>Meaning:</b> In the same difficult situation as someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> If a boat sinks, everyone on it faces the same danger regardless of status. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We're all in the same boat; none of us got a bonus this year."
"It takes two to tango","<b>Meaning:</b> Both people involved in a bad situation are responsible for it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 1952 song; the tango dance cannot be performed alone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He says it's her fault, but it takes two to tango."
"Keep an eye on","<b>Meaning:</b> To watch something or someone carefully. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for constant observation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Can you keep an eye on my bag while I go to the bathroom?"
"Keep someone at arm's length","<b>Meaning:</b> To avoid being too friendly or close to someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Physically pushing someone away so they cannot touch you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I like him as a coworker, but I keep him at arm's length socially."
"Kill them with kindness","<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely nice to someone who is being mean to you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A strategy to make an enemy feel ashamed of their own behavior. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Instead of arguing with the angry customer, she just killed him with kindness."
"Last resort","<b>Meaning:</b> The final option you use when everything else has failed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Legal; the highest court you can appeal to. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We will sue them only as a last resort."
"Let the cat out of the bag","<b>Meaning:</b> To accidentally reveal a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a trick where a cat was sold in a bag as a pig; if the cat was let out, the secret was revealed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I let the cat out of the bag about the surprise party."
"Like a broken record","<b>Meaning:</b> Saying the same thing over and over again in an annoying way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A scratch on a vinyl record causes the needle to skip and repeat the same music. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know I need to clean my room; stop sounding like a broken record!"
"Live from hand to mouth","<b>Meaning:</b> To have just enough money to buy food and nothing else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> When food is so scarce it goes directly from the hand to the mouth with no storage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Many people are living from hand to mouth during the recession."
"Long story short","<b>Meaning:</b> To skip the details and get to the point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal summary. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Long story short, we got married in Vegas."
"Look before you leap","<b>Meaning:</b> Think about the possible dangers before you do something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You should check the house for repairs before buying it; look before you leap."
"Loose lips sink ships","<b>Meaning:</b> Talking too much or sharing secrets can cause a disaster. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A WWII slogan to warn people that spies might hear their secrets. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't tell anyone about the merger yet; loose lips sink ships."
"Make a killing","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a large amount of money very quickly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hunting; a 'killing' provided a massive amount of food for little effort. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He made a killing on the stock market last year."
"Method to my madness","<b>Meaning:</b> There is a logical reason for my strange behavior. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Shakespeare's 'Hamlet.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It looks messy, but there's a method to my madness; I know where everything is."
"Money doesn't grow on trees","<b>Meaning:</b> Money is limited and you have to work hard to get it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A parent's classic warning to children about spending. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Turn off the lights; money doesn't grow on trees!"
"Neck of the woods","<b>Meaning:</b> A specific neighborhood or area. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Neck' refers to a narrow strip of land or a clearing in a forest. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't know you lived in this neck of the woods."
"No pain, no gain","<b>Meaning:</b> You have to work hard and suffer a little to achieve something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 17th-century proverb, popularized by fitness culture. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My muscles hurt after the gym, but no pain, no gain."
"Not my cup of tea","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that I do not like or am not interested in. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the British obsession with tea; if it's not 'your' cup, it doesn't suit your taste. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Opera isn't really my cup of tea."
"Off the top of my head","<b>Meaning:</b> From memory, without checking the facts. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Information that is 'resting' on the surface of the mind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Off the top of my head, I think we have twenty employees."
"On the ball","<b>Meaning:</b> Alert, knowledgeable, and quick to act. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sports; a player who keeps their eyes on the ball. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She's really on the ball; she noticed the mistake before anyone else."
"Once in a blue moon","<b>Meaning:</b> Very rarely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'blue moon' is the second full moon in a single month, which happens only once every few years. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I only see my cousins once in a blue moon."
"Out of the blue","<b>Meaning:</b> Suddenly and unexpectedly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Short for 'a bolt out of the blue' (a lightning bolt from a clear blue sky). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He called me out of the blue after ten years."
"Piece of cake","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is very easy to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from 19th-century 'cake walks' where the prize was easy to win. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That exam was a piece of cake."
"Play it by ear","<b>Meaning:</b> To decide what to do as a situation develops rather than following a plan. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From musicians who play without reading sheet music. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We don't have a hotel booked; we'll just play it by ear."
"Pull yourself together","<b>Meaning:</b> To calm down and control your emotions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'gathering' your scattered thoughts or feelings. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop crying and pull yourself together; we have work to do."
"Put all your eggs in one basket","<b>Meaning:</b> To risk everything on a single plan or idea. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> If you drop the basket, you lose every egg. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't invest all your money in one stock; don't put all your eggs in one basket."
"Ring a bell","<b>Meaning:</b> To sound familiar. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from Pavlovian conditioning where a bell triggers a memory. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That name rings a bell, but I can't remember where I heard it."
"Rule of thumb","<b>Meaning:</b> A general or approximate rule based on experience rather than exact science. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From using the thumb as a rough measurement tool. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As a rule of thumb, you should save 10% of your income."
"See eye to eye","<b>Meaning:</b> To agree with someone completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; looking directly at someone because you are in accord. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My boss and I don't always see eye to eye."
"Shed light on","<b>Meaning:</b> To provide more information to help understand a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for shining a lamp into a dark room. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Can you shed some light on why the project was delayed?"
"Sit tight","<b>Meaning:</b> To wait patiently and not take any action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for sitting still so you aren't noticed or don't move. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Sit tight while I go find the manager."
"Skeleton in the closet","<b>Meaning:</b> An embarrassing or shameful secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a hidden body that has turned to bone over time. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Every family has a skeleton in the closet."
"Sleep on it","<b>Meaning:</b> To wait until the next day to make a decision. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The idea that your mind works on a problem while you sleep. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm not sure if I should buy the car; let me sleep on it."
"Small world","<b>Meaning:</b> Said when you encounter a surprising coincidence. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A common reaction to unexpected meetings. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You know my brother? Small world!"
"Smooth operator","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is very skillful at dealing with people or situations, often in a deceptive way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized by the 1984 Sade song. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He managed to get the promotion without doing any work; he's a smooth operator."
"Snowed under","<b>Meaning:</b> To have too much work or too many things to deal with. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for being buried by a heavy snowfall. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm sorry I didn't call; I've been snowed under with reports."
"Spill the beans","<b>Meaning:</b> To give away a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Greek voting jars. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Who spilled the beans about my surprise party?"
"Stand your ground","<b>Meaning:</b> To refuse to change your opinion or give in to pressure. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; refusing to retreat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You must stand your ground and demand a refund."
"Steal the show","<b>Meaning:</b> To get all the attention and praise in a performance or event. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From theater; when a supporting actor performs better than the star. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The little girl's dance really stole the show."
"Stiff upper lip","<b>Meaning:</b> To remain brave and not show emotion in a difficult time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A trembling lip is a sign of crying. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He kept a stiff upper lip during the funeral."
"Straight from the horse's mouth","<b>Meaning:</b> From the most reliable source. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Examining a horse's teeth to find its age. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I heard it straight from the horse's mouth: we're getting a raise."
"Take it easy","<b>Meaning:</b> To relax or be calm. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A standard modern instruction to reduce stress. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Take it easy; there's no need to rush."
"The ball is in your court","<b>Meaning:</b> It is your turn to take action or make a decision. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From tennis; the ball has been hit to your side of the net. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I've made my offer; now the ball is in your court."
"The best of both worlds","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where you can enjoy the advantages of two different things. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A descriptive phrase. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She has the best of both worlds: a great job and a happy family."
"The elephant in the room","<b>Meaning:</b> An obvious problem that no one wants to discuss. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Something too large to ignore, yet ignored anyway. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to talk about the elephant in the room: our debt."
"The last straw","<b>Meaning:</b> The final problem in a series of problems that makes you lose your patience. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'the straw that broke the camel's back.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When he was late again, it was the last straw; I fired him."
"The whole nine yards","<b>Meaning:</b> Everything; the entire amount. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Disputed; possibly ammunition belt lengths. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We did the kitchen, the bathroom, the whole nine yards."
"Think big","<b>Meaning:</b> To have ambitious goals and plans. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A modern motivational phrase. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you want to be successful, you have to think big."
"Through thick and thin","<b>Meaning:</b> In both good times and bad times. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From thickets and thin woods. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She stayed with him through thick and thin."
"Throw in the towel","<b>Meaning:</b> To give up. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Boxing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't solve this puzzle; I'm throwing in the towel."
"Tie the knot","<b>Meaning:</b> To get married. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hand-fasting ceremonies. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They're planning to tie the knot in June."
"Time flies","<b>Meaning:</b> Time passes very quickly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Latin 'Tempus fugit.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It's already midnight? Time flies!"
"To each their own","<b>Meaning:</b> Everyone has their own preferences or opinions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 16th-century proverb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't like raw fish, but to each their own."
"Touch base","<b>Meaning:</b> To briefly contact someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Baseball. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Let's touch base next week."
"Under the weather","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling sick. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm feeling a bit under the weather."
"Up in the air","<b>Meaning:</b> Uncertain or undecided. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for something not yet landed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our plans are still up in the air."
"Variety is the spice of life","<b>Meaning:</b> New experiences make life interesting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 1785 poem. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I like to travel; variety is the spice of life."
"Walking on air","<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely happy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Metaphor for lightness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm walking on air today."
"Water under the bridge","<b>Meaning:</b> Past events no longer worth worrying about. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> River current metaphor. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It's water under the bridge."
"Wear your heart on your sleeve","<b>Meaning:</b> To show your emotions openly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Shakespeare's 'Othello.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He wears his heart on his sleeve."
"When in Rome","<b>Meaning:</b> Follow local customs. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> St. Ambrose. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They eat late here, so when in Rome..."
"When pigs fly","<b>Meaning:</b> Never. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Impossible image. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He'll clean his room when pigs fly."
"Wild goose chase","<b>Meaning:</b> A pointless search. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Shakespeare. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Looking for that old key was a wild goose chase."
"Work like a charm","<b>Meaning:</b> To work perfectly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Magic metaphor. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This new software works like a charm."
"You can't judge a book by its cover","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't judge based on appearance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 19th-century proverb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He's very smart despite his messy clothes; you can't judge a book by its cover."

"Speak of the devil","<b>Meaning:</b> Said when a person appears just as you are talking about them. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the superstition that mentioning the devil would cause him to appear. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was just telling her about your new car, and here you are! Speak of the devil."
"Spill the tea","<b>Meaning:</b> To share gossip or juicy secrets. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized in drag culture; 'T' stands for 'Truth.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I saw them arguing at the party—do you want me to spill the tea?"
"Square one","<b>Meaning:</b> The very beginning; the starting point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from board games or early radio football broadcasts where the field was divided into squares. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The deal fell through, so we are back to square one."
"Stand your ground","<b>Meaning:</b> To refuse to change your opinion or give in to pressure. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; a soldier refusing to retreat from their position. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The neighbors tried to make me move my fence, but I stood my ground."
"Start from scratch","<b>Meaning:</b> To begin from the very beginning with no preparation or advantage. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From races where the starting line was 'scratched' into the dirt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We lost the files, so we have to start the project from scratch."
"Steal someone's thunder","<b>Meaning:</b> To take the credit or attention away from someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An 18th-century playwright invented a thunder sound effect; a rival theater stole the method for their own play. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was about to announce my engagement, but my sister stole my thunder by announcing her pregnancy."
"Stiff upper lip","<b>Meaning:</b> To show great courage and not show emotion in a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> If your lip trembles, you are about to cry; keeping it 'stiff' shows control. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Even though he lost the race, he kept a stiff upper lip."
"Stone's throw","<b>Meaning:</b> A very short distance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The distance a person can physically throw a stone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The hotel is just a stone's throw from the beach."
"Straight from the horse's mouth","<b>Meaning:</b> Information directly from the original or most reliable source. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; checking a horse's teeth is the only way to know its true age. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know the company is moving; I heard it straight from the horse's mouth."
"Straw that broke the camel's back","<b>Meaning:</b> The final small problem that makes a situation intolerable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for adding one tiny piece of weight to an already overloaded animal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was already stressed, but the flat tire was the straw that broke the camel's back."
"Stretch the truth","<b>Meaning:</b> To exaggerate or lie slightly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for pulling the truth until it becomes distorted. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He said he caught a giant fish, but I think he was stretching the truth."
"Strike a chord","<b>Meaning:</b> To cause someone to feel sympathy or enthusiasm; to remind someone of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From music; playing a note that resonates with another. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Her story about growing up in a small town really struck a chord with the audience."
"Swan song","<b>Meaning:</b> A final performance or activity before retirement or death. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient belief that swans sing a beautiful song just before they die. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The legendary singer's concert in London was her swan song."
"Sweep it under the rug","<b>Meaning:</b> To hide a problem or an embarrassing fact instead of dealing with it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of a lazy cleaner hiding dirt under a carpet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You can't just sweep these complaints under the rug and hope they go away."
"Swing for the fences","<b>Meaning:</b> To take a big risk in order to achieve a huge success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From baseball; trying to hit a home run over the outfield fence. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We're swinging for the fences with this new product launch."
"Take it with a grain of salt","<b>Meaning:</b> To not believe something completely; to be skeptical. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Romans believed salt made poison less effective. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He says he's a billionaire, but you should take that with a grain of salt."
"Take the bull by the horns","<b>Meaning:</b> To deal with a difficult situation directly and bravely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of a person wrestling a bull. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop complaining about your job and take the bull by the horns—talk to your boss."
"Take the plunge","<b>Meaning:</b> To decide to do something difficult or important after thinking about it for a long time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for jumping into deep water. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They finally decided to take the plunge and get married."
"Talk the hind legs off a donkey","<b>Meaning:</b> To talk for a very long time without stopping. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An exaggeration of how exhausting someone's talking can be. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I like my aunt, but she can talk the hind legs off a donkey."
"That ship has sailed","<b>Meaning:</b> It is too late to take an opportunity. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for missing a boat that has already left the port. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I wanted to buy that house, but someone else already did. That ship has sailed."
"The elephant in the room","<b>Meaning:</b> An obvious problem or difficult situation that people are avoiding talking about. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Something as large as an elephant cannot be ignored, yet people often try to. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We talked about the budget, but no one mentioned the elephant in the room: our boss is leaving."
"The grass is always greener on the other side","<b>Meaning:</b> Other people's situations always seem better than your own. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A proverb dating back to ancient times about envy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He quit his job for a new one, but now he misses his old coworkers; the grass is always greener."
"The jury is still out","<b>Meaning:</b> A decision has not yet been reached; the result is still unknown. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the legal system; when the jury leaves the courtroom to deliberate. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The jury is still out on whether the new law will actually help the economy."
"The world is your oyster","<b>Meaning:</b> You have the ability and opportunity to do anything you want. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Shakespeare's 'The Merry Wives of Windsor'; implies you can find the 'pearl' inside the world. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’ve just graduated with top honors; the world is your oyster."
"Think outside the box","<b>Meaning:</b> To think in an innovative and creative way, ignoring conventional limits. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the 'nine dots' puzzle which requires drawing lines outside a square box. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to think outside the box to solve this marketing problem."
"Third time's a charm","<b>Meaning:</b> The third time you try something, it will be successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient belief in the luck or power of the number three. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I failed my driving test twice, but third time's a charm!"
"Through thick and thin","<b>Meaning:</b> Under all circumstances, no matter how difficult. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'through thicket and thin wood,' describing a difficult journey through a forest. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They have been best friends through thick and thin for twenty years."
"Throw in the towel","<b>Meaning:</b> To give up or admit defeat. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; a trainer throws a towel into the ring to stop a fight if their boxer is being badly beaten. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After trying to fix the car for six hours, I finally threw in the towel."
"Throw someone under the bus","<b>Meaning:</b> To betray a friend or ally for personal gain. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for sacrificing someone's safety for your own convenience. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My coworker threw me under the bus by blaming me for his mistake."
"Tie the knot","<b>Meaning:</b> To get married. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ancient hand-fasting ceremonies where the couple's hands were literally tied together. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After ten years of dating, they finally decided to tie the knot."
"Tip of the iceberg","<b>Meaning:</b> A small visible part of a much larger problem or situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Only about 10% of an iceberg is visible above the water. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> These complaints are just the tip of the iceberg; the whole system is broken."
"To be in a pickle","<b>Meaning:</b> To be in a difficult or unpleasant situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 16th century; being 'mixed up' or 'preserved' like vegetables in vinegar. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I forgot my wallet and my car is out of gas—I'm in a bit of a pickle."
"To cost a fortune","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very expensive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'fortune' is a massive amount of wealth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That diamond ring must have cost a fortune."
"To have a change of heart","<b>Meaning:</b> To change your opinion or decision. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The heart is seen as the center of decision-making and feelings. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I wasn't going to go to the party, but I had a change of heart."
"To kill two birds with one stone","<b>Meaning:</b> To achieve two things with a single action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 17th century; a metaphor for efficiency. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can drop the kids at school on my way to work and kill two birds with one stone."
"To make a long story short","<b>Meaning:</b> To skip the unnecessary details and get to the point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal request for brevity. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> To make a long story short, we missed the plane."
"To play it safe","<b>Meaning:</b> To avoid risks and act carefully. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A standard advice for caution. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I think we should play it safe and take the umbrella, just in case."
"To pull someone's leg","<b>Meaning:</b> To joke with someone or trick them in a playful way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from the way thieves used to trip people to rob them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't believe him; he's just pulling your leg."
"To put someone on the spot","<b>Meaning:</b> To ask someone a difficult question or put them in a difficult situation in front of others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for forcing someone to stand in a 'spotlight.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't mean to put you on the spot, but we need an answer now."
"To sit on the fence","<b>Meaning:</b> To avoid making a decision or choosing a side in an argument. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A person sitting on a fence is not in either of the two fields on either side. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You can't sit on the fence forever; you have to pick a side."
"To spill the beans","<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Greeks used beans to vote secretly; spilling them revealed the results. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We were going to surprise her, but Tim spilled the beans."
"To take someone under your wing","<b>Meaning:</b> To protect and guide someone who has less experience. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a mother bird protecting her chicks. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The senior manager took the new intern under her wing."
"To turn a blind eye","<b>Meaning:</b> To ignore something that you know is wrong. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Admiral Nelson supposedly held his telescope to his blind eye so he wouldn't 'see' a signal to retreat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The teacher turned a blind eye to the students whispering."
"To walk on eggshells","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very careful about what you say or do around someone to avoid upsetting them. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of how carefully you would have to move to avoid breaking fragile eggs. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone in the office is walking on eggshells because the boss is in a bad mood."
"Top-notch","<b>Meaning:</b> Excellent; of the highest quality. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From competitive games where the 'top notch' on a scoring stick represented the highest score. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The service at that hotel is absolutely top-notch."
"Touch and go","<b>Meaning:</b> An uncertain and risky situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; when a ship's hull 'touches' the bottom but manages to 'go' on. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It was touch and go for a few minutes, but the doctor says he will be fine."
"Trial and error","<b>Meaning:</b> Trying different methods until you find the one that works. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A basic method of problem-solving. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I learned how to use the software through trial and error."
"True colors","<b>Meaning:</b> A person's real character or intentions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See 'Show your true colors.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He seemed nice at first, but he showed his true colors when he got angry."
"Turn over a new leaf","<b>Meaning:</b> To change your behavior and start acting in a better way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Leaf' refers to a page in a book; starting a new chapter of life. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After his arrest, he promised to turn over a new leaf and stay out of trouble."
"Twist someone's arm","<b>Meaning:</b> To persuade someone to do something they don't really want to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for physical coercion. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't want to go to the party, but my friend twisted my arm."
"Two cents","<b>Meaning:</b> Your opinion about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 19th century; the idea that an opinion is worth very little. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you want my two cents, I think you should buy the red one."
"Under the table","<b>Meaning:</b> Secretly and often illegally. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hiding a transaction 'under the table' where officials can't see it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was paid under the table to avoid paying taxes."
"Under the weather","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling slightly ill. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; sailors who were ill would go below deck to get 'under' the bad weather. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m feeling a bit under the weather, so I’m going to stay home today."
"Up in the air","<b>Meaning:</b> Not yet decided or settled. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for something that hasn't 'landed' yet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our travel plans are still up in the air because of the flight delays."
"Up to speed","<b>Meaning:</b> Having all the latest information about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From engines reaching their required operating speed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Give me a few minutes to get up to speed on the new project."
"Upper hand","<b>Meaning:</b> An advantage or a position of control. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an old game of 'hand-over-hand' on a stick or bat to see who goes first. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our team has the upper hand because the other team is missing their best player."
"Upset the apple cart","<b>Meaning:</b> To ruin someone's plans or a stable situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of knocking over a cart full of apples. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We had everything planned, but his resignation really upset the apple cart."
"Vanish into thin air","<b>Meaning:</b> To disappear completely and suddenly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Shakespeare's 'The Tempest'; ghosts or spirits turning into air. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I saw him a minute ago, but he's vanished into thin air."
"Variety is the spice of life","<b>Meaning:</b> Different experiences and changes make life more interesting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 1785 poem by William Cowper. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I like to try a different restaurant every week; variety is the spice of life."
"Wait for the dust to settle","<b>Meaning:</b> To wait for a situation to become calm or clear before taking action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for waiting for the dirt kicked up by a fight or explosion to land. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Let's wait for the dust to settle after the merger before we make any changes."
"Wake-up call","<b>Meaning:</b> An event that makes you realize you need to change something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hotels providing a telephone call to wake guests up. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His heart attack was a real wake-up call to start exercising."
"Walk on air","<b>Meaning:</b> To feel extremely happy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for feeling so light with joy that you don't touch the ground. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I've been walking on air since I got my promotion."
"Water under the bridge","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that happened in the past and is no longer important or worth arguing about. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Water that has already flowed past is gone forever. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We had a fight last year, but that's water under the bridge now."
"Weather the storm","<b>Meaning:</b> To survive a difficult or dangerous situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; a ship surviving a literal storm at sea. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company is struggling, but I think we can weather the storm."
"What goes around comes around","<b>Meaning:</b> The way you treat others will eventually happen to you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A modern proverb about karma. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was mean to his coworkers, and now no one will help him. What goes around comes around."
"When in Rome, do as the Romans do","<b>Meaning:</b> When you are in a new place, follow the local customs and behavior. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Advice from St. Ambrose to St. Augustine in the 4th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't usually eat spicy food, but everyone here does, so when in Rome..."
"When pigs fly","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that will never happen. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An impossible image used to mock an unlikely statement. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’ll give you your money back when pigs fly."
"Whistle-blower","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who tells the public or the authorities about something illegal or dishonest happening in an organization. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From referees or police using a whistle to stop an action. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The whistle-blower revealed that the factory was dumping chemicals into the river."
"Whole nine yards","<b>Meaning:</b> Everything; the entire amount. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Disputed; possibly from the length of WWII ammunition belts or fabric for kilts. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I want the full package—the insurance, the warranty, the whole nine yards."
"Wild goose chase","<b>Meaning:</b> A search that is completely unsuccessful and a waste of time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Used by Shakespeare; refers to an old type of horse race that was difficult to follow. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I spent all day looking for that shop, but it was a wild goose chase—it closed years ago."
"Win-win situation","<b>Meaning:</b> A result that is good for everyone involved. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Modern business terminology. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If we trade tasks, you can finish early and I can work from home—it's a win-win situation."
"With a grain of salt","<b>Meaning:</b> To not take something too seriously or literally. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See 'Take with a grain of salt.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He exaggerates a lot, so take his stories with a grain of salt."
"Wolf in sheep's clothing","<b>Meaning:</b> A dangerous person who pretends to be harmless. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible and Aesop's fables. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Be careful with him; he seems nice, but he's a wolf in sheep's clothing."
"Word of mouth","<b>Meaning:</b> Information passed from person to person through talking. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of verbal communication. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Most of our customers come to us through word of mouth."
"Work like a charm","<b>Meaning:</b> To work perfectly or very effectively. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likening a successful result to a magical spell (charm). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I put some oil on the door hinge, and it works like a charm now."
"Wrap your head around something","<b>Meaning:</b> To understand something complicated or surprising. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the mind 'encompassing' a difficult idea. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m trying to wrap my head around the fact that I just won the lottery."
"Wrong side of the bed","<b>Meaning:</b> To be in a bad mood for no particular reason. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See 'Get up on the wrong side of the bed.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't talk to her today; she got up on the wrong side of the bed."
"You are what you eat","<b>Meaning:</b> Your health is determined by the food you consume. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 19th-century French philosophy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you only eat junk food, you'll feel terrible. You are what you eat."
"You can't judge a book by its cover","<b>Meaning:</b> You shouldn't judge someone or something based only on how they look. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 19th-century proverb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He looks mean, but he's actually very kind; you can't judge a book by its cover."
"You can't teach an old dog new tricks","<b>Meaning:</b> It is difficult to make someone change their habits or learn something new when they are old. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal observation about training animals. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My grandfather refuses to use a smartphone; you can't teach an old dog new tricks."
"You reap what you sow","<b>Meaning:</b> You will eventually face the consequences of your actions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Galatians 6:7). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was lazy all year and failed his exams. You reap what you sow."
"Your guess is as good as mine","<b>Meaning:</b> I don't know the answer either. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Implies that both people have the same lack of knowledge. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> 'When will the train arrive?' 'Your guess is as good as mine.'"
"Zip your lip","<b>Meaning:</b> To be quiet; to keep a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for closing your mouth like a zipper. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'll tell you the secret, but you have to zip your lip."
"A day late and a dollar short","<b>Meaning:</b> Too late and not enough. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An American idiom from the early 20th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The government's help was a day late and a dollar short for the victims of the flood."
"All bark and no bite","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who acts tough or threatening but is actually harmless. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of a dog that barks loudly but never attacks. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't be afraid of the manager; he's all bark and no bite."
"At the drop of a hat","<b>Meaning:</b> Immediately; without any hesitation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From races or fights that started when a hat was dropped. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She’s so helpful that she’ll come and help you at the drop of a hat."
"Back to the wall","<b>Meaning:</b> In a difficult situation where you have no choice but to fight or act. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; having no way to retreat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> With the company failing, he has his back to the wall."
"Barking up the wrong tree","<b>Meaning:</b> Accusing the wrong person or following the wrong idea. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hunting dogs barking at a tree where they think a raccoon is hiding. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you think I took your phone, you're barking up the wrong tree."
"Beat around the bush","<b>Meaning:</b> To avoid talking about the main topic or saying what you really mean. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hunting; beaters would hit the bushes to make birds fly out so the hunters could shoot them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop beating around the bush and tell me if I'm fired."
"Best of both worlds","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where you can enjoy the advantages of two very different things at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A modern descriptive phrase. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Working from home gives me the best of both worlds: a good salary and time with my family."
"Bite off more than you can chew","<b>Meaning:</b> To try to do something that is too difficult for you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal warning about taking a piece of food that is too large to swallow. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I think I bit off more than I could chew when I volunteered to lead three projects."
"Blessing in disguise","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that seems bad at first but results in something good happening later. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 1746 hymn. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Losing my job was a blessing in disguise because I found a much better one."
"Burn the midnight oil","<b>Meaning:</b> To stay up very late working or studying. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the time before electricity when people used oil lamps to see at night. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have to burn the midnight oil to finish this report by tomorrow."
"By the book","<b>Meaning:</b> Following the rules exactly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to following a manual or a law book. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The inspector does everything by the book."
"Call it a day","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop working on something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for finishing the day's tasks. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We've been working for ten hours; let's call it a day."
"Cold feet","<b>Meaning:</b> To become nervous and pull out of a deal or event at the last minute. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 19th century; having 'cold feet' meant you couldn't stand your ground in a fight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was going to propose, but he got cold feet."
"Cut corners","<b>Meaning:</b> To do something in the easiest or cheapest way, often ignoring the rules or quality. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From driving; 'cutting the corner' instead of following the curve of the road. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They cut corners on the construction, and now the roof is leaking."
"Cut to the chase","<b>Meaning:</b> To get to the most important part of something and stop wasting time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From silent movies; the audience wanted to get to the exciting 'chase' scene. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't need all the details; just cut to the chase and tell me the price."
"Don't cry over spilled milk","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't waste time worrying about things that have already happened and cannot be changed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 17th-century proverb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you're upset about the broken vase, but don't cry over spilled milk."
"Every dog has its day","<b>Meaning:</b> Everyone will have a period of success or luck at some point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A proverb dating back to the 16th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He hasn't won a game yet, but every dog has its day."
"Front","Back"
"In the bag","<b>Meaning:</b> Certain to be won or achieved. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hunting; once the prey is in the bag, the hunt is successfully over. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We’ve practiced so hard that I’m sure the trophy is in the bag."
"In the blink of an eye","<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely quickly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of how fast a human eyelid moves. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The accident happened in the blink of an eye."
"In the dark","<b>Meaning:</b> Uninformed; not knowing about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Darkness represents a lack of vision or knowledge. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The staff were kept in the dark about the company's merger until the very last minute."
"In the long run","<b>Meaning:</b> Over a long period of time; eventually. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From long-distance racing; the final result matters more than the initial sprint. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It seems expensive now, but it will save us money in the long run."
"In the loop","<b>Meaning:</b> Part of a group that is kept informed about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to a communication 'loop' where information circulates. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Please keep me in the loop regarding any changes to the schedule."
"In the nick of time","<b>Meaning:</b> At the very last possible moment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'nick' was a small notch cut into a stick to keep track of time or scores. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The ambulance arrived in the nick of time."
"In the red","<b>Meaning:</b> Spending more money than you are earning; in debt. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Accountants traditionally used red ink to show losses or debt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company has been in the red for three years."
"Inside out","<b>Meaning:</b> Knowing something thoroughly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Like knowing a piece of clothing so well you know the seams on the inside as well as the outside. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s been a mechanic for 40 years; he knows engines inside out."
"It's a small world","<b>Meaning:</b> Said when you meet someone you know in an unexpected place. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A common reaction to surprising coincidences. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I saw my old teacher while on vacation in Italy; it’s a small world!"
"Jack of all trades","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who can do many different types of work. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 1600s; 'Jack' was a generic name for a common man. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He can fix cars, paint houses, and garden; he’s a real jack of all trades."
"Jump the gun","<b>Meaning:</b> To start something too early, before you are supposed to. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From track races where runners start before the starter's pistol is fired. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We shouldn't celebrate yet; we don't want to jump the gun before the deal is signed."
"Jump on the bandwagon","<b>Meaning:</b> To join an activity or movement because it has become popular. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In politics, candidates would ride a wagon with a band; people would literally jump on to show support. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone is starting to invest in that stock; I might jump on the bandwagon too."
"Keep your chin up","<b>Meaning:</b> To remain joyful and confident in a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for not bowing your head in sadness or defeat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know things are hard right now, but keep your chin up."
"Keep your fingers crossed","<b>Meaning:</b> To hope for good luck or a positive result. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An ancient superstition that crossing fingers creates a 'cross' to ward off bad luck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm keeping my fingers crossed that I get the job."
"Keep your shirt on","<b>Meaning:</b> A way of telling someone to remain calm and patient. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Before a fight, men would take off their shirts; keeping it on means staying peaceful. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Keep your shirt on! I’ll be ready in five minutes."
"Kicked the bucket","<b>Meaning:</b> To die. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> One theory involves a person standing on a bucket to hang themselves, then 'kicking' it away. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our old neighbor finally kicked the bucket at age 98."
"Kill the messenger","<b>Meaning:</b> To blame the person who brings bad news. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient practice where kings would execute messengers bringing news of defeat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't kill the messenger, but your car has been towed."
"Knock on wood","<b>Meaning:</b> Said to avoid 'tempting fate' after saying something positive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient belief that good spirits lived inside trees. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I haven't been sick all year, knock on wood."
"Know the ropes","<b>Meaning:</b> To understand how a particular job or task is done. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sailors had to learn which ropes controlled which sails on a ship. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Ask Sarah for help; she’s been here for years and knows the ropes."
"Larger than life","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who attracts a lot of attention because they are more exciting than average people. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Describing something that exceeds normal proportions. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My uncle was a larger-than-life character who always had the best stories."
"Laugh all the way to the bank","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a lot of money from something, especially if others criticized it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Attributed to the pianist Liberace. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Critics hated the movie, but it was a huge hit; the director is laughing all the way to the bank."
"Left in the lurch","<b>Meaning:</b> Abandoned in a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an old French game similar to backgammon called 'lourche.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My partner quit right before the deadline and left me in the lurch."
"Let bygones be bygones","<b>Meaning:</b> To forget past disagreements and be friends again. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Bygones' are simply things that have 'gone by' (the past). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know we fought, but let's just let bygones be bygones."
"Level playing field","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where everyone has the same opportunities or advantages. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sports; ensuring the field doesn't slope in favor of one team. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new regulations are designed to create a level playing field for small businesses."
"Like a fish out of water","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling uncomfortable because you are in an unfamiliar environment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of an animal unable to breathe in a new setting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> At the formal gala, I felt like a fish out of water."
"Lion's share","<b>Meaning:</b> The largest part of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables; the lion takes all the meat for himself because he is the strongest. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She does the lion's share of the housework."
"Live and let live","<b>Meaning:</b> To accept other people's opinions and behavior even if they are different from yours. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 17th-century proverb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't agree with their lifestyle, but live and let live, I guess."
"Loose cannon","<b>Meaning:</b> An unpredictable person who may cause damage or trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A cannon on a ship that has broken its ropes; it rolls around the deck dangerously as the ship moves. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We have to be careful what we tell him; he’s a bit of a loose cannon."
"Low-hanging fruit","<b>Meaning:</b> The easiest tasks or goals to achieve. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fruit on the bottom branches of a tree is easiest to pick. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Let's focus on the low-hanging fruit first to get some quick wins."
"Make a mountain out of a molehill","<b>Meaning:</b> To exaggerate a small problem and make it seem like a huge one. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Compares a tiny pile of dirt made by a mole to a massive mountain. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It was just a tiny mistake; stop making a mountain out of a molehill."
"Make ends meet","<b>Meaning:</b> To have just enough money to pay for the things you need. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from making the two ends of a belt meet around the waist. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s hard to make ends meet on a minimum-wage job."
"Make no bones about it","<b>Meaning:</b> To be very clear and direct about something; to not hesitate. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Finding bones in your soup makes it hard to eat; 'no bones' means everything is smooth and clear. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He made no bones about his dislike for the new manager."
"Middle of the road","<b>Meaning:</b> Ordinary; not extreme or controversial. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Avoiding the 'ditches' or 'gutters' on either side of a path. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Their music is very middle of the road; it's not great, but it's not bad."
"Mind your P's and Q's","<b>Meaning:</b> To be careful about your behavior and manners. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from bartenders tracking 'pints' and 'quarts' for customers. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The guests will be here soon, so mind your P's and Q's."
"Miss the mark","<b>Meaning:</b> To fail to achieve a result or be incorrect. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From archery or shooting; failing to hit the target. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His prediction about the election completely missed the mark."
"Monday morning quarterback","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who criticizes or judges a situation after it has already happened. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fans who watch American football on Sunday and criticize the plays on Monday. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It's easy to be a Monday morning quarterback and say what we should have done."
"More than one way to skin a cat","<b>Meaning:</b> There are many different ways to achieve the same goal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 19th-century American proverb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If this plan doesn't work, we'll try another; there's more than one way to skin a cat."
"Mum's the word","<b>Meaning:</b> Keep this secret; don't tell anyone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Mum' is the sound made when the lips are closed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't tell her about the surprise party; mum's the word!"
"Neither here nor there","<b>Meaning:</b> Irrelevant or unimportant. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Something that doesn't fit into any specific category of importance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Whether I like the color or not is neither here nor there; we have to buy it."
"Never bite the hand that feeds you","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't hurt the person who is helping you or giving you money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal warning about how a dog should treat its owner. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You shouldn't criticize your boss to the clients; never bite the hand that feeds you."
"New kid on the block","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who has just joined a group or moved to a new place. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of a child moving to a new neighborhood. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As the new kid on the block, I had to work hard to prove myself."
"Nip it in the bud","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop a problem while it is still small, before it becomes large. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gardening; cutting off a flower 'bud' before it can grow and bloom. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to nip this argument in the bud before it turns into a fight."
"No love lost","<b>Meaning:</b> When two people dislike each other intensely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Implies there was never any love to begin with, so none could be lost. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> There is no love lost between the two rival teams."
"No strings attached","<b>Meaning:</b> An offer that has no hidden conditions or requirements. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the textile industry or puppetry; no 'strings' to control you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ll give you the money as a gift—no strings attached."
"Not a spark of truth","<b>Meaning:</b> Completely false. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'spark' is the smallest bit of light; its absence means total darkness (lies). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> There wasn't a spark of truth in his story about the accident."
"Not playing with a full deck","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is not very intelligent or is mentally unstable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a deck of cards missing some cards. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He thinks he’s a superhero; I don't think he's playing with a full deck."
"Nutty as a fruitcake","<b>Meaning:</b> Very crazy or eccentric. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fruitcakes are full of literal nuts. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My old neighbor is nutty as a fruitcake."
"Off the beaten path","<b>Meaning:</b> In a place where few people go; unusual. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal path that hasn't been 'beaten' or flattened by many footsteps. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We found a great little restaurant off the beaten path."
