"Off the hook","<b>Meaning:</b> No longer in trouble or responsible for something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From fishing; a fish that escapes the hook is free. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My sister said she would do my chores, so I'm off the hook."
"Off the record","<b>Meaning:</b> Information that is not intended to be made public. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From journalism; information that cannot be officially 'recorded' or quoted. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Off the record, I think the manager is going to resign."
"On a roll","<b>Meaning:</b> Having a series of successes. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from gambling (rolling dice) or the momentum of a rolling object. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ve won the last three games; I’m on a roll!"
"On cloud nine","<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely happy and excited. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a weather classification system; 'Cloud Nine' was the highest and fluffiest type of cloud. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She’s been on cloud nine since she got the job."
"On thin ice","<b>Meaning:</b> In a risky or dangerous situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal danger of falling through thin ice on a frozen lake. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’ve been late three times this week; you’re on thin ice."
"Once bitten, twice shy","<b>Meaning:</b> When you are careful to avoid something because you had a bad experience with it before. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 19th-century proverb about learning from pain. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm not going to invest in that company again; once bitten, twice shy."
"Out of the frying pan and into the fire","<b>Meaning:</b> Going from a bad situation to an even worse one. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> First used in the 1500s. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I quit my stressful job only to find my new one is even worse; out of the frying pan and into the fire."
"Out of the woods","<b>Meaning:</b> No longer in danger or difficulty. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for being lost in a dark, dangerous forest and finally finding the exit. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The surgery was successful, but the patient isn't out of the woods yet."
"Over my dead body","<b>Meaning:</b> I will never allow this to happen. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A dramatic way to say you would rather die than see something occur. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’re selling my car? Over my dead body!"
"Paddle your own canoe","<b>Meaning:</b> To be independent and take care of yourself. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An American idiom from the 19th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Now that you're 18, it's time to learn how to paddle your own canoe."
"Paint the town red","<b>Meaning:</b> To go out and celebrate wildly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from an 1837 incident where a group of men literally painted buildings red. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s my birthday; let’s go out and paint the town red!"
"Pandora's box","<b>Meaning:</b> A source of many unforeseen troubles. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Greek mythology; Pandora opened a box that released all the evils into the world. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Investigation into the minor theft opened a Pandora's box of corruption."
"Pass the buck","<b>Meaning:</b> To shift the responsibility for something to someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From poker; a 'buck' (knife) was used as a marker for the dealer, which was passed to the next person. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't try to pass the buck; it was your job to lock the door."
"Penny-pincher","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is very careful with money; a stingy person. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone holding onto their coins so tightly they 'pinch' them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My boss is such a penny-pincher that he won't even buy new pens for the office."
"Pipe dream","<b>Meaning:</b> An unrealistic hope or plan. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the hallucinations experienced by people smoking opium pipes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He wants to be a movie star, but it’s just a pipe dream."
"Play devil's advocate","<b>Meaning:</b> To argue against an idea just to test its strength or provoke discussion. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An official position in the Catholic Church whose job was to find flaws in evidence for sainthood. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I agree with you, but let me play devil's advocate for a moment."
"Play second fiddle","<b>Meaning:</b> To be less important or powerful than someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In an orchestra, the 'first fiddle' plays the lead part; the 'second' is supportive. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm tired of playing second fiddle to my brother."
"Point of no return","<b>Meaning:</b> The stage in a process where it is impossible to go back. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From aviation; the point on a flight where a plane doesn't have enough fuel to return to its start. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Once we sign this contract, we’ve reached the point of no return."
"Pull a fast one","<b>Meaning:</b> To trick or deceive someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from card games or magic tricks where fast hand movements hide the truth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He tried to pull a fast one by charging me double the price."
"Pull your weight","<b>Meaning:</b> To do your fair share of work in a group. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From rowing; each person must pull their oar with enough strength to move the boat's weight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you don't start pulling your weight, the team will fail."
"Put a damper on","<b>Meaning:</b> To make something less enjoyable or exciting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'damper' is a device in a piano or stove that reduces sound or airflow. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The rain really put a damper on our picnic."
"Put your money where your mouth is","<b>Meaning:</b> To back up your words with action or money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A challenge to stop talking and start proving. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You say you can win the race? Put your money where your mouth is and enter it."
"Queer the pitch","<b>Meaning:</b> To spoil someone's chances of success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From street performers (buskers); a 'pitch' was where they performed. Spoil the area, and they can't earn money. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His late arrival really queered the pitch for the whole presentation."
"Rain on someone's parade","<b>Meaning:</b> To spoil someone's plans or excitement. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of a celebratory parade being ruined by bad weather. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't want to rain on your parade, but the concert is canceled."
"Raise the bar","<b>Meaning:</b> To increase the standards or expectations for something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From high jumping; physically raising the wooden bar that athletes must jump over. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Her brilliant performance has really raised the bar for the rest of us."
"Rally the troops","<b>Meaning:</b> To encourage and organize people to work together. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; bringing soldiers together to prepare for battle. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to rally the troops if we want to finish this project by Friday."
"Red herring","<b>Meaning:</b> A piece of information intended to be misleading or distracting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using a strong-smelling smoked fish (red herring) to distract hunting dogs from a scent. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The plot of the mystery novel was full of red herrings."
"Red tape","<b>Meaning:</b> Official rules and processes that seem unnecessary and cause delays. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In the past, official legal documents in England were tied together with red ribbon or tape. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> There is so much red tape involved in getting a passport."
"Right off the bat","<b>Meaning:</b> Immediately; from the very beginning. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From baseball; the moment the ball is hit by the bat, the action begins. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I knew right off the bat that we were going to be friends."
"Ring a bell","<b>Meaning:</b> To sound familiar, even if you can't remember the details. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from psychological experiments (like Pavlov's dogs) where a bell triggers a memory. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Her name rings a bell, but I can't remember where I met her."
"Rock the boat","<b>Meaning:</b> To cause trouble by changing a situation that most people are happy with. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Physically moving side-to-side in a boat can make it tip over. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone is happy with the current schedule, so don't rock the boat."
"Rough around the edges","<b>Meaning:</b> Having good qualities but lacking polish or refinement. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to a raw diamond or a piece of wood that hasn't been sanded smooth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a great worker, but his manners are a bit rough around the edges."
"Rub someone the wrong way","<b>Meaning:</b> To annoy or irritate someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to petting a cat's fur in the wrong direction, which makes the cat angry. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't know why, but his voice just rubs me the wrong way."
"Run circles around someone","<b>Meaning:</b> To be much better or faster than someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for having so much energy or skill that you can circle your opponent while they struggle. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She can run circles around the other students when it comes to math."
"Save for a rainy day","<b>Meaning:</b> To save money for a time in the future when you might need it unexpectedly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'rainy day' represents a time of difficulty when you cannot work outside. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I put $50 from every paycheck into a savings account to save for a rainy day."
"Saved by the bell","<b>Meaning:</b> Rescued from a difficult situation by a timely interruption. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; a fighter who is losing is 'saved' if the bell rings to end the round. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't know the answer to the teacher's question, but the lunch bell rang—I was saved by the bell."
"Scrape the bottom of the barrel","<b>Meaning:</b> To use the worst people or things because there is nothing better left. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> When food is running low, you have to scrape the very last (often lower quality) bits from the bottom. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They really scraped the bottom of the barrel when they hired him."
"See red","<b>Meaning:</b> To become very angry. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From bullfighting; bulls were thought to be angered by the color red (though they are actually colorblind). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I saw red when I found out he had lied to me."
"Set the record straight","<b>Meaning:</b> To correct a mistake or a false statement by giving the true facts. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for fixing a written historical record. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I want to set the record straight: I was not the one who broke the window."
"Shot in the dark","<b>Meaning:</b> A guess that is very unlikely to be correct. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Firing a gun at night without being able to see the target. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't know the answer, so I just took a shot in the dark."
"Shoulder to cry on","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who listens to your problems and gives you sympathy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for leaning on someone while weeping. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was a great friend and always provided a shoulder to cry on."
"Show your true colors","<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal what you are really like. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ships; pirate ships would fly a friendly flag and then switch to their real (true) flag before attacking. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He seemed nice at first, but he showed his true colors when he got angry."
"Sick and tired","<b>Meaning:</b> Very annoyed and frustrated with something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An exaggeration of emotional fatigue. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm sick and tired of this cold weather."
"Silver bullet","<b>Meaning:</b> A simple and magical solution to a complicated problem. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From folklore; the only way to kill a werewolf was with a silver bullet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> There is no silver bullet for the country's economic problems."
"Sitting duck","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is in a vulnerable position and easy to attack. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A duck sitting on the water is an easy target for a hunter compared to one that is flying. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Without security, the store was a sitting duck for thieves."
"Skeleton staff","<b>Meaning:</b> The smallest number of people needed to keep a business or organization running. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'skeleton' is the basic framework of a body without any 'extra' parts. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The office is open during the holidays, but only with a skeleton staff."
"Skin in the game","<b>Meaning:</b> Having a personal investment or risk in an outcome. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized by Warren Buffett; 'skin' refers to one's own money or self. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m much more careful with this project now that I have skin in the game."
"Sling mud","<b>Meaning:</b> To make insulting or damaging remarks about someone to ruin their reputation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of throwing dirt at someone's clean clothes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The two candidates spent the whole debate slinging mud at each other."
"Small fry","<b>Meaning:</b> People or things that are not important. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Fry' are newly hatched fish, which are tiny and insignificant to a fisherman. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The police caught the small fry, but the leaders of the gang escaped."
"Smooth over","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a problem or disagreement seem less serious. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for sanding down a rough surface. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I tried to smooth over the argument between my two friends."
"Sods law","<b>Meaning:</b> The idea that if something can go wrong, it will. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> British version of Murphy's Law. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s Sods law that it started raining the moment I left my umbrella at home."
"Sour grapes","<b>Meaning:</b> Pretending to dislike something because you cannot have it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables; a fox can't reach some grapes, so he says they were probably sour anyway. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He says he didn't want the promotion anyway, but I think it's just sour grapes."
"Front","Back"
"Break the bank","<b>Meaning:</b> To cost more money than one can afford. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; when a player wins more money than the house has on hand. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You can get a great meal there without breaking the bank."
"Bridge the gap","<b>Meaning:</b> To reduce the differences between two things or groups. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Engineering; building a structure to connect two sides of a divide. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This new scholarship is designed to bridge the gap between rich and poor students."
"Bring to light","<b>Meaning:</b> To make something known; to reveal a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for taking something out of a dark hiding place and into the sun. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The investigation brought to light several cases of corruption."
"Brush up on","<b>Meaning:</b> To refresh one's knowledge of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from cleaning or 'brushing' off dust from an old object to make it usable again. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I need to brush up on my French before our trip to Paris."
"Burn the bridge behind you","<b>Meaning:</b> To act in a way that makes it impossible to return to a previous state. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; destroying a bridge after crossing so the enemy cannot follow and you cannot retreat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He insulted his boss on the way out, effectively burning the bridge behind him."
"Bury your head in the sand","<b>Meaning:</b> To ignore a problem and hope it will go away. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Based on the false belief that ostriches hide their heads in the sand when in danger. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You can't just bury your head in the sand; the debt isn't going anywhere."
"By a landslide","<b>Meaning:</b> To win an election or competition by a very large margin. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing a massive victory to the overwhelming force of falling earth and rock. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The mayor was re-elected by a landslide."
"By the skin of one's teeth","<b>Meaning:</b> To manage to do something by the smallest possible margin. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Job 19:20); teeth don't actually have skin, implying a margin that is almost non-existent. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I passed the exam by the skin of my teeth."
"Call a spade a spade","<b>Meaning:</b> To speak directly and bluntly about something, even if it is unpleasant. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ancient Greece; a 'spade' was a common garden tool, used as a symbol for plainness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Let's call a spade a spade: the project was a total failure."
"Call it a night","<b>Meaning:</b> To stop an activity and go to bed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Similar to 'call it a day,' but specifically for evening activities. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s 11 PM and I'm tired; let's call it a night."
"Can't make heads or tails of it","<b>Meaning:</b> To be completely unable to understand something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the two sides of a coin; if you can't see either side, you don't know what you have. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I've read this manual three times, but I can't make heads or tails of it."
"Carry a tune","<b>Meaning:</b> To be able to sing melody accurately. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'holding' the notes of a song in the correct place. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a nice guy, but he really can't carry a tune."
"Cash in your chips","<b>Meaning:</b> To die, or to quit something while you have an advantage. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; turning in your betting chips for cash at the end of the game. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After forty years in the business, he decided it was time to cash in his chips and retire."
"Catch-22","<b>Meaning:</b> A paradoxical situation from which an individual cannot escape because of contradictory rules. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Joseph Heller’s 1961 novel; a pilot can be grounded if he's crazy, but asking to be grounded proves he's sane. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't get a job without experience, but I can't get experience without a job—it's a Catch-22."
"Change your tune","<b>Meaning:</b> To change your opinion or attitude, especially when it is convenient. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From music; switching to a different melody to please an audience. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He hated the idea at first, but he changed his tune when he saw the profit potential."
"Checkered past","<b>Meaning:</b> A history marked by many periods of both success and failure, or honesty and dishonesty. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the pattern of a chessboard (checkers), alternating between black and white. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The candidate has a checkered past, so the voters are hesitant."
"Chip off the old block","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is very similar to their parent in character or appearance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A small piece (chip) of wood or stone that looks just like the larger piece it came from. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s just as stubborn as his father; a real chip off the old block."
"Clean slate","<b>Meaning:</b> A new beginning where past mistakes are forgotten. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the use of chalkboards (slates) in schools; wiping them clean to start a new lesson. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Moving to a new city gave him a clean slate."
"Climb the corporate ladder","<b>Meaning:</b> To advance in rank and status within a company. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the hierarchical levels of a business being like rungs on a ladder. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s worked hard for ten years to climb the corporate ladder."
"Close shave","<b>Meaning:</b> A narrow escape from danger. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 19th century; a shave so close that it barely misses cutting the skin. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That car nearly hit us; it was a real close shave."
"Cold comfort","<b>Meaning:</b> Poor or very little consolation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Used by Shakespeare; comfort that is so minimal it feels 'cold' rather than 'warm.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Knowing that the thief was caught was cold comfort for the loss of my family photos."
"Come hell or high water","<b>Meaning:</b> No matter what happens; determined to do something regardless of difficulties. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the American West, referring to the extreme dangers of cattle drives. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ll be at your wedding, come hell or high water."
"Cook the books","<b>Meaning:</b> To dishonestly change financial records to steal money or hide losses. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Cook' is used here to mean 'manipulate' or 'alter' for a desired (deceptive) result. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The accountant was arrested for cooking the books to hide his embezzlement."
"Crocodile tears","<b>Meaning:</b> A false or insincere display of grief. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an ancient myth that crocodiles weep while eating their prey to lure or mock them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't believe her crying; those are just crocodile tears."
"Cross that bridge when you come to it","<b>Meaning:</b> To deal with a problem only when it actually happens, not before. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A reminder to stay focused on the present rather than worrying about future obstacles. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We might run out of money in June, but we'll cross that bridge when we come to it."
"Cry wolf","<b>Meaning:</b> To raise a false alarm so often that people stop believing you when there is real danger. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fable about the boy who cried 'Wolf!' to trick villagers. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you keep calling in sick when you're fine, no one will believe you when you're actually ill; don't cry wolf."
"Cut a long story short","<b>Meaning:</b> To leave out the boring details and get to the main point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal instruction to summarize. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> To cut a long story short, we ended up staying in a tent."
"Cut someone some slack","<b>Meaning:</b> To be less strict or critical with someone because they are in a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sailing; loosening a tight rope (slackening it) to reduce tension. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s new at the job, so cut him some slack while he learns the ropes."
"Dance to someone's tune","<b>Meaning:</b> To do exactly what someone else wants you to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a musician controlling the movements of dancers. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm tired of dancing to the manager's tune; I want to make my own decisions."
"Dark horse","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who keeps their interests or talents secret, or a surprise winner. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a horse that the bettors know very little about. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> No one expected him to win the election; he was a real dark horse."
"Day in the sun","<b>Meaning:</b> A period of success, popularity, or fame. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for standing in the light and warmth of public attention. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After years of training, the athlete finally had her day in the sun."
"Dead in the water","<b>Meaning:</b> A plan or project that has failed and has no chance of succeeding. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; a sailing ship that cannot move because there is no wind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Without the new investment, the project is dead in the water."
"Dead to the world","<b>Meaning:</b> Fast asleep or unconscious. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A person who is so deeply asleep they are completely unaware of their surroundings. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s been working for 20 hours; he’s dead to the world right now."
"Diamond in the rough","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who has great potential or talent but lacks style or polish. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal diamond looks like a common pebble until it is cut and polished. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a bit rude, but he’s a brilliant programmer—a diamond in the rough."
"Dime a dozen","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is very common and therefore not very valuable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In the 1800s, some goods were so plentiful they cost only ten cents (a dime) for twelve (a dozen). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Aspiring actors in Hollywood are a dime a dozen."
"Do a 180","<b>Meaning:</b> To completely change your opinion or behavior. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From geometry; turning 180 degrees means facing the exact opposite direction. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He used to hate exercise, but he's done a 180 and now goes to the gym every day."
"Dog-eat-dog world","<b>Meaning:</b> A situation of fierce competition where people will hurt each other to succeed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a Latin proverb; usually 'dog does not eat dog,' which was later inverted to describe extreme ruthlessness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Business can be a dog-eat-dog world."
"Don't hold your breath","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't expect something to happen soon (because it probably won't). <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> If you held your breath until the event happened, you would suffocate. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He said he'd call you back, but don't hold your breath."
"Don't put words in my mouth","<b>Meaning:</b> Don't claim that I said something when I didn't. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone trying to control or falsify another's speech. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I said the movie was long, not that it was bad; don't put words in my mouth."
"Double take","<b>Meaning:</b> Looking at someone or something a second time because you are surprised by what you saw. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From film and theater; a delayed reaction for comedic effect. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I did a double take when I saw him walking down the street in a tuxedo."
"Down in the dumps","<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling sad or depressed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Dumps' comes from an old word for a daze or a melancholy state. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She’s been a bit down in the dumps since her cat died."
"Down the drain","<b>Meaning:</b> Wasted or lost forever. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for something being washed away into the sewer system. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> All that hard work went down the drain when the computer crashed."
"Down to earth","<b>Meaning:</b> Practical, realistic, and friendly; not arrogant. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A person who is 'connected' to the ground rather than having their head in the clouds. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Despite being a movie star, she is very down to earth."
"Drag your feet","<b>Meaning:</b> To act slowly or delay a decision because you don't want to do it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of someone walking reluctantly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The government is dragging its feet on passing the new environmental laws."
"Drop the ball","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a mistake or fail in your responsibility. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sports like football or baseball; failing to catch or hold the ball. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I really dropped the ball by forgetting to invite the guest speaker."
"Dry run","<b>Meaning:</b> A rehearsal or practice session before the real event. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from fire departments practicing without actually using water (staying 'dry'). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Let's do a dry run of the presentation before the meeting tomorrow."
"Dumb luck","<b>Meaning:</b> Success that happens purely by chance, without any skill or effort. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Implies that the person was 'too dumb' to have planned the success. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It was just dumb luck that I found my lost wallet on the sidewalk."
"Dutch courage","<b>Meaning:</b> False confidence gained from drinking alcohol. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 17th-century wars between the English and the Dutch; soldiers drank gin before battle. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I needed a bit of Dutch courage before I could go on stage."
"Easy as pie","<b>Meaning:</b> Very easy to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely refers to how easy it is to *eat* a pie, rather than make one. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Setting up the new computer was easy as pie."
"Eat humble pie","<b>Meaning:</b> To admit you were wrong and apologize in a humiliating way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Umbles' were the internal organs of a deer, given to servants to eat in a pie; later confused with the word 'humble.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He had to eat humble pie after his 'guaranteed' prediction turned out to be wrong."
"Egg on your face","<b>Meaning:</b> To be embarrassed because of a mistake you made. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from people throwing eggs at bad performers in a theater. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s going to have egg on his face when he finds out he sent the email to the wrong person."
"Every cloud has a silver lining","<b>Meaning:</b> There is something good in every bad situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 1634 poem by John Milton; even a dark storm cloud has sunlight behind it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I lost my job, but it gave me time to start my own business; every cloud has a silver lining."
"Explore every avenue","<b>Meaning:</b> To try every possible way to achieve something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for searching every street (avenue) in a city. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We must explore every avenue to find a cure for this disease."
"Eye-opener","<b>Meaning:</b> An event or situation that proves to be very surprising or revealing. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Something that 'forces' your eyes to open and see the truth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Traveling through the poor neighborhood was a real eye-opener for the wealthy students."
"Face the music","<b>Meaning:</b> To accept the unpleasant consequences of one's actions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from the military, where a dismissed soldier was 'drummed out' to the sound of music. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You broke the window; now you have to face the music and tell your parents."
"Fair-weather friend","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is only a friend when things are going well. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A sailor who only works when the weather is good and deserts when it gets stormy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I thought he was my friend, but he disappeared as soon as I lost my money; he’s just a fair-weather friend."
"Fall on deaf ears","<b>Meaning:</b> To be ignored or disregarded. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone who refuses to listen as if they were physically deaf. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My warnings about the ice fell on deaf ears, and she slipped."
"Fast track","<b>Meaning:</b> A way of achieving something more quickly than usual. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a dry, hard track that allows for faster speeds. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s on the fast track to becoming a partner at the law firm."
"Feather in your cap","<b>Meaning:</b> An achievement that you can be proud of. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From many cultures (like Native Americans or Scots) where adding a feather to your headgear symbolized a brave deed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Winning the national award was a real feather in her cap."
"Feel it in your bones","<b>Meaning:</b> To have a strong intuition or feeling about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the way old injuries or arthritis can flare up when the weather changes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I just feel it in my bones that something good is going to happen today."
"Field day","<b>Meaning:</b> A time of great enjoyment or success, often at someone else's expense. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From military maneuvers performed in an open field for review. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The newspapers had a field day with the politician's latest scandal."
"Fight tooth and nail","<b>Meaning:</b> To fight very fiercely and with all your energy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for fighting like a wild animal using both teeth and claws. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She fought tooth and nail to keep her business from closing."
"Find your feet","<b>Meaning:</b> To become confident and comfortable in a new situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a baby learning to walk or a sailor getting used to a moving ship. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It took a few weeks, but I’m finally finding my feet in my new job."
"Fine line","<b>Meaning:</b> A very small difference between two things, often one being good and one being bad. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a narrow boundary that is easy to cross accidentally. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> There is a fine line between being confident and being arrogant."
"Finger in every pie","<b>Meaning:</b> Being involved in many different activities or businesses, often in an annoying way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone who can't resist touching everything. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s very powerful in this town; he has a finger in every pie."
"First things first","<b>Meaning:</b> Do the most important things before anything else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A simple prioritization rule. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you want to see the house, but first things first: we need to get the keys."
"Fish out of water","<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who feels uncomfortable because they are in an unfamiliar situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of an animal that cannot survive outside its natural environment. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As a city person, I felt like a fish out of water in the middle of the farm."
"Fit to be tied","<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely angry or upset. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 19th century; someone so furious they had to be physically restrained (tied up). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My mother was fit to be tied when she saw the mess in the kitchen."
"Flash in the pan","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is successful only for a very short time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old muskets; sometimes the gunpowder flared in the pan without firing the bullet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Many people thought the internet was just a flash in the pan."
"Follow your gut","<b>Meaning:</b> To make a decision based on your feelings or intuition rather than logic. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The 'gut' (intestines) is often where people feel a physical reaction to stress or truth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't have any proof he was lying, so I just followed my gut."
"Food for thought","<b>Meaning:</b> Something worth thinking about carefully. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likening information to food that needs to be 'digested' by the mind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The lecture gave me a lot of food for thought."
"For the birds","<b>Meaning:</b> Worthless, ridiculous, or uninteresting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the idea that only birds would find seeds in horse manure interesting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This new television show is for the birds."
"Freudian slip","<b>Meaning:</b> An unintentional mistake in speech that is thought to reveal a person's true feelings. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Named after Sigmund Freud, the founder of psychoanalysis. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She called her new boyfriend by her ex-husband's name; it was a classic Freudian slip."
"From rags to riches","<b>Meaning:</b> Going from being very poor to being very wealthy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A common theme in folklore and literature (like Cinderella). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The billionaire’s story is a classic case of from rags to riches."
"From the bottom of my heart","<b>Meaning:</b> With sincere and deep feeling. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The deepest part of the heart is seen as the most honest. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I want to thank you from the bottom of my heart for your help."
"From the horse's mouth","<b>Meaning:</b> Information from the person directly involved or the most reliable source. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See 'Straight from the horse's mouth.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know the company is closing; I heard it from the horse's mouth."
"Full of hot air","<b>Meaning:</b> Talking a lot without saying anything important or true. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likening a person's speech to the useless steam or air in a balloon. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't listen to him; he’s just full of hot air."
"Gain ground","<b>Meaning:</b> To make progress or become more successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; taking territory from the enemy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our company is finally starting to gain ground in the European market."
"Get a grip","<b>Meaning:</b> To control your emotions and behave more calmly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for physically grabbing onto something to stop yourself from falling. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You need to get a grip and stop panicking."
"Get wind of","<b>Meaning:</b> To hear a rumor or secret information. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hunting; animals can 'smell' a hunter if the wind carries the scent to them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The press got wind of the celebrity’s secret wedding."
"Get your ducks in a row","<b>Meaning:</b> To get organized and prepared before starting something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From bowling, where the pins were sometimes called 'ducks,' or from mother ducks leading their babies. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to get our ducks in a row before we meet with the investors."
"Give someone the slip","<b>Meaning:</b> To escape from someone who is following or chasing you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from sailing; 'slipping' a cable to leave a harbor quickly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The thief managed to give the police the slip in the crowded mall."
"Go out on a limb","<b>Meaning:</b> To take a risk by saying or doing something that others might not support. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See 'Out on a limb.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm going out on a limb and saying that this will be the best-selling book of the year."
"Go the extra mile","<b>Meaning:</b> To do more than what is expected or required. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Matthew 5:41), referring to a Roman law where a person could be forced to carry a soldier's pack for one mile. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She always goes the extra mile to make sure her customers are happy."
"Go with the flow","<b>Meaning:</b> To accept a situation and not try to change it or argue about it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a person being carried along by the current of a river. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't have a plan for the weekend; I'll just go with the flow."
"Graveyard shift","<b>Meaning:</b> A work shift that runs through the middle of the night (usually 12 AM to 8 AM). <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely because the night is as quiet and dark as a graveyard. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s tired because he’s been working the graveyard shift all week."
"Grease someone's palm","<b>Meaning:</b> To bribe someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 16th century; 'greasing' a machine makes it work better, just as money makes a person 'work' for you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He had to grease the building inspector's palm to get the permit."
"Green light","<b>Meaning:</b> Permission to start a project or activity. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From traffic signals where a green light means you can proceed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The boss finally gave us the green light to start the new campaign."
"Hard to swallow","<b>Meaning:</b> Difficult to believe or accept. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a piece of food that is too large or unpleasant to go down the throat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The news of the factory closing was hard to swallow for the workers."
"Haste makes waste","<b>Meaning:</b> If you do something too quickly, you will make mistakes and waste time or resources. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A proverb from the 14th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Slow down and check your work; haste makes waste."
"Have a bone to pick with someone","<b>Meaning:</b> To have a complaint or grievance that you want to discuss. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a dog chewing on a bone until every scrap of meat is gone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a bone to pick with you about the way you spoke to me yesterday."
"Have your cake and eat it too","<b>Meaning:</b> To want two incompatible things at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Once you eat a cake, it is gone; you cannot keep it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You want a high salary but you don't want to work long hours; you can't have your cake and eat it too."
"Head in the clouds","<b>Meaning:</b> Being unrealistic or distracted by daydreams. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone who isn't looking at the ground (reality). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He has his head in the clouds if he thinks he can win without practicing."
"Heard it through the grapevine","<b>Meaning:</b> To hear news or rumors through informal talk. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See 'Through the grapevine.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I heard through the grapevine that they are getting a divorce."
"High and dry","<b>Meaning:</b> Left in a difficult situation without any help. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; a ship that is stuck on the shore when the tide goes out. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When the company went bankrupt, the employees were left high and dry."
"Hit the books","<b>Meaning:</b> To study hard. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'attacking' your studies with energy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a big exam tomorrow, so I need to hit the books tonight."
"Hit the jackpot","<b>Meaning:</b> To have a sudden, great success or to win a lot of money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From slot machines or poker. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She really hit the jackpot when she found that old painting at the garage sale."
"Hit the road","<b>Meaning:</b> To leave or begin a journey. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of feet or tires hitting the pavement. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s getting late; it’s time for us to hit the road."
"Hold your horses","<b>Meaning:</b> Wait a moment; be patient. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the days of horse-drawn carriages; pulling the reins to stop the horses. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Hold your horses! We can't leave until I find my keys."
"In a nutshell","<b>Meaning:</b> Briefly and clearly summarized. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the idea of writing something so small it could fit inside a nutshell. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> In a nutshell, the plan failed because we didn't have enough money."
